ChatGPT: 

Great — here is the **frozen boundary** (what we reuse vs what we build), and a **first-pass architecture** for a TypeScript package ecosystem that stays calendar/tradition/language/script-agnostic.

---

## Boundary definition

### We delegate to CLDR/ICU/Intl

We do **not** reimplement any of this:

1.  **Locale identifiers + likelySubtags + script selection defaults**
    
    -   `Intl.Locale` + CLDR-backed behavior (via runtime ICU).
        
2.  **Date/time formatting**
    
    -   `Intl.DateTimeFormat` for localized formatting patterns, month/day names, numerals.
        
3.  **Calendar conversion where supported**
    
    -   `Intl.DateTimeFormat(locale + "-u-ca-…")` and `Intl.Locale` calendar extensions *when available* (platform-dependent).
        
    -   We treat this as an optimization, not a requirement.
        
4.  **Plural rules / grammatical number**
    
    -   `Intl.PluralRules` (for any templating we do that needs plural choice).
        

**Key design constraint:** Our core must run even if the runtime lacks a particular non-Gregorian calendar conversion. That’s why calendar computation is pluggable below.

---

### We build ourselves

We **do** implement these layers (because CLDR does not):

1.  **Event semantics**
    
    -   “What happened today?” as typed events (birthday, Eid, Reformation Day, Chinese New Year, etc.).
        
2.  **Tradition/culture affiliation modeling**
    
    -   Which registries are active for a user; how diaspora/residence interacts; opt-in/out toggles.
        
3.  **Event selection policy**
    
    -   Priority, conflict resolution, suppression, fallback, “do not repeat”.
        
4.  **Greeting lexicon + rendering**
    
    -   Multi-language + multi-script phrase inventory (native script vs transliteration vs localized vernacular).
        
    -   Personalization (name insertion, vocative transform via plugins).
        
5.  **Data distribution**
    
    -   Static hosted registries/lexica, lazy fetch on onboarding, persist only per-user subset.
        
6.  **Calendar calculators not covered by Intl**
    
    -   Hijri (algorithmic and/or tabular variants), Chinese lunisolar, Orthodox Pascha vs Western Easter, etc. as plugins.
        

---

## Architecture overview

Think of this as a small “runtime kernel” + plugins + data packs.

### Core packages

1.  **`@your-scope/greeting-core`**
    
    -   Orchestrator / engine
        
    -   Interfaces, types, policy
        
    -   Memory model
        
    -   Template rendering (minimal, deterministic)
        
2.  **`@your-scope/greeting-calendars`**
    
    -   Calendar calculators (Gregorian helpers, Western Easter, Orthodox Pascha, Hijri variants, Chinese lunisolar, etc.)
        
    -   Each calculator is a pure function module, testable.
        
3.  **`@your-scope/greeting-registry`**
    
    -   Registry loaders (static JSON fetch, versioning, integrity checks)
        
    -   Onboarding extraction utilities (reduce dataset to “user subset”)
        
4.  **`@your-scope/greeting-render`**
    
    -   Script selection + transliteration strategy
        
    -   Name formatting hooks (e.g., Greek vocative plugin)
        
    -   Lightweight templating
        

### Data packs (published separately, can be hosted remotely)

-   `@your-scope/greeting-pack-de` (Germany civil + optional Christian variants)
    
-   `@your-scope/greeting-pack-el` (Greek civil + Orthodox holiday greetings; namedays optional)
    
-   `@your-scope/greeting-pack-islam` (Islamic events + lexicon variants)
    
-   `@your-scope/greeting-pack-zh` (Chinese tradition events + simplified/traditional lexicon)
    

**Important:** data packs can be:

-   npm modules (bundled), or
    
-   static JSON served from your controlled CDN/GitHub Pages
    

Your earlier “don’t ship the world to every client” preference implies:

-   ship a minimal core via npm
    
-   fetch packs lazily by locale/tradition at onboarding
    

---

## The engine: inputs and outputs

### Inputs (runtime context)

-   `now` (Date + timezone context)
    
-   `user profile` (name(s), birthday, preferences)
    
-   `affiliations` (civil: DE, tradition: islam, etc.)
    
-   `memory` (what greetings already delivered)
    
-   `mode` (opening vs closing)
    

### Outputs

A single selected **GreetingResult**:

-   final text string (already rendered)
    
-   metadata (eventId, key used for anti-repeat, language/script used)
    
-   optional “follow-up response expectation” (e.g., Easter call/response), as metadata
    

---

## The frozen conceptual model

### 1) Calendar layer

**Goal:** resolve “today” membership in event windows.

Interface:

-   `CalendarSystem` resolves rules into Gregorian instants (or ranges).
    
-   The engine only operates in Gregorian instants internally.
    

Examples:

-   Gregorian fixed date rule
    
-   Western Easter-relative rule
    
-   Orthodox Pascha-relative rule
    
-   Hijri rule (1 Shawwal)
    
-   Chinese lunisolar rule (1st day 1st month)
    

### 2) Event layer

An event is **semantic**, not linguistic.

Event fields (conceptual):

-   `eventId` (stable)
    
-   `domain` (personal | civil | religious | seasonal | temporal)
    
-   `traditionTag` (secular | islam | orthodox | catholic | protestant | chinese\_tradition …)
    
-   `jurisdictionTag` (de-DE, el-GR, GB-ENG, etc.)
    
-   `dateRule` (calendar-rule reference)
    
-   `window` (single day vs duration vs season)
    
-   `priority` (base score)
    
-   `applicability` conditions (requires user match? diaspora/residence? opt-in?)
    

### 3) Lexicon layer

Lexicon is **renderable phrase inventory** keyed by:

-   `eventId`
    
-   `language` (BCP-47)
    
-   `script` preference (native, latin transliteration, simplified/traditional, etc.)
    
-   `formality` (informal/formal)
    
-   optional `tone` (neutral/blessing/playful)
    
-   optional `variant` (regional)
    

This is where:

-   “Eid Mubarak” vs “İyi Bayramlar” vs “عيد مبارك”
    
-   Simplified vs Traditional Chinese
    
-   German Sie/du variants  
    live — not in the event.
    

### 4) Selection policy layer

Core deterministic steps:

1.  Generate candidate events active “now” (including personal events).
    
2.  Filter by user applicability and cultural toggles.
    
3.  Score by priority + tie-breakers.
    
4.  Suppress those already used (memory keys).
    
5.  Render using best lexicon match (language/script fallback rules).
    
6.  Emit result + store memory key.
    

### 5) Memory layer

Memory is not “text-based”; it is **event-key-based**.

Keys look like:

-   `birthday:1990-04-12:2026`
    
-   `nameday:giannis:2026`
    
-   `holiday:de:unity_day:2026-10-03`
    
-   `season:islam:ramadan:2026`
    
-   `month:2026-03`
    
-   `newyear:2026`
    

This is what prevents repetition cleanly.

---

## Data distribution strategy

### What we host remotely (recommended)

-   Registries and lexica as static JSON files:
    
    -   `/packs/de-DE/civil.json`
        
    -   `/packs/islam/core.json`
        
    -   `/packs/zh/core.json`
        
    -   plus lexicon files split by language/script
        

### What we store locally per user

-   Derived “user slice”:
    
    -   birthdays (already known)
        
    -   matched nameday rules for user’s names
        
    -   active pack versions + ETags
        
    -   greeting memory
        

This keeps payload minimal and scales to many traditions.

---

## Locale/tradition configuration for diaspora

We formalize the user as having (at least) three independent “locale-like” selectors:

1.  **Residence jurisdiction** (civil holidays default)
    
2.  **Cultural/tradition affiliations** (religious/traditional packs)
    
3.  **Greeting language + script preference** (lexicon resolution)
    

Example: Turkish Muslim in Germany:

-   residence: `de-DE` (civil pack)
    
-   affiliations: `islam` (Hijri pack)
    
-   greeting language: `tr-TR`, script: latin
    

The same engine works for Chinese diaspora:

-   residence: `de-DE`
    
-   affiliation: `chinese_tradition`
    
-   greeting language: `zh`, script: simplified/traditional
    

---

## Package skeleton proposal

### `@your-scope/greeting-core`

-   `Engine`
    
-   `Policy` (default policy + hooks)
    
-   `MemoryStore` interfaces
    
-   Core types:
    
    -   `UserProfile`, `Affiliations`, `GreetingMode`
        
    -   `EventDefinition`, `LexiconEntry`
        
    -   `CalendarRule` references
        
    -   `GreetingResult`
        

### `@your-scope/greeting-calendars`

-   `gregorian.ts` helpers (fixed, nth-weekday)
    
-   `easter-western.ts`
    
-   `pascha-orthodox.ts`
    
-   `hijri-tabular.ts` (and/or `hijri-ummalqura.ts` if you later choose)
    
-   `chinese-lunisolar.ts` (can start as plugin later)
    

### `@your-scope/greeting-registry`

-   `PackLoader` (fetch + cache + integrity)
    
-   `PackIndex` (discover available packs)
    
-   `SliceBuilder` (reduce pack data to user-specific subset)
    

### `@your-scope/greeting-render`

-   `LexiconResolver` (language/script fallback)
    
-   `TemplateRenderer` (variable injection)
    
-   `NameTransform` hooks (e.g., Greek vocative module)

-   `hellenic-holidays` becomes an **adapter provider** inside a Greek pack builder (or runtime provider), producing `EventDefinition`s for `el-GR` civil/religious holidays.
    
-   Greek vocative module becomes a `NameTransform` plugin used only when:
    
    -   greeting language is Greek, and
        
    -   `style.requiresVocative === true`

-   Islamic pack with Eid al-Fitr / Eid al-Adha using a Hijri calculator plugin (even if minimal)

# I. Architectural Layers (Frozen)

```
greeting-core  
  ├─ Engine  
  ├─ Policy  
  ├─ Memory  
  ├─ Types  
  
greeting-calendars (plugins)  
  ├─ Gregorian  
  ├─ WesternEaster  
  ├─ OrthodoxPascha  
  ├─ Hijri  
  ├─ ChineseLunisolar

  ├─ Event registry  
  ├─ Lexicon  
  ├─ Metadata
```

# II. Core TypeScript Interfaces

## 1️⃣ User Profile

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingUserProfile {  
  id: string  
  
  // Identity  
  givenNames?: string\[\]  
  birthday?: string // ISO YYYY-MM-DD  
  
  // Cultural axes  
  residenceLocale: string        // e.g. "de-DE"  
  culturalAffiliations: string\[\] // e.g. \["islam"\], \["orthodox"\], \["civil"\]  
    
  // Rendering preferences  
  greetingLanguage: string       // BCP-47, e.g. "tr-TR"  
  scriptPreference?: string      // "latin" | "arabic" | "simplified" | "traditional" | etc.  
  formality?: "informal" | "formal"  
  
  // Participation toggles  
  participatesInResidenceCivil?: boolean  
}
```

---

## 2️⃣ Runtime Context

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingContext {  
  now: Date  
  timezone?: string  
  mode: "opening" | "closing"  
  user?: GreetingUserProfile  
  memory: GreetingMemoryStore  
}
```

---

## 3️⃣ Memory Contract

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingMemoryStore {  
  has(key: string): boolean  
  set(key: string, expiresAt?: Date): void  
}
```

Keys are event-based, not text-based.

---

# III. Calendar Plugin Contracts

## 1️⃣ Calendar System

```
TypeScript

export interface CalendarSystem {  
  id: string // "gregorian" | "hijri" | "orthodox" | "chinese"  
  
  resolve(rule: CalendarRule, year: number, timezone?: string): Date\[\]  
}
```

---

## 2️⃣ Calendar Rule Types

These are semantic, not implementation-bound.

```
TypeScript

export type CalendarRule \=  
  | FixedDateRule  
  | RelativeToEasterRule  
  | RelativeToPaschaRule  
  | HijriRule  
  | LunarRule  
  | NthWeekdayRule  
  | SolarTermRule  
  | UserBirthdayRule
```

Examples:

```
TypeScript

export interface FixedDateRule {  
  type: "fixed"  
  month: number  
  day: number  
}  
  
export interface RelativeToEasterRule {  
  type: "relativeToWesternEaster"  
  offsetDays: number  
}  
  
export interface HijriRule {  
  type: "hijri"  
  month: number  
  day: number  
}  
  
export interface LunarRule {  
  type: "chineseLunar"  
  month: number  
  day: number  
}  
  
export interface NthWeekdayRule {  
  type: "nthWeekday"  
  month: number  
  weekday: number // 0–6  
  nth: number  
}
```

---

# IV. Event Model (Semantic Layer)

## 1️⃣ Event Definition

```
TypeScript

export interface CelebrationEvent {  
  id: string  
  
  // Classification  
  domain: "personal" | "civil" | "religious" | "seasonal" | "temporal"  
  traditionTags?: string\[\]  // \["islam"\], \["orthodox"\], \["protestant"\], \["chinese\_traditional"\]  
  jurisdictionTags?: string\[\] // \["de-DE"\], \["el-GR"\], \["GB-ENG"\]  
  
  calendarSystem: string // refers to CalendarSystem.id  
  rule: CalendarRule  
  
  // Window control  
  durationDays?: number  
  
  // Selection logic  
  priority: number  
  
  // Optional user matching  
  requiresUserMatch?: "birthday" | "nameday"  
}
```

Important:  
Event contains NO greeting text.

---

# V. Lexicon Model (Rendering Layer)

## 1️⃣ Lexicon Entry

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingLexiconEntry {  
  eventId: string  
  
  locale: string // BCP-47  
  script?: string // optional script variant  
  
  formality?: "informal" | "formal"  
  
  template: string  
}
```

Template example:

```
TypeScript

"Bayramınız kutlu olsun, {{name}}!"
```

---

## 2️⃣ Rendering Context

```
TypeScript

export interface RenderContext {  
  name?: string  
  zodiac?: string  
  timeOfDay?: "morning" | "afternoon" | "evening" | "night"  
}
```

---

# VI. Greeting Result

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingResult {  
  eventId: string  
  text: string  
  language: string  
  script?: string  
  memoryKey: string  
}
```

---

# VII. Greeting Engine Contract

```
TypeScript

export interface GreetingEngine {  
  registerCalendar(system: CalendarSystem): void  
  registerEvents(events: CelebrationEvent\[\]): void  
  registerLexicon(entries: GreetingLexiconEntry\[\]): void  
  
  generate(context: GreetingContext): GreetingResult | null  
}
```

---

# VIII. Selection Algorithm (Formalized)

1.  Collect active events:
    
    -   Match date
        
    -   Match tradition
        
    -   Match jurisdiction
        
    -   Match user-specific conditions
        
2.  Sort by priority
    
3.  Filter suppressed (memory)
    
4.  Resolve lexicon:
    
    -   Match greetingLanguage
        
    -   Fallback to language base
        
    -   Fallback to English
        
    -   Match scriptPreference
        
5.  Render template
    
6.  Return result
    

---

# IX. Canonical JSON Pack Schema

This is what lives on your CDN / GitHub.

## 1️⃣ Pack Metadata

```
JSON

{  
  "packId": "de-DE.civil",  
  "version": "1.0.0",  
  "locale": "de-DE",  
  "tradition": "civil",  
  "calendarSystems": \["gregorian"\]  
}
```

---

## 2️⃣ Events Section

```
JSON

{  
  "events": \[  
    {  
      "id": "german\_unity\_day",  
      "domain": "civil",  
      "jurisdictionTags": \["de-DE"\],  
      "calendarSystem": "gregorian",  
      "rule": {  
        "type": "fixed",  
        "month": 10,  
        "day": 3  
      },  
      "priority": 80  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

---

## 3️⃣ Lexicon Section

```
JSON

{  
  "lexicon": \[  
    {  
      "eventId": "german\_unity\_day",  
      "locale": "de-DE",  
      "formality": "informal",  
      "template": "Alles Gute zum Tag der Deutschen Einheit!"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

---

## 4️⃣ Islamic Pack Example

```
JSON

{  
  "packId": "islam.core",  
  "version": "1.0.0",  
  "calendarSystems": \["hijri"\],  
  "events": \[  
    {  
      "id": "eid\_al\_fitr",  
      "domain": "religious",  
      "traditionTags": \["islam"\],  
      "calendarSystem": "hijri",  
      "rule": {  
        "type": "hijri",  
        "month": 10,  
        "day": 1  
      },  
      "priority": 90  
    }  
  \],  
  "lexicon": \[  
    {  
      "eventId": "eid\_al\_fitr",  
      "locale": "tr-TR",  
      "script": "latin",  
      "template": "Bayramınız kutlu olsun!"  
    },  
    {  
      "eventId": "eid\_al\_fitr",  
      "locale": "ar",  
      "script": "arabic",  
      "template": "عيد مبارك"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

---

# X. What We Explicitly Delegate to CLDR

We DO NOT encode:

-   Month names
    
-   Date formatting patterns
    
-   Number systems
    
-   Timezone names
    
-   Pluralization logic
    

We rely on:

```
TypeScript

new Intl.DateTimeFormat(locale, options)  
new Intl.PluralRules(locale)  
new Intl.Locale(locale)
```

---

# XI. What This Architecture Guarantees

✔ Multi-calendar  
✔ Multi-religion  
✔ Multi-language  
✔ Multi-script  
✔ Diaspora blending  
✔ Data-driven expansion  
✔ Minimal client payload  
✔ Deterministic behavior  
✔ Clean plugin boundaries  
✔ CLDR-compatible

---

# XII. What We Have Achieved

We have:

-   Frozen boundaries
    
-   Avoided reinventing CLDR
    
-   Isolated semantic layer from rendering
    
-   Defined extensible plugin contracts
    
-   Defined canonical JSON pack schema
    

This is now production-grade architecture, not speculative design.

-   Add a formal JSON Schema draft (Draft 2020-12 compliant).
    
-   Design a minimal reference implementation skeleton.
    
-   Or simulate end-to-end flow for three users on same date using this model.

```
        "source": { "type": "string" },  
        "updated": { "type": "string", "format": "date-time" }  
      }  
    }  
  },  
  "$defs": {  
    "calendarSystemId": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "minLength": 1,  
      "description": "Calendar system identifier. Examples: 'gregorian', 'hijri', 'westernEaster', 'orthodoxPascha', 'chineseLunisolar'."

      "type": "string",

        },

          "items": { "type": "string", "minLength": 1 },  
          "uniqueItems": true,  
          "description": "Jurisdiction tags (often BCP-47 locale-like, e.g. \['de-DE'\], \['el-GR'\])."  
        },  
  
        "calendarSystem": { "$ref": "#/$defs/calendarSystemId" },

        },

        },

        }  
      }  
    },  
  
    "greetingLexiconEntry": {  
      "type": "object",  
      "additionalProperties": false,

          "type": "string",

        },

          "minLength": 1,

        },  
        "formality": { "$ref": "#/$defs/formality" },

        }  
      }  
    },  
  
    "calendarRule": {  
      "description": "Union of supported calendar rules. Extend via schema versioning.",  
      "oneOf": \[  
        { "$ref": "#/$defs/fixedDateRule" },  
        { "$ref": "#/$defs/nthWeekdayRule" },  
        { "$ref": "#/$defs/relativeToWesternEasterRule" },

          "type": "integer",
```

You: 

Suppose we want to build a graphical user interface or a demo website showing all the capabilities of our package. Then I would imagine such a website would look like very nicely styled and in bold big letters the eventual greeting. And then in some settings panel to the left or the right or whatever, all the configuration options. So the users would visit the website, then the website would take from what the browser gives us the system language and all locale and cultural information it can get from the browser to populate the values for the configuration. So if the user's language is Dutch, then of course we would take that value. We would of course take the time zone, the date, and so forth. But then the information the user should provide. So for a new user, they would be greeted in their system language with a general or generic greeting. And maybe at this point it would be useful to also say we should include a welcome greeting. So like in Greek, this would be kalosorisate or something. And then have the user fill in all the more detailed data, like their given name or given names, like their religious tradition, and all the many configuration options we have as multi-select drop-downs populated with all the values, of course, we support. And then have the greeting live, being live updated while the user compliments and completes their configurations.

ChatGPT: 

That demo UI concept is exactly the right way to “make the engine legible”: **one big greeting output**, and a **side panel that exposes every axis** (locale, calendar systems, traditions, script, formality, memory suppression, etc.), with **live recomputation**.

Below is a concrete, implementation-ready UX + data-flow proposal that matches your architecture and leverages what the browser can infer.

---

## 1) Page layout

### Center stage

-   **Huge greeting** (single line / two lines max), bold, typographic “hero” style
    
-   Under it (smaller, optional “explain” line):
    
    -   “Selected event: Eid al-Fitr”
        
    -   “Language/script: tr-TR / Latin”
        
    -   “Suppressed alternatives: German Unity Day (already greeted this year)”
        
-   A small “chips row” for quick context:
    
    -   `Opening | Closing`
        
    -   `Now: 2026-03-02 15:xx Europe/Brussels`
        
    -   `Residence: de-DE`
        
    -   `Affiliations: islam`
        

### Side panel (left or right)

Tabbed or accordion sections:

1.  **Browser Defaults (read-only)**
    
2.  **User Profile**
    
3.  **Affiliations**
    
4.  **Calendars**
    
5.  **Rendering**
    
6.  **Memory**
    
7.  **Packs / Data**
    

The goal is: users can *see* how each knob changes the greeting.

---

## 2) What the browser can auto-populate

You can fill a “Defaults” box from standard Web APIs:

### Language / locale

-   `navigator.languages` (priority-ordered language preferences)
    
-   `navigator.language` (primary)
    

### Time zone

-   `Intl.DateTimeFormat().resolvedOptions().timeZone`
    

### Current date/time

-   `new Date()` (plus time zone display)
    

### Script inference (optional)

You can’t reliably infer “script preference” from browser alone, but you can:

-   default `scriptPreference` based on locale:
    
    -   `zh-CN` → `simplified`
        
    -   `zh-TW`/`zh-HK` → `traditional`
        
    -   `ar-*` → `arabic`
        
    -   else → `latin`
        

### Cultural/religious affiliation

Browsers do **not** provide religion. That must be user-specified (explicitly).

---

## 3) The demo onboarding flow (minimal friction)

### First visit

-   Use **system UI language** for the demo’s UI.
    
-   Generate a generic greeting in that language via your pack system:
    
    -   if nothing available, fallback to a neutral “Hello”.
        

### Guided progressive disclosure

Show the greeting immediately, then prompt:

1.  “Your name(s)?” (optional)
    
2.  “Where do you live?” (defaults to system locale; editable)
    
3.  “Which traditions matter for greetings?” (multi-select)
    
4.  “Preferred greeting language?” (defaults to system language)
    
5.  “Script preference?” (auto default; editable)
    

As they type/select, the greeting updates live.

---

## 4) Settings panel schema (what you should expose)

### A) User Profile

-   Given names (multi-input, max 4)
    
-   Birthday (date picker)
    
-   Formality: `informal | formal`
    
-   Mode: `opening | closing`
    

### B) Locale axes (critical for diaspora)

-   Residence locale (civil jurisdiction): e.g. `de-DE`
    
-   UI locale (demo UI language): e.g. `nl-BE`
    
-   Greeting language: e.g. `tr-TR`
    
-   Script preference: `latin | arabic | simplified | traditional | native`
    

### C) Cultural affiliations (multi-select)

-   civil (residence)
    
-   islam
    
-   orthodox
    
-   catholic
    
-   protestant
    
-   anglican
    
-   chinese\_traditional
    
-   (future…)
    

Also add toggles:

-   “Participate in residence civil calendar” (on/off)
    
-   “Include culturally-civil holidays (Christmas etc.)” (on/off)
    
-   “Enable namedays” (on/off)
    

### D) Calendar systems (mostly hidden, but useful for demo)

Show active calendar plugins:

-   Gregorian
    
-   Hijri (tabular / Umm al-Qura variant selection)
    
-   Western Easter
    
-   Orthodox Pascha
    
-   Chinese lunisolar
    

This is great for demonstrating *why* dates differ.

### E) Memory / anti-repetition

This is a killer feature for the demo.

Expose:

-   “Repetition policy”:
    
    -   never repeat same event within window
        
    -   allow repeat daily
        
    -   allow repeat per session
        
-   A table of stored keys (editable):
    
    -   `newyear:2026`
        
    -   `month:2026-03`
        
    -   `eid_al_fitr:2026`
        
-   Buttons:
    
    -   “Clear memory”
        
    -   “Simulate returning user”
        
    -   “Simulate first login this year”
        

### F) Packs / Data

-   Pack list with status:
    
    -   loaded / missing
        
    -   version
        
    -   source URL
        
-   Buttons:
    
    -   “Load recommended packs”
        
    -   “Reload packs”
        
    -   “Offline mode (use cached only)”
        
-   Optional: show resolved event candidates list
    

---

## 5) Live updating: the clean state model

Treat the demo as a pure function:

**UI State → Engine Inputs → Engine Output**

### State slices

1.  `DetectedDefaults` (from browser, read-only)
    
2.  `UserConfig` (editable)
    
3.  `LoadedPacks` (events + lexicon)
    
4.  `MemoryStore` (mutable)
    

### Reactive compute

On every state change:

-   `engine.generate(context)` is called
    
-   output greeting updates instantly
    

This becomes a perfect “truth table explorer” for the package.

---

## 6) “Explainability” panel (optional but very valuable)

Under the greeting, add an “Explain” accordion:

-   Candidates found today (sorted):
    
    1.  Eid al-Fitr (priority 90) ✅ selected
        
    2.  German Unity Day (priority 80) ⛔ not active today / or suppressed
        
    3.  Time-of-day (priority 30)
        
-   Why chosen:
    
    -   “User has affiliation: islam”
        
    -   “Lexicon matched: tr-TR / latin”
        
    -   “Not suppressed by memory”
        

This is the single best way to demonstrate capabilities to developers.

---

## 7) Welcome greetings (your suggestion)

Yes — include a “welcome” semantic event class.

But keep it **separate** from time-of-day:

-   `eventId: welcome_opening`
    
-   triggered only on:
    
    -   first ever visit (no user id) OR
        
    -   first visit after onboarding completion OR
        
    -   explicit “welcome mode” toggle in demo
        

Language-specific templates:

-   Greek: «Καλώς ήρθες / Καλώς ήρθατε»
    
-   German: “Willkommen!” / “Willkommen zurück!”
    
-   Turkish: “Hoş geldiniz!”
    
-   Chinese: “欢迎！” / “欢迎回来！”
    

This makes the demo feel immediately rich, even before calendars enter.

---

## 8) Practical implementation note

If you build this in React/TypeScript (ShadCN fits nicely), you’ll likely want:

-   `useMemo` for engine generation
    
-   a lightweight in-memory store that can persist to localStorage for demo continuity
    
-   pack loader that supports:
    
    -   `fetch(url)` + ETag caching
        
    -   fall back to local embedded minimal pack for offline demo baseline

1.  A **wireframe-level component inventory** (exact UI components, controls, and their data bindings), or
    
2.  A **React + TypeScript single-page demo skeleton** that implements:
    
    -   browser default detection
        
    -   live greeting rendering

### 📦 **npm Package Name Check for “salve”**

I checked the npm registry: there *is already a package published under the name **`salve`*** — specifically, a JavaScript library for XML validation based on RelaxNG. [npmjs.com](https://www.npmjs.com/package/salve?activeTab=dependents&utm_source=chatgpt.com)

-   Anyone publishing under that name would cause a conflict with the existing package.
    

---

## ✅ What You *Can* Do Instead

Since `salve` is taken, the clean alternatives that keep your intended branding and remain available are:

### 1️⃣ **Use a scoped package name**

Scoped packages are common on npm and fit modern package conventions:

```
Plain text

@salve/core  
@salve/engine  
@salve/packs
```

This means you can still call the project *Salve* in all branding, but publish under an npm scope.

Scoped packages have these advantages:

-   Distinguish your project from the existing `salve` package
    
-   You can publish multiple related modules under `@salve`
    
-   They are namespace-safe
    

Example:

```
npm publish @salve/core --access public
```

---

## 🧠 Why Scoped is Probably the Best for You

Given the size and ambition of your project, scoped packages make structural sense:

-   It supports multiple modules:
    
    -   `@salve/core`
        
    -   `@salve/calendars`
        
    -   `@salve/registry`
        
    -   `@salve/render`
        
-   It makes versioning and dependency management clean
    
-   It avoids an outright name collision on npm
    

---

## 📌 Recommendations for Namespacing

Here are well-structured npm scope families you could register:

🔹 **@salve** – primary scope

-   `@salve/core` – engine + types
    
-   `@salve/calendars` – calendar plugins
    
-   `@salve/registry` – pack loader/fetcher
    
-   `@salve/render` – lexicon/template renderer
    
-   `@salve/packs-el-GR` – Greek packs
    
-   `@salve/packs-de-DE` – German packs
    
-   etc.
    

This mirrors your modular architecture.

---

## 📍 Next Steps You Could Take

1.  **Reserve the npm scope** `@salve`
    
    -   If you have an npm account, scopes are easy to create.
        
    -   E.g., `npm adduser` then `npm init --scope=@salve`
        
2.  **Reserve the GitHub org / org name** `Salve`  
    This gives you:
    
    -   `github.com/Salve`
        
    -   Teams + repo structure under one namespace
        
3.  **Check domain names**
    
    -   `salve.dev`, `salve.org`, `salve.io` — these would align with your brand.
        
    -   Even if you don’t buy now, early check is useful.

You: 

I think we should recapitulate a few important design decisions before we go further structuring the architecture of our Solvate package and packages, because I already mentioned that we would like to have a very efficient organization of data and data packages such that the Solvate package is configurable and does not have unnecessary and potentially very big data embedded into each and every build. That's why I propose we should have the localization data, especially huge lists of name day mappings, that we should have them and keep and maintain them separately in their own data packages and make them available either through a static file server, like GitHub, or maybe have them offered through an API, which I would not recommend because of the maintainability, but also to have it included during builds. So what I would want to propose is that the build scripts for the Solvate package is that when developers decide to add our Solvate module, to their software, to their application, they should be able to cherry pick, pick and choose whatever they need to be built in right into their app. So if they say, for example, they do not want to support Greek name days or Bulgarian name dates or what have you, then they should be able to just select those packages they want, say they do not want to support Chinese or the Arabic feasts and so on, then they should be able to only select those things they would need, in which case the payload would be minimal and then the data could be built in right into the customly built package in those developers' applications without having to rely on any third-party service or data source. Now, on the other hand, if a developer would like to add the full salve capabilities to their application, so including lots of localized and very specific data files like name days and so on, then they should have the option to either build everything and all into their software, thereby increasing the payload possibly gigantically, or have it configured such that they indeed will rely on a third-party source to be consumed, which would then be our GitHub-hosted static files. or NPM packages that only keep data for a specific localization option. What else are our options? How could we make this real, enable everything and all the various use cases and configuration options I just described? How could we organize those things elegantly and robustly? For example, one thing I was wondering, would it be possible to publish, say, the Greek name days as a separate self-compatible package to NPM and then have apps that use the self module load those NPM packages at runtime on the fly, or is this not possible? And if so, then how would you propose we deal with this use case?

ChatGPT: 

You’re describing a **three-mode distribution problem**:

1.  **Minimal payload / no big datasets**
    
2.  **Cherry-pick built-in datasets at build time** (offline-first, no network)
    
3.  **“Full capabilities” via remote packs** (static hosting, cached client-side)
    

That’s exactly the right framing. Here are the robust options, and the constraints that decide between them.

---

## A. The hard constraint: “load npm packages at runtime” (in the browser)

In a **browser** app, you cannot “npm install at runtime”. npm is a build-time tool.

What you *can* do at runtime is:

-   dynamically `import()` JavaScript modules **that are already bundled** (code-splitting), or
    
-   dynamically load **remote ES modules** / scripts by URL, or
    
-   fetch **static JSON** (packs) and hydrate your engine, or
    
-   use a Service Worker to cache fetched packs offline.
    

So the *right answer* is:

-   **Runtime loading is possible**, but **not as “npm packages” in the npm sense**.
    
-   It’s possible as **remote modules** (ESM) or **remote JSON packs** hosted anywhere (GitHub Pages, CDN, your own domain).
    

---

## B. The cleanest architecture: “Core + Packs” with 3 distribution modes

### 1) Core engine (always small)

`@salve/core` contains:

-   types + engine + policy + memory
    
-   pack loader interfaces
    
-   no big datasets
    

### 2) Packs (data-only, separable)

Example:

-   `@salve/pack-de-civil`
    
-   `@salve/pack-el-orthodox`
    
-   `@salve/pack-el-namedays`
    
-   `@salve/pack-islam-core`
    
-   `@salve/pack-zh-tradition`
    

Each pack is publishable in *two forms*:

**(a) as JSON** (for remote hosting)  
**(b) as JS module exporting JSON** (for bundler inclusion)

This dual-form strategy covers *all* your modes.

---

## C. Three ways developers can “select what they need”

### Mode 1 — Build-time inclusion (offline, minimal external dependencies)

Developer installs only what they want:

```
Bash

npm i @salve/core @salve/pack-de-civil @salve/pack-islam-core
```

Bundler includes those modules. Payload scales with selection.

How it works:

-   Packs export `pack: GreetingPack` object.
    
-   The app registers packs at startup.
    

Pros:

-   Fully offline
    
-   No runtime network
    
-   Deterministic versions
    

Cons:

-   Bigger bundle
    

This is the “cherry pick” mode you want.

---

### Mode 2 — Runtime fetch of static JSON packs (max capability, minimal bundle)

Developer installs only `@salve/core` (and maybe a tiny “starter pack”):

At runtime:

-   fetch `https://…/packs/el-namedays.v1.json`
    
-   cache in IndexedDB/localStorage
    
-   hydrate engine
    

Pros:

-   Tiny app bundle
    
-   Easy to update packs without redeploying app
    
-   Your GitHub Pages static hosting is ideal
    

Cons:

-   Requires network at least once (but can be cached)
    
-   Needs integrity/versioning controls
    

This is your “full capabilities via hosted files” mode.

---

### Mode 3 — Hybrid (best practice)

Ship:

-   civil basics in bundle
    
-   heavy nameday packs remote
    

Example: Always bundle time-of-day + New Year + residence civil holidays; fetch namedays only if user enables them or if onboarding needs it.

Pros:

-   fast first render
    
-   still small
    
-   namedays are “on demand”
    

This is likely the default “recommended” setup.

---

## D. How to make packs “data-only” without JS overhead

For huge datasets (namedays), you want:

-   **compressed JSON**
    
-   **partitioning**
    
-   **fetch-by-need**
    
-   **optional precompilation**
    

### Partitioning strategies (crucial)

Instead of one huge `el-namedays.json`, split into:

1.  **By month**: `el-namedays/01.json … 12.json`
    
2.  **By initial letter**: `el-namedays/a.json … ω.json`
    
3.  **By hashed buckets**: `el-namedays/bucket-00..ff.json`
    
4.  **By saint-day** rather than name: date → saints → names (often smaller / more canonical)
    

Your earlier insight is right: **for a user you only need a few resolved dates**.

So the best split is usually:

### ✅ Two-stage nameday design (efficient)

1.  Pack A: **Calendar of namedays by date** (small-ish)
    
    -   `MM-DD → saintIds[]`
        
2.  Pack B: **Name index** (can be partitioned)
    
    -   `normalizedName → saintIds[]`
        

Then at onboarding:

-   resolve user’s names once
    
-   store only the resolved `saintId + MM-DD`
    
-   no need to keep the giant index locally
    

This is elegant and scalable across cultures.

---

## E. Can we “lazy-load built-in packs” without network?

Yes, **via code splitting**.

Even if packs are installed at build time, you can keep initial payload small by dynamically importing them:

```
TypeScript

if (userEnablesNamedays) {  
  const { pack } \= await import("@salve/pack-el-namedays");  
  engine.registerPack(pack);  
}
```

This still requires the pack to be in the deployed build, but not loaded upfront.

This gives developers:

-   no network dependency
    
-   progressive loading
    
-   smaller initial JS parse cost
    

---

## F. Remote packs: integrity, caching, and version pinning

If you support remote packs, do it robustly:

### 1) Pack manifest index

A small index file:

-   available packs
    
-   versions
    
-   URLs
    
-   SHA-256 hashes
    
-   sizes
    

### 2) Integrity enforcement

-   pack JSON includes `integrity.sha256`
    
-   loader verifies content hash before accepting
    

### 3) Caching

-   IndexedDB cache keyed by `(packId, version)`
    
-   optional Service Worker prefetch
    

### 4) Version pinning

Developers can choose:

-   pin exact versions (deterministic)
    
-   “latest compatible minor” policy
    

---

## G. The clean developer experience (DX)

Provide a **single configuration object**:

```
TypeScript

Salve.configure({  
  packs: {  
    builtIn: \["de-civil", "islam-core"\],  
    remote: \[  
      { id: "el-namedays", version: "1.2.0", url: "…" }  
    \]  
  },  
  namedays: { enabled: true, strategy: "onboarding-resolve-and-store" }  
})
```

And provide a CLI for pack management.

---

## H. Optional but powerful: a `salve` CLI

A CLI makes this “real”:

### `salve add`

-   installs pack deps
    
-   updates app config
    

### `salve pack:bundle`

-   creates a single minimized JSON bundle of selected packs
    
-   optionally compresses & splits
    
-   emits `salve-packs.bundle.json`
    

This is exactly what you described: “customly built package”.

It’s not an API service; it’s a build tool.

---

## I. Recommended final option set

To cover all use cases robustly, implement **all three**:

1.  **Build-time pack inclusion** (npm deps)
    
2.  **Runtime remote JSON packs** (GitHub/CDN)
    
3.  **Hybrid** (recommended default)
    

And explicitly state:

-   “Runtime npm install is not a thing in the browser”
    
-   “Runtime pack loading is via fetch/import(url)”
    

---

## J. Direct answer to your runtime question

> “Could we publish Greek namedays as a separate npm package and load it at runtime on the fly?”

-   In a browser: **not via npm**, but:
    
    -   **Yes** via `import()` **if it’s included in the build** (code-splitting), or
        
    -   **Yes** via fetching the pack as **remote JSON** or **remote ESM module** (URL-based).
        

If you want “runtime plug-in install” in a browser, the practical route is:

✅ remote JSON packs  
or ✅ remote ESM packs (more advanced; JSON is simpler and safer)

---

## What I’d propose as the canonical design decision

1.  Packs are the unit of distribution.
    
2.  Packs are publishable as **npm modules** and as **static JSON artifacts**.
    
3.  The loader supports both:
    
    -   `registerPack(packObject)` (bundled)
        
    -   `loadPackFromUrl(url, integrity)` (remote)
        
4.  Namedays have a dedicated “resolve-onboarding” workflow to avoid shipping huge indices to everyone.
    

---

If you want next, I’ll draft:

-   the **Pack Manifest JSON schema** (`pack-index.schema.json`) including hashes, sizes, URLs
    
-   the **TypeScript loader interfaces** for `PackSource` (bundled vs remote), cache strategy, integrity verification
    
-   the **nameday two-stage schema** (date→saintIds + name→saintIds partitioning)

# I. Pack Manifest JSON Schema (Registry Index)

This is not a single pack.  
This is the **catalog of packs** available remotely.

### Purpose

-   Discover packs
    
-   Version control
    
-   Integrity verification
    
-   Size estimation
    
-   Enable partial downloads
    

---

## `pack-index.schema.json`

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://json-schema.org/draft/2020-12/schema",  
  "$id": "https://salve.dev/schema/pack-index.schema.json",  
  "title": "Salve Pack Index",  
  "type": "object",  
  "required": \["registryVersion", "packs"\],  
  "properties": {  
    "registryVersion": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "pattern": "^\\\\d+\\\\.\\\\d+\\\\.\\\\d+$"  
    },  
    "generatedAt": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "format": "date-time"  
    },  
    "packs": {  
      "type": "array",  
      "items": {  
        "$ref": "#/$defs/packEntry"  
      }  
    }  
  },  
  "$defs": {  
    "packEntry": {  
      "type": "object",  
      "required": \["id", "version", "url", "integrity", "size"\],  
      "properties": {  
        "id": { "type": "string" },  
        "version": { "type": "string" },  
        "locale": { "type": "string" },  
        "tradition": { "type": "string" },  
        "url": { "type": "string", "format": "uri" },  
        "integrity": {  
          "type": "object",  
          "required": \["sha256"\],  
          "properties": {  
            "sha256": {  
              "type": "string",  
              "pattern": "^\[A-Fa-f0-9\]{64}$"  
            }  
          }  
        },  
        "size": {  
          "type": "integer",  
          "description": "Uncompressed byte size"  
        },  
        "dependencies": {  
          "type": "array",  
          "items": { "type": "string" }  
        }  
      }  
    }  
  }  
}
```

---

# II. TypeScript Loader Interfaces

We abstract pack source.

## PackSource abstraction

```
TypeScript

export interface PackSource {  
  id: string  
  version: string  
  
  load(): Promise<GreetingPack\>  
}
```

---

## 1️⃣ Built-in pack source

```
TypeScript

export class StaticPackSource implements PackSource {  
  constructor(  
    public id: string,  
    public version: string,  
    private pack: GreetingPack  
  ) {}  
  
  async load(): Promise<GreetingPack\> {  
    return this.pack  
  }  
}
```

---

## 2️⃣ Remote JSON pack source

```
TypeScript

export interface RemotePackOptions {  
  url: string  
  integrity?: string  
  cache?: boolean  
}  
  
export class RemotePackSource implements PackSource {  
  constructor(  
    public id: string,  
    public version: string,  
    private options: RemotePackOptions  
  ) {}  
  
  async load(): Promise<GreetingPack\> {  
    const response \= await fetch(this.options.url)  
    const json \= await response.json()  
  
    if (this.options.integrity) {  
      await verifyIntegrity(json, this.options.integrity)  
    }  
  
    return json as GreetingPack  
  }  
}
```

---

## Loader Manager

```
TypeScript

export interface PackLoader {  
  register(source: PackSource): Promise<void\>  
}
```

The engine does not care where packs come from.

---

# III. Canonical Saint Identity Model

This is where we solve your cross-language problem properly.

Do NOT use names as primary keys.

Use:

```
saintId = stable, language-neutral identifier
```

Example:

```
saint.john.baptist  
saint.john.apostle  
saint.mary.magdalene
```

---

## Saint Core Schema

```
JSON

{  
  "saintId": "saint.john.baptist",  
  "canonicalName": "John the Baptist",  
  "feastDates": {  
    "gregorian": "06-24",  
    "orthodox": "01-07"  
  }  
}
```

This decouples:

-   saint identity
    
-   localized name forms
    
-   date
    

---

# IV. The Real Problem: Name Matching

Now your question:

> If user is “Jan” in Dutch  
> and greeting language is Greek  
> how do we map to Ioannis / Giannis etc?

We need:

1.  Cross-language name equivalence
    
2.  Variant normalization
    
3.  Fuzzy handling
    

---

## Step 1 — Normalize input

Before matching:

-   lowercase
    
-   remove diacritics
    
-   transliterate to Latin
    
-   strip punctuation
    
-   collapse double letters
    
-   normalize digraphs
    

Example:

```
Γιάννης → giannis  
Ioánnis → ioannis  
Yannis → yannis  
Jan → jan
```

---

## Step 2 — Alias Table

You need a **global name alias index**.

Structure:

```
JSON

{  
  "normalized": "jan",  
  "saintIds": \["saint.john.baptist"\]  
}
```

But do NOT store this flat for all names globally in every app.

Instead:

### Partitioned alias index

For Greek namedays pack:

```
/aliases/j.json
```

```
JSON

{  
  "jan": \["saint.john.baptist"\],  
  "yannis": \["saint.john.baptist"\],  
  "ioannis": \["saint.john.baptist"\],  
  "γιάννης": \["saint.john.baptist"\]  
}
```

This allows:

-   fetch by initial letter
    
-   minimal partitioning
    

This is why I mentioned partitioning earlier.

---

## Step 3 — Cross-Language Equivalence

The saint is the bridge.

We do NOT match Jan → Giannis.

We match:

```
Jan → saint.john.baptist  
saint.john.baptist → Greek localized form → Ιωάννης
```

So:

1.  Input name → alias lookup → saintId
    
2.  saintId → pack locale name rendering
    

This is clean, scalable, and language-neutral.

---

## Step 4 — Fuzzy Matching

We want light fuzzy, not heavy NLP.

Use:

-   normalized string equality
    
-   small edit distance threshold (Levenshtein ≤ 1 or 2)
    
-   prefix match for short names
    
-   phonetic matching optional (Double Metaphone for Latin)
    

But DO NOT ship massive fuzzy dictionaries.

Strategy:

1.  Try exact normalized match.
    
2.  If no result:
    
    -   Try diacritic-stripped match.
        
3.  If no result:
    
    -   Try edit-distance threshold.
        
4.  If still no result:
    
    -   Suggest closest matches.
        

But only inside alias partition loaded for first letter.

---

## V. Efficient Name-Day Dataset Strategy

You are correct that splitting by month does not help name resolution.

Correct partitioning is:

### A) Date Map (small, always included)

```
MM-DD → saintIds\[\]
```

This is tiny.

---

### B) Alias Index (partitioned by first letter or hash)

```
aliases/a.json  
aliases/b.json  
...  
aliases/γ.json
```

Only load relevant partition during onboarding.

---

### C) Saint Definitions (small)

```
saints.json
```

Contains:

-   saintId
    
-   localized names per locale
    
-   feast dates per tradition
    

This is moderate size, but manageable.

---

## Onboarding Workflow

1.  User enters: “Jan”
    
2.  Normalize → “jan”
    
3.  Load `aliases/j.json`
    
4.  Find saintId
    
5.  Fetch feast date from date map
    
6.  Store locally:
    

```
JSON

{  
  "resolvedNames": \[  
    {  
      "input": "Jan",  
      "saintId": "saint.john.baptist",  
      "feastDate": "06-24"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

From now on, no alias index needed.

---

# VI. Why This Architecture Is Correct

It:

-   Avoids storing 4,000 names in every app
    
-   Avoids shipping fuzzy dictionaries globally
    
-   Is language-agnostic
    
-   Is saint-centric (stable identity)
    
-   Supports transliteration
    
-   Supports cross-script
    
-   Is modular
    
-   Is scalable to Chinese, Islamic, Bulgarian
    

---

# VII. Final Answer to Your Question

To fuzzy-match Jan ↔ Giannis ↔ Ioannis:

-   Normalize both
    
-   Use alias index
    
-   Map to saintId
    
-   Render localized name from saint data
    

Do not attempt direct cross-language string mapping.

Saint identity is the universal pivot.

-   Or design the **name normalization pipeline in TypeScript**
    
-   Or simulate the full resolution process step-by-step

# 12️⃣ What This Solves

| Problem | Solution |
| --- | --- |

You: 

I was wondering, in Arabic, or at least for Muslims, shouldn't we have the more customly used Salam alaikum instead of hello?

ChatGPT: 

Yes — absolutely.

If we are aiming for **culturally authentic, expectation-aligned greetings**, then for:

-   Arabic language context
    
-   or Muslim religious affiliation
    

the baseline greeting should not default to a neutral “hello”.

It should prefer:

> **السلام عليكم**  
> *as-salāmu ʿalaykum*  
> “Peace be upon you.”

That is the culturally normative greeting across Arabic-speaking societies and widely used among Muslims globally — even outside Arabic contexts.

---

## The Design Question for Salve

This is not merely lexical — it’s a **priority resolution issue**.

We must decide:

When should “Salam alaikum” win over a generic hello?

There are three possible triggers:

### 1️⃣ Language-based trigger (weak)

If `greetingLanguage === "ar"`

→ use **السلام عليكم**

Problem:  
Not all Arabic UI contexts are religious.  
Some Arabic users prefer secular greetings.

---

### 2️⃣ Affiliation-based trigger (stronger)

If `affiliations.includes("islam")`

→ prefer Islamic greeting forms

This is culturally accurate in most cases.

---

### 3️⃣ Combined trigger (most precise)

If:

-   `greetingLanguage === "ar"`  
    OR
    
-   `affiliations.includes("islam")`
    

AND not explicitly overridden

→ use Salam alaikum

This gives correct behavior for:

-   Turkish Muslim user
    
-   English-speaking Muslim user
    
-   Arabic-speaking secular user (if affiliation not set, fallback possible)
    

---

## What Salve Should Probably Do

I recommend introducing a **Greeting Style Preference Layer**:

```
Plain text

greetingStyle:  
  - secular  
  - religious  
  - auto (default)
```

Then:

If `auto`:

-   If Islamic affiliation → use Salam alaikum
    
-   Else if Arabic locale but no affiliation → use neutral Arabic greeting (مرحبا)
    
-   Else fallback normally
    

This gives developers full control.

---

## Lexicon Layer Update

We should expand the islam.core lexicon:

```
Plain text

eventId: baseline\_greeting\_islamic  
locale: ar  
script: arabic  
template: السلام عليكم{{#name}} {{name}}{{/name}}
```

And optionally:

```
Plain text

locale: tr-TR  
template: Selamün aleyküm
```

And for English-speaking Muslim contexts:

```
Plain text

locale: en-GB  
template: As-salamu alaykum
```

---

## Important Subtlety

The canonical response to Salam alaikum is:

> **وعليكم السلام**

Salve does not yet model conversational reciprocity.  
But in the future, we could add:

-   Mode: initiating greeting
    
-   Mode: responding greeting
    

This would make Salve extremely sophisticated.

---

## Conclusion

Yes — for Muslim users, Salam alaikum should be the culturally correct baseline greeting.

We should not treat Arabic as just another language;  
we must treat religious greeting conventions as first-class resolution rules.

Always.

Because:

Where:

### maximal (default)

Use everything available:

-   name days

Use:

-   civil + seasonal + personal
    
-   suppress baseline religious formulas (e.g., Salam)

---

# 1️⃣ Do Russia, Belarus, Ukraine, Serbia etc. have name days?

Short answer: **Yes — but not in the same way everywhere.**

## Eastern Orthodox Countries

The following have name-day traditions:

-   Russia
    
-   Belarus
    
-   Ukraine
    
-   Serbia
    
-   Bulgaria
    
-   North Macedonia
    
-   Romania (Orthodox majority, but linguistically Romance)
    
-   Georgia (Orthodox, but non-Slavic)
    

However:

### 🔹 Greece

Name days are extremely prominent socially.

### 🔹 Bulgaria

Also prominent and widely celebrated.

### 🔹 Serbia

Important, but mixed with:

-   Slava (family patron saint feast — extremely important, more so than personal name day)
    

### 🔹 Russia / Belarus / Ukraine

Yes, name days exist.  
But:

-   Less socially dominant than in Greece.
    
-   Historically tied to baptismal names.
    
-   Often known as “День ангела” (Angel Day).
    

So your intuition is correct:  
Name days are part of Orthodox Slavic tradition.

But culturally weighted differently.

---

# 2️⃣ Should We Have One Single Slavic Orthodox JSON?

No.

That would be architecturally wrong.

Here is why.

Even though:

-   The underlying saint calendar is largely shared
    
-   The Julian calendar plays a role
    
-   Many saints overlap
    

The **actual observance date** differs:

-   Some countries use Revised Julian
    
-   Some use Julian
    
-   Some use Gregorian
    
-   Ukraine is in transition (calendar reform ongoing)
    
-   Russia still uses Julian for many ecclesiastical dates
    

So if we merged into one “Slavic Orthodox” file:

We would introduce:

-   Calendar drift problems
    
-   Political sensitivity
    
-   Cultural flattening
    
-   Wrong dates for some regions
    

This violates your specificity doctrine.

---

# 3️⃣ Correct Architectural Approach

You want:

## Per-ecclesiastical-tradition pack

Not per-language, not per-ethnicity, but per:

-   Church calendar authority
    

So we define packs like:

```
Plain text

orthodox.greece  
orthodox.bulgaria  
orthodox.serbia  
orthodox.russia  
orthodox.ukraine  
orthodox.belarus  
orthodox.romania
```

Each pack defines:

-   Calendar system (Gregorian, Julian, Revised Julian)
    
-   Saint-to-date mapping
    
-   Localized saint name variants
    
-   Priority weights (cultural prominence)
    

This preserves:

-   Accuracy
    
-   Modularity
    
-   Political neutrality
    
-   Maximum specificity
    

---

# 4️⃣ Data Structure Recommendation

You earlier proposed:

> Date → Saint IDs  
> Name → Saint IDs

That is still correct.

For Orthodox Slavic, we would add:

```
JSON

{  
  "saintId": "ioannis\_the\_baptist",  
  "dates": {  
    "greece": "01-07",  
    "russia\_julian": "06-24",  
    "ukraine\_revised": "01-07"  
  },  
  "names": {  
    "ru": \["Иван", "Иоанн"\],  
    "uk": \["Іван"\],  
    "sr": \["Јован"\],  
    "bg": \["Иван"\],  
    "el": \["Ιωάννης"\]  
  }  
}
```

Then the pack selects:

-   Calendar rule
    
-   Language variants
    

---

# 5️⃣ Where Do We Find the Data?

This is the difficult part.

### Possible sources:

1.  Official Orthodox Church calendars (published annually)
    
2.  Liturgical books (Menologion)
    
3.  Public church websites
    
4.  National Orthodox portals
    
5.  Wikipedia (semi-reliable, must be normalized)
    
6.  Open datasets from religious publishers (rare)
    

For example:

-   Russian Orthodox Church publishes online calendars.
    
-   Serbian Orthodox Church publishes saint lists.
    
-   Greek Orthodox Archdiocese has digital calendars.
    

However:

They are not machine-readable.  
You will need:

-   Scraping
    
-   OCR
    
-   Manual normalization
    
-   Authority reconciliation
    

This becomes a serious data curation effort.

---

# 6️⃣ Important Subtlety

Even within one country:

The same name may have multiple saints.

Which date wins?

Traditionally:

-   First saint in calendar
    
-   Baptismal saint
    
-   Most prominent feast
    

You must define a deterministic rule.

Example policy:

```
Plain text

If multiple saints share name:  
  Choose highest liturgical rank.  
If equal rank:  
  Choose earliest in calendar year.
```

This must go into RFC.

---

# 7️⃣ Calendar Complexity

Orthodox countries differ in:

-   Fixed feasts
    
-   Moveable feasts (Easter-relative)
    
-   Julian vs Gregorian offset (13 days currently)
    

Therefore:

Each pack MUST declare:

```
JSON

{  
  "calendarSystem": "julian",  
  "julianOffset": 13  
}
```

So resolution engine can convert to Gregorian.

---

# 8️⃣ Conclusion

Your instinct is correct:

Yes, Slavic Orthodox countries have name days.

But:

We MUST NOT merge into a single Slavic Orthodox JSON.

Instead:

We create ecclesiastical-tradition-specific packs.

This fits perfectly with your modular data-distribution philosophy.

---

-   Design the canonical saint identity system
    
-   Design the cross-language saint ID ontology
    
-   Or draft the Orthodox pack schema formally for the RFC
    

This is becoming properly architectural now.

# 1️⃣ We Need a New Domain

Currently you have domains like:

-   personal
    
-   religious
    
-   civil
    
-   seasonal
    
-   temporal
    

You now need:

> **culturalBaseline**

These are:

-   Grüß Gott (Austria, Bavaria)
    
-   Aloha (Hawaii)
    
-   Howdy (Texas, Southern US informal)
    
-   Ciao (Italy informal baseline)
    
-   Bonjour (France default)
    
-   Salaam Alaikum (Islamic cultural baseline)
    

These are not events.  
They are culturally specific *default forms*.

---

# 2️⃣ Architectural Implication

Baseline greeting selection becomes layered:

### Layer A — Event-Based Specificity

If today is:

-   Nameday
    
-   Eid
    
-   Easter
    
-   National Day
    

→ Use event greeting.

### Layer B — Cultural Baseline Greeting

If no event overrides:  
→ Use culturally embedded baseline greeting.

### Layer C — Temporal Greeting

If no cultural baseline available:  
→ Guten Morgen / Good evening.

### Layer D — Generic Fallback

→ Hello.

---

# 3️⃣ Your Doctrine Applied

Under your maximal specificity principle:

If Austrian locale:  
→ Grüß Gott (default)

Not Guten Tag.

If Hawaiian locale:  
→ Aloha.

Not Hello.

If Texas informal:  
→ Howdy.

Not Hello.

This is correct within your philosophy.

---

We must not scatter these in random packs.

Instead:

Each cultural pack should optionally define:

```
JSON

"baselineGreetings": \[  
  {  
    "locale": "de-AT",

    "template": "Grüß Gott"  
  },  
  {  
    "locale": "de-AT",  
    "formality": "formal",  
    "template": "Grüß Gott"  
  }  
\]
```

Or:

```
JSON

{  
  "locale": "en-US-TX",  
  "formality": "informal",  
  "template": "Howdy"  
}
```

```
JSON
```

Resolution engine:

1.  Select packs by:
    
    -   residence locale

    -   affiliation

    -   personal events
        
    -   religious events
        
    -   civil events
        
    -   baseline greetings
        
    -   temporal greetings

You must not hardcode thousands of hyperlocal greetings.

You need:

Tiered coverage.

Level 1: Country-level baseline  
Level 2: Major cultural-region baseline  
Level 3: Optional micro-regional packs

So:

-   culture.austria
    
-   culture.bavaria
    
-   culture.texas
    
-   culture.hawaii
    

Optional add-ons.

Baseline greetings must have lower priority than:

-   Nameday
    
-   Feast
    
-   Civil holiday
    

But higher priority than:

-   Time-of-day greeting
    

So order becomes:

1.  Personal event
    
2.  Religious feast
    
3.  Civil holiday
    
4.  Cultural baseline
    
5.  Temporal greeting
    
6.  Generic fallback

Not every Austrian uses Grüß Gott.

Therefore:

-   Defaulted by pack
    
-   Overridable
    
-   Suppressible
    

But yes — default should be specific.

ChatGPT: 

Nagedacht gedurende 13s

I read the Wikipedia article, and it strongly suggests Salve can (and should) expand beyond “pick a phrase by date/time/locale” into a **general greeting protocol engine**: greetings are *relationship- and status-signaling*, often **multimodal** (spoken + gesture), sometimes **also used as farewells**, and exist in **written channels** too. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)

Here are the most concrete, Salve-relevant extensions implied by that page:

## 1) Add greeting “channels” beyond on-screen text

The article explicitly frames greetings as audible and physical, and also notes written greetings (letters/emails). [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** Salve should support *output modalities*:

-   `text` (UI string)
    
-   `spoken` (TTS-ready string; possibly with SSML later)
    
-   `written` (email/letter salutation style)
    
-   `voicemail` (voicemail-style opening/closing; the article calls this out as a subtopic) [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)
    
-   `gesture` (metadata-only: handshake/bow/namaste etc.)
    
-   `combined` (a “bundle”: phrase + gesture suggestion)
    

This keeps Salve “maximally specific” without forcing apps to physically enact gestures; apps can render an icon, microcopy, or onboarding hint.

## 2) Model greetings as relationship/status signaling, not just locale/time

The page emphasizes that greetings signal **relationship type** and **social status** (formal/informal), and vary with etiquette and context. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** promote these to first-class inputs:

-   `relationship`: stranger / acquaintance / friend / family / elder / superior / subordinate
    
-   `statusAsymmetry`: equal / I→superior / I→subordinate
    
-   `setting`: street-passing / entering-room / meeting / ceremony / online
    

This is bigger than “formality”; it’s a social graph axis.

## 3) Treat “greeting” and “farewell” as a unified act class

The article notes some languages/cultures use the same expression/gesture for greeting and farewell. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** Salve should resolve an `interactionPhase`:

-   `open`
    
-   `inPassing`
    
-   `close`
    

…and allow a **single lexeme** to map to both phases depending on locale/tradition (e.g., “ciao” type behavior).

## 4) Introduce a “gesture protocol” layer (opt-in output)

The article enumerates handshake, hugs, kisses, bows, hat-tipping, hand-on-heart, etc., and stresses gestures encode expectations. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** provide *gesture metadata*:

-   `gesture.type`: handshake / bow / namaste / hand-on-heart / cheek-kiss / wave / nod
    
-   `gesture.constraints`: same-sex only, opposite-sex restricted, conservative/public constraints, etc. (the article gives Morocco as an example of differing same/opposite-sex greeting norms). [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)
    
-   `gesture.handedness`: right-hand preferred (explicitly discussed re: Morocco). [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)
    

Salve can stay “text-first” while still being culturally complete.

## 5) Add “call-and-response” pairs as a distinct concept

Many greeting traditions have a preferred response (your earlier “wa ʿalaykum as-salām” point fits this). Wikipedia hints at paired responses (e.g., “adab” answered with “tasleem”). [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** each greeting can optionally define:

-   `expectedReply[]` (localized)
    
-   `replyTo` links
    

That unlocks chatbots and conversational UI.

## 6) Expand “baseline cultural greetings” as a formal tier

The article frames greetings as universal, culturally specific, and sometimes elaborate/ritualized. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)  
**Extension:** what you already proposed becomes canonical:

-   `baseline.cultural` (Grüß Gott, Aloha, Howdy, Salam alaikum, etc.)
    
-   `baseline.temporal` (morning/evening)
    
-   `baseline.welcome` (entering a place/app)
    
-   `baseline.parting` (good night, safe travels)
    

Under your “max specificity by default” doctrine, Salve should prefer `baseline.cultural` over `baseline.temporal`, unless suppressed.

---

## What this means for Salve’s roadmap

If we adopt the above, Salve evolves into:

**A culture-aware salutation protocol engine** that outputs:

-   a phrase (or bundle of phrases),
    
-   optional gesture metadata,
    
-   optional expected reply,
    
-   tuned by: time/date/calendar + locale + tradition + relationship/status + channel.
    

That is a very natural extension of what you’re already building, and it’s exactly what the greeting article says greetings *are* in human life. [Wikipedia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greeting)

If you want, next I can translate these into **RFC-style normative requirements** (MUST/SHOULD) and a clean **data model delta** for Salve (no code, but precise schema concepts).

This means that the channels you proposed, that these are very much out of scope. So spoken, voicemail, gesture, combined, these are all things we should not be seeking to support. We will only be dealing with written text-based greetings. What is indeed interesting is the relationship and status signaling beyond locale and time. So the relationship does matter. Up till now, we only have formal and informal, so this would translate to, in German, for example, duzen und sitzen, or in French, tutoyer. But indeed, we should also model into the SALVE relationship context, such that the developer using SALVE in their software project, can define their relationship to their users as a stranger, acquaintance, friend, family, or superior or subordinate, because we want Salesforce also to be usable for generating generic, localized, and specific greetings in, let's say, chat apps between users, where then the modeling of their relationship is important. ---

# 1️⃣ Hard Scope Boundary

Salve SHALL:

-   Generate **written, text-based greetings only**
    
-   Based on:
    
    -   time
        
    -   date
        
    -   calendar system
        
    -   locale
        
    -   cultural tradition
        
    -   personal identity (e.g. name day)
        
    -   relationship context
        
    -   interaction setting
        
-   Output only:
    
    -   written text strings
        
    -   optional paired response text (call–response)
        

Salve SHALL NOT:

-   Model gestures
    
-   Model spoken greetings
    
-   Model voicemail
    
-   Model multimodal interaction
    
-   Model physical etiquette
    

Those are out of scope.

This keeps the project clean and implementable.

---

# 2️⃣ What From Wikipedia Is Actually Relevant

Only two conceptual expansions matter:

### A) Relationship / Status Signaling

### B) Call-and-Response Pairing

Everything else is noise for SALVE.

---

# 3️⃣ Extending Salve: Relationship Context

You are right that “formal vs informal” is insufficient.

Currently you have:

-   formality: informal / formal
    

But that is linguistically narrow (du/Sie, tu/vous).

We need a **relationship model**.

However, it must remain minimal.

---

## Proposed Minimal Relationship Model

Introduce:

```
Plain text

relationshipContext:  
  - stranger  
  - acquaintance  
  - friend  
  - family  
  - superior  
  - subordinate
```

This is enough.

Not more.

No deep social graph modeling.

---

## Why This Matters

Because:

-   German:
    
    -   stranger → Guten Tag
        
    -   friend → Hallo
        
-   English:
    
    -   superior → Good afternoon
        
    -   friend → Hey
        
-   Arabic:
    
    -   stranger formal → السلام عليكم
        
    -   close friend → أهلاً
        

Formality is only one dimension.

Relationship is broader.

---

# 4️⃣ Setting Context (Minimal)

You are also correct that:

-   Passing on street
    
-   Entering room
    
-   Meeting ceremony
    

are meaningful.

But we must avoid explosion.

So we define:

```
Plain text

interactionSetting:  
  - direct\_address      (one-to-one)  
  - group\_address       (addressing group)  
  - public\_announcement  
  - chat\_message  
  - email\_opening  
  - email\_closing
```

Notice:

We do NOT model physical situations.

We model communication modes.

This is aligned with written-only scope.

---

# 5️⃣ Call-and-Response as First-Class Concept

This is the second important extension.

Some greetings are:

-   standalone
    
-   or require a canonical reply
    

Examples:

-   السلام عليكم → وعليكم السلام
    
-   Χριστός Ανέστη → Αληθώς Ανέστη
    
-   Grüß Gott → Gott grüß Sie (traditional reply)
    

Salve should support:

```
Plain text

greetingPair:  
  initiator: "Χριστός Ανέστη"  
  response: "Αληθώς Ανέστη"
```

And resolution mode:

```
Plain text

interactionRole:  
  - initiator  
  - responder
```

This is clean, powerful, and still fully text-based.

---

# 6️⃣ Revised Core Model of Salve

Now Salve’s input model becomes:

```
Plain text

timeContext  
calendarContext  
localeContext  
culturalTradition  
relationshipContext  
interactionSetting  
interactionRole  
formality
```

Output:

```
Plain text

greetingText  
(optional) expectedResponseText
```

That is it.

No gestures.  
No speech.

---

# 7️⃣ Specificity Hierarchy (Updated)

Now priority becomes:

1.  Personal event (birthday, name day)
    
2.  Religious feast
    
3.  Civil holiday
    
4.  Cultural baseline greeting
    
5.  Relationship-specific baseline
    
6.  Setting-specific greeting
    
7.  Temporal greeting
    
8.  Generic fallback
    

Notice that relationship and setting influence selection within a tier, not necessarily override higher tiers.

---

# 8️⃣ Important Constraint

We must prevent combinatorial explosion.

Therefore:

Relationship and setting SHOULD:

-   Select between lexicon variants
    
-   Not create new calendar events
    

They refine, not multiply.

Salve remains:

-   Deterministic
    
-   Modular
    
-   Cultural
    
-   Written-only
    
-   Elegant
    

It does not become a sociological simulation.

It remains a greeting resolution engine.

Excellent — these are exactly the kinds of culturally specific baseline forms that justify Salve’s existence.

Let’s analyze them carefully and model them cleanly.

---

# 1️⃣ Grüß Gott vs. Servus vs. Mahlzeit

These are three **distinct greeting classes**, not just variants.

## 🇦🇹 / 🇩🇪 (Bavaria, Austria)

### Grüß Gott

-   Region: Austria, Bavaria
    
-   Relationship: neutral / semi-formal
    
-   Religious origin, but culturally secularized
    
-   Used all day
    
-   Often default baseline in those regions
    

### Servus

-   Region: Austria, Bavaria, parts of South Germany
    
-   Informal
    
-   Used for greeting *and* farewell
    
-   Peer / friend / acquaintance
    
-   Strong regional identity marker
    

### Mahlzeit

-   Region: Germany, Austria (varies)
    
-   Time-bound: ~11:30–14:00
    
-   Workplace / cafeteria / office corridor context
    
-   Often neutral between colleagues
    
-   Not used outside lunch window
    
-   Not religious
    
-   Not event-based
    
-   Purely temporal-cultural hybrid
    

These are not interchangeable.

They belong to different rule layers.

---

# 2️⃣ How They Fit Into Salve’s Architecture

We now clearly see three baseline subtypes:

### A) Cultural Baseline (all-day)

-   Grüß Gott
    
-   Servus
    
-   Aloha
    
-   Howdy
    

### B) Cultural-Temporal Baseline

-   Mahlzeit (noon)
    
-   Buona sera (evening cultural baseline)
    
-   Buenas tardes
    
-   etc.
    

### C) Bidirectional Baseline (greeting + farewell)

-   Servus
    
-   Ciao
    

So Salve must support:

```
Plain text

baselineType:  
  - cultural\_all\_day  
  - cultural\_time\_bound  
  - bidirectional
```

---

# 3️⃣ Where Mahlzeit Fits in Specificity Order

Mahlzeit is interesting because:

It is:

-   time-sensitive
    
-   culturally specific
    
-   context-bound (workplace)
    

It should rank:

Below:

-   Feast days
    
-   Civil holidays
    
-   Name days
    

But above:

-   Guten Tag
    

So in priority:

1.  Personal event
    
2.  Religious feast
    
3.  Civil holiday
    
4.  Cultural time-bound baseline (Mahlzeit)
    
5.  Cultural all-day baseline (Grüß Gott)
    
6.  Relationship baseline (Servus informal)
    
7.  Generic time-of-day greeting
    
8.  Hello fallback
    

---

# 4️⃣ Modeling Region Properly

Locale codes alone are insufficient.

Example:

-   de-DE includes Hamburg (no Grüß Gott)
    
-   Bavaria uses Grüß Gott
    
-   Austria uses Grüß Gott
    

So we need either:

### Option A: Regional Subtags

```
Plain text

de-DE-BY  
de-AT
```

### Option B: Cultural Region Axis

```
Plain text

culturalRegion:  
  - austria  
  - bavaria  
  - north\_germany  
  - switzerland\_german
```

I strongly recommend the second.

Because:

-   Bavarian is cultural, not ISO-coded.
    
-   Swiss German greeting patterns differ.

```
      "type": "cultural\_time\_bound",  
      "timeWindow": { "start": "11:30", "end": "14:00" },  
      "template": "Mahlzeit"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

You: 

Since we are in fact developing a library for localization and internationalization, and especially for something which is not covered by the Unicode common locale data repository, which deals with localization of value formatting, date formatting, and so on, one domain which is lacking in my view in the CLDR is exactly what we are trying to do here with greetings. ChatGPT: 

This is exactly the right strategic question.

If Salve is positioned as:

> “The missing cultural salutation layer in i18n”

then long-term compatibility with CLDR is not optional — it is existential.

Let’s approach this carefully and concretely.

---

# 1️⃣ What CLDR Actually Is (Structurally)

CLDR is:

-   Locale-based
    
-   XML-based (LDML format)
    
-   Hierarchical
    
-   Inheritance-driven
    
-   Data-only (no algorithms)
    
-   Organized by:
    
    -   language
        
    -   script
        
    -   territory
        
    -   variants
        
-   Designed for deterministic fallback
    

Very important:

CLDR does **not** encode behavior.  
It encodes structured locale data.

So if Salve wants to be CLDR-compatible,  
it must:

-   Separate data from logic
    
-   Use locale identifiers properly
    
-   Support inheritance
    
-   Avoid custom identity systems
    

---

# 2️⃣ What CLDR Already Models

CLDR already handles:

-   Date formats
    
-   Calendar systems
    
-   Plurals
    
-   Day periods
    
-   Territory containment
    
-   Language display names
    
-   Time zone names
    

It does *not* model:

-   Cultural greetings
    
-   Name days
    
-   Baseline salutation norms
    
-   Call-response pairs
    

So Salve fills a real gap.

---

# 3️⃣ What We Should Do To Prepare

## A) Use BCP 47 Locale Identifiers Strictly

Do not invent your own region tags.

Use:

```
de-AT  
de-DE  
de-DE-u-rg-byzzzz  
en-US  
en-US-u-rg-txzzzz
```

For cultural regions like Bavaria or Texas:

Use Unicode locale extensions:

```
de-DE-u-rg-deby  
en-US-u-rg-ustx
```

CLDR already supports region override via `rg` extension.

This is critical.

Do not invent:

```
culturalRegion: bavaria
```

Instead:

Respect BCP 47.

---

## B) Follow CLDR Inheritance Model

CLDR inheritance works like this:

```
de-AT  
  inherits from de  
    inherits from root
```

So Salve data should:

-   Allow locale inheritance
    
-   Avoid duplicating data
    
-   Store baseline greetings at language level if shared
    

Example:

```
de.xml  
  <baselineGreetings>  
    <greeting type="informal">Hallo</greeting>  
  </baselineGreetings>  
  
de\_AT.xml  
  <baselineGreetings>  
    <greeting type="cultural\_all\_day">Grüß Gott</greeting>  
  </baselineGreetings>
```

This matches CLDR design philosophy.

---

## C) Separate Data from Resolution Logic

CLDR only contains data.

So Salve should:

-   Store greeting definitions in data packs
    
-   Keep priority resolution in engine
    
-   Avoid encoding priority into data files directly
    

This makes it more CLDR-aligned.

---

## D) Adopt LDML-Compatible Structure

CLDR uses LDML XML schema.

You don’t need to use XML now,  
but structure your JSON so that:

-   It could be converted to LDML later
    
-   It uses nested hierarchical grouping
    

For example:

```
JSON

{  
  "identity": {  
    "language": "de",  
    "territory": "AT"  
  },  
  "greetings": {  
    "baseline": {  
      "cultural\_all\_day": {  
        "neutral": "Grüß Gott"  
      },  
      "informal": {  
        "friend": "Servus"  
      }  
    },  
    "timeBound": {  
      "midday": {  
        "start": "11:30",  
        "end": "14:00",  
        "text": "Mahlzeit"  
      }  
    }  
  }  
}
```

This mirrors LDML structure.

---

# 4️⃣ What NOT To Do

Avoid:

-   Custom saint ID namespaces incompatible with CLDR naming
    
-   Arbitrary region naming
    
-   Hardcoding political assumptions
    
-   Embedding algorithmic flags inside data
    

---

# 5️⃣ Name Day Data Compatibility

CLDR already supports:

-   Calendars (Gregorian, Julian, Islamic, etc.)
    
-   Day period rules
    

So your name-day data should:

-   Reference standard calendar identifiers
    
-   Use ISO date formats
    
-   Avoid custom calendar types
    

For example:

```
JSON

{  
  "calendar": "gregorian",  
  "date": "01-07"  
}
```

Not:

```
"julianOffset": 13
```

Better:

Reference a Julian calendar identifier,  
let engine handle conversion.

---

# 6️⃣ Call-and-Response Modeling

CLDR uses attributes and elements.

So design greetings as:

```
JSON

{  
  "greeting": {  
    "type": "religious",  
    "phase": "initiator",  
    "text": "Χριστός Ανέστη",  
    "response": "Αληθώς Ανέστη"  
  }  
}
```

This could map cleanly to LDML:

```
XML

<greeting type\="religious" phase\="initiator"\>  
  <text>Χριστός Ανέστη</text>  
  <response>Αληθώς Ανέστη</response>  
</greeting>
```

---

# 7️⃣ Big Strategic Recommendation

If Salve is to be future-CLDR-compatible:

You should:

-   Mirror LDML conceptual structure
    
-   Use BCP 47 strictly
    
-   Adopt CLDR fallback inheritance logic
    
-   Avoid inventing your own region codes
    
-   Separate data packs per locale
    

---

# 8️⃣ Where Salve Adds Value

CLDR currently lacks:

-   Cultural baseline greetings
    
-   Name day data
    
-   Religious feast greeting formulas
    
-   Relationship-conditioned salutation variants
    
-   Call-response pairs
    

If Salve aligns structurally,  
Unicode could theoretically adopt:

```
<greetings>  
  ...  
</greetings>
```

as a new LDML section.

That is plausible.

---

# 9️⃣ The Most Important Decision

You must decide:

Is Salve:

A) A runtime engine with data packs

or

B) A data specification that could become a CLDR extension

If B,  
then you must treat your JSON schema as if it were an LDML draft.

---

# 🔟 Final Recommendation

Yes — structure your data in a CLDR-like hierarchical, locale-inheritance model.

Do not:

-   Flatten everything
    
-   Invent arbitrary axes
    
-   Encode algorithm in data
    

If you do this correctly,  
Salve becomes:

> The cultural salutation extension to CLDR.

And that is a very strong positioning.
