-   Draft a CLDR-compatible greeting data model
    
-   Show how Salve JSON would translate to LDML XML
    
-   Or propose a hypothetical `<greetings>` LDML extension section
    

This is now long-term architectural strategy.

You: 

I'm very pleased that we can implement CLDR compatibility already, so especially that we can have our data packs in JSON, which would nicely translate to LDNL XML without having to do that already. As for the options we have, well, Salve is indeed a runtime engine with data packs, because as you say, we have to strictly separate logic from data. So Salve will be both, it will be data specification with the ambition to become a CLDR extension, so the data should be stored in JSON schemas compatible with an LDNL draft, but on top of that, Salve, of course, also is a runtime implementing the fallback logic and so on. So I think we should conceive the entire architecture of Salve in that fashion. So what I would like you to do next is indeed draft the CLDR compatible greeting data model, and then model also the logic per se, so the runtime strictly separated from the data.

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **CLDR-aligned greeting data model draft** (JSON-first, LDML-friendly) and a **strictly separated runtime model** (logic-only). I’m keeping this minimal/slick, written-text only, but with the extra context axes you approved: **relationship/status**, **setting**, and **call–response**.

# Part A — CLDR-compatible greeting data model (JSON packs)

## A.1 Design principles

1.  **BCP 47 locale IDs** are the primary key (`de`, `de-AT`, `de-DE-u-rg-atzzzz`, etc.).
    
2.  **Inheritance is externalized** (runtime handles fallback), but the pack MAY declare `extends` for convenience.
    
3.  **Data is declarative only**: phrases, constraints, and metadata; **no algorithmic branching** beyond simple predicates that can map to LDML attributes.
    
4.  **LDML-friendly**: everything can map to `<greetings>` elements with attributes like `type`, `context`, `formality`, `setting`, `role`.
    

## A.2 Core concepts

### 1) Greeting Items

A “greeting” is a localized text with optional response, constrained by a small set of context attributes.

Key attributes (all optional unless stated):

-   `type`: semantic class (baseline, time, civil, religious, personal, closing, emailOpening, emailClosing, etc.)
    
-   `formality`: `formal|informal|neutral`
    
-   `relationship`: `stranger|acquaintance|friend|family|superior|subordinate`
    
-   `setting`: `chat|email|ui|public` (keep minimal; you can add later)
    
-   `phase`: `open|close`
    
-   `role`: `initiator|responder`
    
-   `dayPeriod`: `morning|afternoon|evening|night|midday` (aligns with CLDR dayPeriods)
    
-   `calendarRef`: reference to a calendar event ID (civil/religious/personal), not computed here
    
-   `responseTo`: links to another greeting item id (call–response) OR inline `expectedResponse`
    

### 2) Calendar Event References

Greeting packs should NOT encode full holiday logic (CLDR has calendars; your other packs may provide events).  
So greetings link to events by stable IDs:

-   `eventRef`: `"islam.eid_al_fitr"`, `"orthodox.easter_greeting_window"`, `"civil.new_year_window"`, `"personal.nameday"` etc.
    

The event computation belongs to separate “event packs” (which can be fed by `hellenic-holidays`, etc.). Greetings remain text + constraints.

### 3) Variables (Templates)

Use a **small, stable** variable set:

-   `{name}` (already vocativized if language wants it; vocative logic is runtime/plugin-level)
    
-   `{group}` (optional)
    
-   `{title}` (optional)
    
-   `{eventName}` (optional)
    
-   `{date}` (optional)
    

Avoid arbitrary templating; keep it LDML-translatable.

---

## A.3 JSON Pack Schema (draft)

A single locale pack file:

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schemas/greetings-pack.schema.json",  
  "identity": {  
    "language": "de",  
    "territory": "AT"  
  },  
  "locale": "de-AT",  
  "extends": \["de"\],  
  "metadata": {  
    "version": "1.0.0",  
    "source": \["curated"\],  
    "license": "CC-BY-4.0"  
  },  
  "greetings": \[  
    {  
      "id": "baseline.gruess\_gott",  
      "type": "baseline",  
      "formality": "neutral",  
      "phase": "open",  
      "setting": \["ui", "chat"\],  
      "text": "Grüß Gott"  
    },  
    {  
      "id": "baseline.servus.open",  
      "type": "baseline",  
      "formality": "informal",  
      "relationship": \["friend", "acquaintance"\],  
      "phase": "open",  
      "setting": \["ui", "chat"\],  
      "text": "Servus"  
    },  
    {  
      "id": "baseline.servus.close",  
      "type": "baseline",  
      "formality": "informal",  
      "relationship": \["friend", "acquaintance"\],  
      "phase": "close",  
      "setting": \["ui", "chat"\],  
      "text": "Servus"  
    },  
    {  
      "id": "time.mahlzeit",  
      "type": "time",  
      "dayPeriod": "midday",  
      "phase": "open",  
      "setting": \["ui", "chat"\],  
      "text": "Mahlzeit"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

### Notes

-   Arrays mean “allowed values”; omitted means “unspecified”.
    
-   This maps cleanly to LDML: `<greeting id="..." type="baseline" formality="neutral" ...>…</greeting>`.
    

---

## A.4 Call–response in data (draft)

Example: Greek Orthodox Easter pair.

```
JSON

{  
  "locale": "el-GR",  
  "extends": \["el"\],  
  "greetings": \[  
    {  
      "id": "religious.christos\_anesti.initiator",  
      "type": "religious",  
      "eventRef": "orthodox.easter\_window",  
      "role": "initiator",  
      "phase": "open",  
      "text": "Χριστός ἀνέστη!",  
      "expectedResponse": "Ἀληθῶς ἀνέστη!"  
    },  
    {  
      "id": "religious.alithos\_anesti.responder",  
      "type": "religious",  
      "eventRef": "orthodox.easter\_window",  
      "role": "responder",  
      "phase": "open",  
      "text": "Ἀληθῶς ἀνέστη!"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

Same for:

-   `as-salamu alaykum` → `wa alaykum as-salam`
    
-   (and your other pairs)
    

This stays text-only and LDML-friendly.

---

## A.5 How this aligns with CLDR

CLDR already models:

-   locale inheritance
    
-   day periods
    
-   calendars
    
-   formality distinctions (in various parts; not uniformly)
    

Salve adds a `<greetings>` domain that is:

-   locale-keyed
    
-   inheritable
    
-   declarative
    

If someday CLDR adopts it, your JSON can be mechanically converted into LDML.

---

# Part B — Runtime model (logic only, strictly separated)

Runtime responsibilities:

1.  **Load** greeting packs (and event packs) according to locale + configured traditions.
    
2.  **Resolve applicable greetings** given context.
    
3.  **Rank** them according to your **maximal-specificity doctrine**.
    
4.  **Apply fallback** using CLDR-like locale inheritance.
    
5.  **Apply suppression policies** (if developer opts out of religious/cultural).
    
6.  **Render template** with variables and optional language-specific morphology (vocatives).
    

## B.1 Runtime input context

Minimal context object:

-   `locale`: BCP 47 (primary)
    
-   `fallbackLocales[]`: computed
    
-   `now`: date-time
    
-   `phase`: open|close
    
-   `role`: initiator|responder
    
-   `formality`: formal|informal|neutral
    
-   `relationship`: one of the 6 values
    
-   `setting`: ui|chat|email|public (minimal set)
    
-   `person`:
    
    -   `names[]`
        
    -   optional `birthday`
        
    -   optional `namedayKey` (resolved during onboarding via your “elegant distribution approach”)
        
-   `traditions[]`: e.g. `["islam", "orthodox", "catholic"]` (used to select event packs)
    

## B.2 Event resolution interface (pluggable)

Greeting packs refer to `eventRef`. Runtime asks event providers:

-   “Which events are active now?”
    
-   “Which events are upcoming (for timeline)?”
    
-   “What is the user’s nameday today?”
    

This stays modular and supports your distribution model.

## B.3 Applicability filter

A greeting item is applicable if all specified constraints match:

-   if it has `phase`, must match
    
-   if it has `relationship[]`, must contain current
    
-   if it has `setting[]`, must contain current
    
-   if it has `dayPeriod`, must match computed dayPeriod
    
-   if it has `eventRef`, that event must be active now
    
-   if it has `role`, must match
    
-   etc.
    

No pack contains branching logic; runtime does.

## B.4 Specificity-first ranking

This is the key policy you’ve decided:

> prefer the most specific culturally/time/tradition-aware greeting by default

Implement as a deterministic scoring function:

**Score components** (example; tuneable, but fixed constants):

-   +1000 if `eventRef` active AND event kind = personal (birthday/nameday)
    
-   +900 if `eventRef` active AND event kind = religious
    
-   +800 if `eventRef` active AND event kind = civil
    
-   +700 if type = cultural baseline (tradition baseline like Salam / Grüß Gott)
    
-   +600 if dayPeriod-bound cultural (Mahlzeit)
    
-   +500 if relationship-specific baseline
    
-   +200 if plain time-of-day
    
-   +0 fallback hello
    

Then add “match specificity bonuses”:

-   +30 if greeting constrains `relationship`
    
-   +20 if greeting constrains `setting`
    
-   +10 if greeting constrains `formality`
    
-   +5 if greeting constrains `role`
    

Tie-breakers:

1.  higher score
    
2.  shorter fallback distance (exact locale > parent locale > root)
    
3.  stable id lexical order (determinism)
    

This gives “maximal richness” without weird randomness.

## B.5 Locale fallback (CLDR-style)

Runtime computes fallback chain:

Example:

-   `de-AT` → `de` → `root`
    

And applies it in **pack selection** and **lexicon fallback**.

## B.6 Memory / repetition suppression

This is runtime-only state:

-   “do not repeat Χρόνια Πολλά for new year more than once per window”
    
-   “do not repeat birthday greeting after first visit that day”
    

This can stay exactly as you already modeled:

-   keyed by `(greetingId | eventRef, year/window)`
    
-   localStorage persistence for demo / client
    

No CLDR involvement.

---

# Part C — What I recommend you lock down next

To make this “architecture conceived in CLDR fashion” (your goal), you should freeze:

1.  **The greeting item attribute set** (so pack writers don’t invent fields)
    
2.  **The locale fallback semantics**
    
3.  **The eventRef mechanism** (how greetings reference events without embedding event logic)
    
4.  **The specificity scoring rubric** (even if the numeric weights are adjustable)
    

If you want, in the next step I can:

-   write an RFC-style section (“Greeting Data Model”) with MUST/SHOULD language,
    
-   and a parallel section (“Runtime Resolution Algorithm”) with a normative scoring and tie-break procedure,
    
-   and show how one JSON pack would map to a plausible `<greetings>` LDML draft.
    

No code, just crisp spec text.

You: 

There is a very important aspect of domain or variable which we have not considered and which is critically important for proper formal greeting. So we now have pretty much, I think, covered many ways of informal greeting in various traditions, locales, languages, countries, and so on. And we already make the distinction between formal and informal greeting, but for formal greeting, we need something more, which is the way how to address people with a certain profession or degree. So formally, if you don't know, you would say Mr. or Mrs. or Miss in English, but if it's a judge or a justice, then you would address them with good day, your honor. If it is an agent, so a police officer in London, you would say good morning, constable. In Germany, especially, people with an academic degree, so PhDs, would invariably be addressed also in informal contexts like at the bakery, formally with guten Morgen, Herr Doktor. So we need to expand the scope of the SALVE package to also address these addresses and these cross-cultural and cross-languages, taking into account the various traditions. So as I said, in Germany, using the academic degree in addressing people like Herr Professor and Herr Doktor is important.

ChatGPT: 

Excellent.

This is not feature creep. This is essential for *formal salutation correctness*.

You have just identified a missing axis:

> **Title / Honorific / Address Form**

Formality (du/Sie) is insufficient.

We need structured modeling of **how a person is addressed** in formal contexts.

This is absolutely within Salve’s scope because it affects **written greeting text**, not gestures or spoken tone.

---

# 1️⃣ New Domain: Address Protocol Layer

Salve must now distinguish between:

-   Greeting phrase (“Guten Morgen”)
    
-   Address form (“Herr Doktor”)
    
-   Combined salutation (“Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor”)
    

These are separable components.

---

# 2️⃣ Why This Is Critical

Across cultures:

### English

-   Mr. Smith
    
-   Dr. Smith
    
-   Professor Smith
    
-   Your Honor
    
-   Officer
    
-   Sir / Madam
    

### German

-   Herr Müller
    
-   Frau Müller
    
-   Herr Doktor Müller
    
-   Herr Professor Dr. Müller
    
-   Herr Präsident
    
-   Frau Vorsitzende
    

### French

-   Monsieur
    
-   Madame
    
-   Monsieur le Président
    
-   Maître (lawyer)
    
-   Docteur
    

### Arabic

-   السيد
    
-   الدكتور
    
-   الأستاذ
    
-   فضيلة الشيخ
    

### Russian

-   Господин
    
-   Товарищ (historical / contextual)
    
-   Профессор
    

This is not just lexical.  
It is culturally regulated.

In Germany especially, academic degrees are part of formal identity.

---

# 3️⃣ Scope Clarification

Salve SHALL:

-   Generate correct written salutation strings
    
-   Including honorifics and titles
    
-   According to locale norms
    

Salve SHALL NOT:

-   Maintain full occupational taxonomy
    
-   Model institutional protocols in full detail
    
-   Replace legal style guides
    

We keep it minimal but correct.

Plain text

```

# 5️⃣ Separation of Concerns

We must separate:

1.  Greeting phrase resolution (already modeled)
    
2.  Address construction logic (new layer)
    

This is crucial for clean architecture.

Greeting packs should not hardcode:

"Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor Müller"

Instead:

Greeting pack provides:

"Guten Morgen"

Address engine produces:

"Herr Doktor Müller"

Runtime concatenates.

---

# 6️⃣ Address Construction Rules Must Be Locale-Specific

Each locale pack may define:

```
Plain text

addressRules:  
  - order of elements  
  - whether titles precede honorific  
  - gender agreement  
  - comma placement  
  - capitalization rules
```

Example:

## 🇩🇪 German

Pattern:

```
\[Greeting\], \[Herr/Frau\] \[AcademicTitle(s)\] \[LastName\]
```

Examples:

-   Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor Müller.
    
-   Guten Tag, Frau Professor Weber.
    

Important:  
In Germany:

-   "Dr." often spoken as “Doktor”
    
-   Academic titles may stack: “Professor Dr.”
    

---

## 🇬🇧 English

Pattern:

```
\[Greeting\], \[Title\] \[LastName\]
```

Examples:

-   Good morning, Dr. Smith.
    
-   Good afternoon, Your Honor.
    

No stacking convention like Germany.

---

## 🇫🇷 French

Pattern:

```
\[Greeting\], \[Monsieur/Madame\] \[Title\] \[LastName\]
```

Example:

-   Bonjour, Monsieur le Président.
    

---

# 7️⃣ Title Hierarchy Problem

We must not attempt to store every possible profession.

Instead:

Define small abstract categories:

```
Plain text

professionalRole:  
  judge  
  police  
  clergy  
  academic  
  military  
  generic
```

Locale packs map these to localized address tokens.

Example:

German:

```
judge → Herr Vorsitzender  
academic → Herr Professor  
police → Herr Wachtmeister
```

English:

```
judge → Your Honor  
police → Officer  
academic → Professor
```

---

# 8️⃣ Interaction with Formality

Formal greeting:

-   Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor Müller.
    

Informal greeting:

-   Guten Morgen, Herr Müller.
    
-   Guten Morgen, Hans.
    

So formality influences:

-   Use of first name vs last name
    
-   Use of title vs omission
    

Relationship axis + formality axis determine which address rule to apply.

---

# 9️⃣ Runtime Architecture Adjustment

The runtime pipeline becomes:

1.  Resolve greeting phrase
    
2.  Resolve address construction (if applicable)
    
3.  Apply template assembly:
    

```
Plain text

"{greeting}, {address}"
```

Address may be empty (for informal contexts).

---

# 10️⃣ Avoiding Overreach

We must not:

-   Store academic degrees as free-text
    
-   Attempt to normalize global educational systems
    
-   Model legal honorific complexities fully
    

Instead:

Treat academic titles as string tokens provided by developer.

Salve only decides placement and usage.

---

# 11️⃣ CLDR Compatibility

CLDR already handles:

-   Person name formatting
    
-   Name order
    
-   Gender
    
-   Titles (limited)
    

We should align with:

-   CLDR PersonName patterns (very important)
    
-   Use similar structure for name formatting
    

This keeps long-term compatibility.

Locale pack may include:

JSON

{

    "informal": "{firstName}"  
  },  
  "honorifics": {  
    "male": "Herr",  
    "female": "Frau"  
  },  
  "roleTitles": {  
    "judge": {  
      "formal": "Vorsitzender"  
    },  
    "academic": {  
      "formal": "Professor"  
    }  
  }  
}
```

Greeting packs remain independent.

# 13️⃣ Final Architecture Layering

Salve now has three clean layers:

### Layer 1: Event + Baseline Greeting Resolution

(time, culture, tradition)

### Layer 2: Relationship + Formality Resolution

(friend vs stranger)

### Layer 3: Address Construction

(title, role, academic degree)

All text-based.

All deterministic.

Still within scope.

# 1️⃣ Architectural Separation (Critical)

We must strictly separate:

### A) Greeting Resolution

Determines:

-   “Guten Morgen”
    
-   “Grüß Gott”
    
-   “Χριστός Ανέστη”
    
-   “Mahlzeit”
    

Based on:

-   time
    
-   locale
    
-   tradition
    
-   relationship
    
-   event
    
-   setting
    
-   role
    

---

### B) Address Construction

Determines:

-   “Herr Müller”
    
-   “Frau Professor Dr. Weber”
    
-   “Your Honor”
    
-   “Officer”
    
-   “Monsieur le Président”
    

Based on:

-   gender
    
-   lastName
    
-   firstName
    
-   academicTitles
    
-   professionalRole
    
-   locale
    
-   relationship
    
-   formality
    

---

### C) Assembly Layer

Determines formatting:

Examples:

-   `{greeting}, {address}`
    
-   `{greeting} {address}`
    
-   `{address},`
    
-   `{greeting}.`
    

This is locale-dependent punctuation logic.

# 2️⃣ Runtime Output Modes (Configurable)

Add a runtime option:

```
Plain text

outputMode:  
  - greeting  
  - address  
  - salutation
```

Meaning:

-   `greeting` → “Guten Morgen”
    
-   `address` → “Herr Doktor Müller”
    
-   `salutation` → “Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor Müller”
    

Default: `salutation` (for maximal specificity)

---

# 3️⃣ CLDR Alignment

CLDR already has:

-   PersonName formatting rules
    
-   Title handling
    
-   Name order patterns
    

Salve should:

-   Not reinvent person-name formatting
    
-   Delegate name formatting to CLDR-compatible patterns
    
-   Only define salutation assembly patterns
    

So the salutation pack must define:

```
JSON

{  
  "salutationPatterns": {  
    "default": "{greeting}, {address}",  
    "emailOpening": "{address},",  
    "chat": "{greeting} {address}"  
  }  
}
```

This mirrors LDML-style pattern definitions.

---

# 4️⃣ Address Model (Minimal and Universal)

To avoid explosion, we define a compact addressProfile:

-   `firstName`
    
-   `lastName`
    
-   `gender`
    
-   `titles[]`
    
-   `professionalRole`
    
-   `nobilityParticle`
    
-   `customHonorific` (optional override)
    

We do NOT:

-   Normalize degrees globally
    
-   Encode entire professional taxonomy
    

The developer supplies structured values.

Salve applies locale rules.

---

# 5️⃣ Locale-Specific Address Rules

Locale packs define:

-   Honorific tokens
    
-   Title stacking order
    
-   Whether titles are spoken or abbreviated
    
-   Gender agreement rules
    
-   Comma usage rules
    

Example (Germany conceptual logic):

Formal:

```
Herr + titles + lastName
```

Informal:

```
firstName
```

English:

Formal:

```
Title + lastName
```

Informal:

```
firstName
```

French:

Formal:

```
Monsieur/Madame + roleTitle + lastName
```

---

# 6️⃣ Relationship Interaction

Relationship influences:

-   Use of first name vs last name
    
-   Inclusion of titles
    
-   Use of honorific at all
    

Examples:

Germany:

-   friend → “Hans”
    
-   acquaintance → “Herr Müller”
    
-   superior → “Herr Professor Müller”
    

English:

-   friend → “John”
    
-   judge → “Your Honor”
    

---

# 7️⃣ Salve Resolution Pipeline (Updated)

Final pipeline:

1.  Resolve active events.
    
2.  Resolve greeting phrase candidates.
    
3.  Rank by specificity.
    
4.  Select greeting.
    
5.  Construct address string (if needed).
    
6.  Apply salutation pattern.
    
7.  Apply repetition suppression rules.
    
8.  Return structured output:
    

```
Plain text

{  
  greeting: "...",  
  address: "...",  
  salutation: "...",  
  expectedResponse?: "...",  
  metadata: { id, locale, eventRef }  
}
```

This is clean.

---

# 8️⃣ Why This Still Avoids Feature Creep

We are still:

-   Text-only
    
-   Deterministic
    
-   Locale-based
    
-   Relationship-aware
    
-   CLDR-compatible
    

We are not:

-   Modeling speech
    
-   Modeling gestures
    
-   Modeling legal protocols exhaustively
    
-   Modeling etiquette beyond minimal necessary
    

---

# 9️⃣ Important Strategic Note

By adding structured address handling:

Salve becomes usable for:

-   UI welcome screens
    
-   Email generators
    
-   CRM systems
    
-   Chat systems
    
-   Legal automation
    
-   Public announcements
    

This dramatically increases adoption potential.

---

# 10️⃣ What We Should Lock Next

To prevent future drift, we should now:

1.  Freeze the **address schema**
    
2.  Freeze the **greeting schema**
    
3.  Freeze the **salutation assembly pattern schema**
    
4.  Define a deterministic resolution algorithm formally
    

Once frozen, implementation can proceed safely.

---

-   A formal RFC-style section defining “Salutation Resolution Model”
    
-   Or a consolidated architectural diagram in structured prose
    
-   Or a compact canonical JSON schema for the combined greeting + address system?

Excellent — this is now a serious integration question.

We’ll assume:

-   **React (TypeScript) app**
    
-   Fully client-side Salve runtime
    
-   Locale scope limited to:
    
    -   **nl-NL** (Calvinist, Lutheran, Muslim, secular)
        
    -   **nl-BE** (Catholic, socialist, secular)
        
    -   **el-GR** (Greek Orthodox, incl. namedays)
        
-   All greeting/address data bundled locally
    
-   **Nameday dataset served via REST API** (external, large)
    

Below is a precise integration guide.

---

# 1\. Package Architecture for This Use Case

You should publish Salve as modular NPM packages:

```
@salve/core  
@salve/runtime  
@salve/locale-nl  
@salve/locale-el  
@salve/plugin-nameday-remote
```

For this React app, the developer would install:

```
Bash

npm install @salve/runtime @salve/locale-nl @salve/locale-el @salve/plugin-nameday-remote
```

---

# 2\. Runtime + Data Separation

Salve should expose:

```
TypeScript

import { Salve } from "@salve/runtime"  
import nlPack from "@salve/locale-nl"  
import elPack from "@salve/locale-el"  
import { createRemoteNamedayPlugin } from "@salve/plugin-nameday-remote"
```

---

# 3\. Nameday Remote API Contract

You want namedays served from your own backend.

Define a simple REST contract:

### Endpoint

```
GET /api/namedays?locale=el-GR&month=1&day=7
```

### Response

```
JSON

{  
  "date": "01-07",  
  "names": \[  
    {  
      "canonical": "Ιωάννης",  
      "variants": \["Γιάννης", "Γιάννης", "Ioannis", "Yannis"\],  
      "priority": 1  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

Important:

-   The server should normalize name variants.
    
-   Client should pass normalized user names.
    
-   Server should not send entire dataset — only the requested day.
    

---

# 4\. React App Initialization

In the React app:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  packs: \[  
    nlPack,  
    elPack  
  \],  
  plugins: \[  
    createRemoteNamedayPlugin({  
      endpoint: "/api/namedays",  
      fetchImpl: fetch  
    })  
  \]  
})
```

This ensures:

-   Dutch + Greek greeting logic built-in
    
-   Nameday data resolved dynamically
    
-   Runtime logic stays local
    

---

# 5\. Locale Configuration Strategy

Since this app only supports Dutch and Greek:

You should restrict locale resolution explicitly:

```
TypeScript

salve.setSupportedLocales(\["nl-NL", "nl-BE", "el-GR"\])
```

This prevents fallback to other packs.

---

# 6\. Affiliation Configuration (Religious / Secular)

In React, user profile might look like:

```
TypeScript

const userProfile \= {  
  locale: "nl-BE",  
  greetingLanguage: "nl-BE",  
  affiliations: \["catholic"\],  
  relationship: "stranger",  
  formality: "formal",  
  gender: "female",  
  surname: "Peeters",  
  titleRole: "dr"  
}
```

For Greek:

```
TypeScript

const userProfile \= {  
  locale: "el-GR",  
  greetingLanguage: "el-GR",  
  affiliations: \["orthodox"\],  
  namedaysEnabled: true,  
  givenNames: \["Γιάννης"\]  
}
```

Example hook:

TypeScript

  date: new Date(),  
  mode: "opening",  
  ...userProfile  
})
```

Return structure:

```
TypeScript

{  
  text: "Καλημέρα, κύριε Ιωάννη",  
  greetingText: "Καλημέρα",  
  addressText: "κύριε Ιωάννη",  
  trace: {...}  
}
```

Then render:

```
TypeScript

<h1>{greeting.text}</h1>
```

---

# 8\. Nameday Plugin (Client-Side Logic)

The remote nameday plugin should:

1.  Detect if:
    
    -   locale = el-GR
        
    -   namedaysEnabled = true
        
    -   givenNames present
        
2.  Extract month/day
    
3.  Call REST endpoint
    
4.  Cache response per day (in memory or localStorage)
    
5.  Inject nameday event candidate into Salve’s event resolver
    

Example plugin skeleton:

```
TypeScript

export function createRemoteNamedayPlugin(config: {  
  endpoint: string  
  fetchImpl: typeof fetch  
}) {  
  return {  
    async resolve(context, next) {  
      if (!context.namedaysEnabled) {  
        return next()  
      }  
  
      const { month, day } \= extractDate(context.date)  
  
      const res \= await config.fetchImpl(  
        \`${config.endpoint}?locale=${context.locale}&month=${month}&day=${day}\`  
      )  
  
      const data \= await res.json()  
  
      if (matchesUserName(data.names, context.givenNames)) {  
        context.injectEvent({  
          id: "nameday",  
          priority: 95  
        })  
      }  
  
      return next()  
    }  
  }  
}
```

---

# 9\. Performance Considerations

Because this is client-side:

### Cache nameday responses:

```
TypeScript

const cacheKey \= \`nameday:${locale}:${month}\-${day}\`
```

Cache in:

-   in-memory Map
    
-   or localStorage with 24h expiry
    

Avoid repeated API calls during session.

---

# 10\. Dutch Religious Variants

For Dutch, you need:

### nl-NL packs

-   secular
    
-   calvinist
    
-   lutheran
    
-   muslim
    

### nl-BE packs

-   catholic
    
-   socialist
    
-   secular
    

These are separate data packs but share base grammar.

Example:

```
@salve/locale-nl-core  
@salve/locale-nl-nl  
@salve/locale-nl-be
```

Runtime merges based on affiliation + region.

---

# 11\. What Should Be Bundled vs Remote?

### Bundle locally:

-   Greeting lexicon
    
-   Title rules
    
-   Address grammar
    
-   Religious holiday calendars (except namedays)
    
-   Temporal rules
    
-   Regional greetings (e.g., "Mahlzeit", "Servus")
    

### Remote:

-   Large Greek nameday dataset (thousands of names)
    
-   Optional saint metadata (if needed)
    

---

# 12\. Security & API Strategy

Nameday API:

-   Should be GET-only
    
-   No PII stored
    
-   Stateless
    
-   Can be CDN cached
    

Example CDN-friendly URL:

```
/api/namedays/el-GR/01-07.json
```

Even simpler.

---

# 13\. Tree-Shaking & Bundle Size

To minimize React bundle:

-   Ensure packs export JSON-only data
    
-   Avoid importing unused locales
    
-   Use ES modules
    

Example:

```
TypeScript

import nlCore from "@salve/locale-nl/core"
```

not full global bundle.

### Minimal Integration Steps

1.  Install packages
    
2.  Configure Salve runtime
    
3.  Provide user profile context
    
4.  Add remote nameday plugin
    
5.  Render `salve.resolve()` result

You: 

Suppose the developer is sloppy, and their app doesn't provide all the required user profile config data (eg. no surname, no gender, no religious or cultural tradition affiliations): we then need sensibly chosen defaults so we can have off-the-shelves, built-in graceful degradation and fall-back. What would you propse these comprehensive defaults be?

ChatGPT: 

Excellent — this is where Salve becomes robust rather than clever.

If Salve is to be production-grade and CLDR-compatible in spirit, it must:

-   **Never crash**
    
-   **Never produce culturally inappropriate output**
    
-   **Prefer safety over specificity**
    
-   **Prefer neutral over incorrect**
    
-   **Prefer greeting-only over mis-address**
    

Below is a comprehensive fallback strategy for Dutch and Greek, designed for a sloppy integration scenario.

---

# I. Design Principle: The “Politeness Safety Ladder”

When data is missing, Salve should climb this ladder:

1.  Full personalized salutation  
    → “Guten Morgen, Herr Doktor Müller.”
    
2.  Formal non-personal salutation  
    → “Guten Morgen.”
    
3.  Neutral generic greeting  
    → “Hello.”
    
4.  Time-aware generic greeting  
    → “Good morning.”
    
5.  Absolute fallback  
    → “Hello.”
    

Never guess:

-   gender
    
-   degree
    
-   religion
    
-   cultural tradition
    

---

# II. Missing Surname

### If:

-   `surname` missing
    
-   formal context requested
    
-   titleRole present
    

### Proposed behavior:

#### Dutch:

Instead of:

```
Goedemorgen, dokter.
```

(which sounds wrong)

Fallback to:

```
Goedemorgen.
```

#### Greek:

Instead of:

```
Καλημέρα, κύριε.
```

(which is odd unless spoken)

Fallback to:

```
Καλημέρα.
```

### Rule:

If no surname AND no reliable vocative form → do not attach address block.

---

# III. Missing Gender

Never infer from first name.

### Dutch default:

Use **non-gendered** forms.

Instead of:

```
Goedemorgen, meneer/mevrouw.
```

Prefer:

```
Goedemorgen.
```

Or:

```
Beste,
```

for email mode.

### Greek:

Avoid:

```
κύριε  
κυρία
```

Fallback:

```
Καλημέρα.
```

---

# IV. Missing Religious / Cultural Affiliation

This is critical.

### Default affiliation:

```
\["civil", "secular"\]
```

This avoids:

-   “Zalig Kerstfeest”
    
-   “Χριστός Ανέστη”
    
-   “Ramadan Mubarak”
    

Instead use:

-   time-of-day
    
-   civil holidays only
    

### Dutch defaults:

-   New Year → “Gelukkig nieuwjaar”
    
-   No confessional variants
    

### Greek default:

-   Use time-of-day greetings
    
-   Do NOT inject namedays unless explicitly enabled
    

---

# V. Missing Relationship

Default to:

```
relationship = "stranger"  
formality = "formal"
```

This is globally safest.

It prevents accidental informal address.

---

# VI. Missing Formality

If formality missing:

Default:

```
formal
```

Reason:

In real-world UI systems:

-   Formal tone is safer
    
-   Informal risks sounding disrespectful
    

---

# VII. Missing Title Role

Default:

```
titleRole = "none"
```

Never assume:

-   Dr
    
-   Mr
    
-   Professor
    

---

# VIII. Missing Given Name

If no name data:

Do not inject:

```
Hallo Jan  
Καλημέρα Γιάννη
```

Use greeting-only.

---

# IX. Missing Nameday Data

If:

-   namedaysEnabled true
    
-   remote API fails
    

Fallback:

-   skip nameday event
    
-   log trace warning
    
-   proceed with normal greeting
    

Never block greeting resolution.

---

# X. Comprehensive Default Profile Object

This is what Salve should internally merge if developer provides incomplete config:

```
TypeScript

const DEFAULT\_PROFILE \= {  
  locale: "en-GB",  
  greetingLanguage: undefined,      // derive from locale  
  affiliations: \["civil", "secular"\],  
  relationship: "stranger",  
  formality: "formal",  
  gender: "unknown",  
  surname: null,  
  givenNames: \[\],  
  titleRole: "none",  
  namedaysEnabled: false,  
  civilParticipation: true,  
  scriptPreference: "native",  
  mode: "opening",  
  setting: "ui",  
  outputMode: "salutation"  
}
```

Then merge:

```
TypeScript

const context \= deepMerge(DEFAULT\_PROFILE, developerProvidedProfile)
```

---

# XI. Dutch-Specific Safe Defaults

## nl-NL

Default affiliation: secular

Use:

-   “Goedemorgen”
    
-   “Goedemiddag”
    
-   “Goedenavond”
    

Avoid:

-   “Zalig Pasen”
    
-   “Gezegende Ramadan”
    

## nl-BE

Default: secular Belgian civil

Use:

-   “Goedemorgen”
    
-   “Fijne feestdagen” (neutral seasonal)
    
-   “Gelukkig nieuwjaar”
    

Avoid:

-   confessional Catholic variants
    

---

# XII. Greek-Specific Safe Defaults

Default:

-   secular civil Greek
    

Use:

-   Καλημέρα
    
-   Καλησπέρα
    
-   Χρόνια Πολλά (only if birthday explicit)
    

Avoid:

-   Χριστός Ανέστη
    
-   Χρόνια Πολλά for namedays
    
-   religious-specific Paschal greetings
    

Unless:

```
affiliations includes "orthodox"
```

---

# XIII. Email Mode Default

If setting = email and no surname:

Use:

Dutch:

```
Beste,
```

Greek:

```
Καλημέρα,
```

English fallback:

```
Hello,
```

Never:

```
Beste meneer,
```

if gender unknown.

---

# XIV. Degradation Matrix

| Missing | Behavior |
| --- | --- |
| surname | drop address block |
| gender | avoid honorific |
| title | ignore |
| affiliation | secular civil |
| nameday API | skip |
| given name | greeting-only |
| relationship | stranger |
| formality | formal |

---

# XV. Absolute Minimum Output Guarantee

No matter how incomplete config is, Salve must guarantee:

For Dutch:

```
Goedemorgen.
```

For Greek:

```
Καλημέρα.
```

For fallback:

```
Hello.
```

---

# XVI. Optional Developer Warning System

In development mode, Salve should log:

```
JSON

{  
  "warning": "No surname provided; address block suppressed",  
  "warning": "No affiliation provided; using secular civil defaults"  
}
```

Not throw errors.

---

# XVII. Philosophical Rule

Salve must prefer:

> Under-personalization over incorrect personalization.

It is better to sound neutral than wrong.

---

# XVIII. Final Recommended Default Policy

If developer provides nothing except:

```
TypeScript

salve.resolve({ locale: "el-GR" })
```

Then Salve should safely output:

```
Καλημέρα.
```

And for Dutch:

```
Goedemorgen.
```

And nothing more.

---

If you'd like, next we can:

-   Formalize this as a “Salve Default Resolution RFC”
    
-   Or implement a strict “Safe Mode” flag in the runtime
    
-   Or define validation severity levels (strict vs permissive runtime)

You: 

Would we have a single package published to npm or should we publish multiple packages to npm?

ChatGPT: 

Short answer: **multiple packages** — but presented as one coherent ecosystem.

If you publish a single monolithic package, you lose:

-   Tree-shaking
    
-   Locale scoping
    
-   Dataset modularity
    
-   Optional plugin architecture (like remote namedays)
    
-   Long-term CLDR-style extensibility
    

Salve is structurally closer to:

-   `date-fns` + locale packs
    
-   `i18next` + resource bundles
    
-   `Luxon` + zone data
    
-   `CLDR` + LDML fragments
    

It should not be a single blob.

---

# The Correct Strategy: Layered, Modular, Opinionated

You want **logical separation**, not fragmentation chaos.

Think in 5 layers.

---

# 1\. Core Architecture (Always Separate)

### `@salve/core`

Contains:

-   Type definitions
    
-   Schema definitions
    
-   Context model
    
-   Event model
    
-   Resolution algorithm
    
-   Address resolver interface
    
-   Plugin interface
    

No locale data.

Small. Stable. Tree-shakeable.

---

### `@salve/runtime`

Contains:

-   Implementation of resolution engine
    
-   Default fallback logic
    
-   Safe-mode defaults
    
-   Plugin orchestrator
    
-   Trace debugger
    

Depends on `@salve/core`.

Still no locale data.

---

# 2\. Locale Data Packs (Always Separate)

### `@salve/locale-nl`

### `@salve/locale-el`

Contain:

-   Greeting lexicon
    
-   Address grammar rules
    
-   Civil holiday rules
    
-   Religious tradition variants
    
-   Temporal rules
    

They are pure JSON/TS data exports.

No runtime code.

This allows:

-   Small bundles
    
-   Only shipping Dutch + Greek if needed
    
-   Future CLDR alignment
    

---

# 3\. Optional Dataset Plugins (Definitely Separate)

### `@salve/plugin-nameday-remote`

Contains:

-   Remote REST connector
    
-   Cache strategy
    
-   Injection logic
    

Must be separate because:

-   Not all apps need namedays
    
-   Some want local dataset
    
-   Some want remote
    
-   Some want none
    

---

# 4\. Convenience Meta Package (Optional)

You *may* also publish:

### `salve`

Which simply re-exports:

```
TypeScript

export \* from "@salve/runtime"  
export \* from "@salve/locale-nl"  
export \* from "@salve/locale-el"
```

This gives:

-   Easy onboarding for small projects
    
-   Full bundle for demos
    

But serious apps will use granular imports.

---

# 5\. Why Not a Single Package?

If you publish one:

-   Dutch-only React app ships Greek + Arabic + Chinese data
    
-   Nameday dataset bloats bundle
    
-   Harder versioning
    
-   Harder CLDR mapping
    
-   Harder institutional adoption
    

And it makes Salve look like a toy.

---

# 6\. Recommended NPM Structure

```
@salve/core  
@salve/runtime  
@salve/locale-nl  
@salve/locale-el  
@salve/plugin-nameday-remote  
@salve/plugin-nameday-local  
salve (meta bundle)
```

---

# 7\. For Your Specific Developer Scenario

They would install:

```
Bash

npm install @salve/runtime \\  
            @salve/locale-nl \\  
            @salve/locale-el \\  
            @salve/plugin-nameday-remote
```

They do not install:

-   Arabic
    
-   German
    
-   Chinese
    
-   Global pack
    

This keeps their React bundle small.

---

# 8\. Versioning Strategy

All packages should share a version number:

```
1.2.0
```

Monorepo.

Changesets or Nx.

This prevents:

-   runtime 1.2 incompatible with locale 1.1
    

---

# 9\. Long-Term: CLDR Integration Path

If Salve aims to align with CLDR:

You need:

-   Data packs structured per locale
    
-   Clear separation of grammar vs runtime
    
-   JSON schema definitions in core
    
-   Locale packs that could theoretically be converted to LDML
    

A monolith would kill that ambition.

---

# 10\. My Strong Recommendation

Publish multiple packages.

But design it so developers feel like:

> “I’m installing Salve.”

Not:

> “I’m assembling a Lego set of 17 parts.”

That means:

-   Good documentation
    
-   Clear starter example
    
-   A minimal integration snippet
    
-   Sensible defaults
    

---

# 11\. Final Architecture Philosophy

Salve is not:

-   A function.
    

It is:

-   A cultural protocol engine.
    

Cultural protocol engines must be modular.

---

If you want, next we can:

-   Design the exact monorepo structure
    
-   Define package boundaries in detail
    
-   Or draft the initial `package.json` layout for each package

You: 

There are some social contexts or subcultures which use specific salutations and greetings. So for example in German corporate student culture, so corporatiste studenten, they have this concept of Fachbandbruder and Kartellbruder and Bundesbruder. So people of the same club or fraternity would address each other with Bundesbruder. Members of clubs that are gathered under the same Kartellverband would call their address each other with Kartellbruder and corporatiste studenten would address each other with Fachbandbruder. This is only one social context or subculture with specific greetings and another is in political terms. People who share the same worldview, like social democrats, communists, socialists all over the world would address each other with kamerad, which can also be localized in Russian, for example, with tovarich. I think we should add a domain for that kind of subculture-specific greetings and conventions.

ChatGPT: 

Yes — and conceptually this is an **important extension of Salve’s domain model**. What you are describing is not simply *locale-based greetings* but **sociolectal or affiliation-based address conventions**. CLDR currently has no structured model for this either, so Salve can introduce a clean abstraction.

The key is to **treat these as optional cultural protocols layered above locale rules**, not as part of the base language greeting system.

Below is the architecture I would recommend.

---

# 1\. Introduce a New Domain: **Socio-Cultural Protocols**

Add a new dimension to the greeting context:

```
subculture
```

Example values:

```
student\_corporation  
socialist  
communist  
military  
religious\_order  
scouting  
academic  
trade\_union  
monastic
```

This sits alongside existing Salve variables:

```
locale  
language  
affiliations (religious/civil)  
relationship  
formality  
setting
```

So the context model becomes:

```
TypeScript

interface SalveContext {  
  locale: string  
  greetingLanguage: string  
  
  affiliations?: string\[\]        // religious/cultural  
  subcultures?: string\[\]         // NEW  
  
  relationship?: string  
  formality?: "formal" | "informal"  
  
  gender?: string  
  titleRole?: string  
}
```

---

# 2\. Resolution Priority

Socio-cultural greetings should **override normal greetings only if explicitly present**.

Priority stack:

```
1\. Institutional roles  
   (judge, professor, police)  
  
2\. Subculture greetings   ← NEW  
  
3\. Religious traditions  
  
4\. Civil holidays  
  
5\. Locale greetings
```

Example:

```
Bundesbruder Schmidt!
```

instead of

```
Guten Morgen, Herr Schmidt
```

---

# 3\. Model: **Protocol Packs**

Subculture systems should be distributed as **separate protocol packs**.

Example packages:

```
@salve/protocol-student-corporations  
@salve/protocol-socialist  
@salve/protocol-military  
@salve/protocol-scouting
```

This prevents the core runtime from embedding ideological or niche conventions.

---

# 4\. Example: German Student Corporations

Context:

```
subculture = student\_corporation
```

Additional variable:

```
corporationRelation
```

Values:

```
bundesbruder  
kartellbruder  
fachbandbruder
```

Protocol data example:

```
TypeScript

{  
  protocol: "student\_corporation",  
  locale: "de-DE",  
  
  addressForms: {  
    bundesbruder: {  
      template: "Bundesbruder {surname}"  
    },  
  
    kartellbruder: {  
      template: "Kartellbruder {surname}"  
    },  
  
    fachbandbruder: {  
      template: "Fachbandbruder {surname}"  
    }  
  }  
}
```

Usage result:

```
Bundesbruder Müller  
Kartellbruder Schmidt
```

---

# 5\. Example: Socialist / Communist Tradition

Context:

```
subculture = socialist
```

Greeting:

```
Kamerad
```

Localized forms:

| language | form |
| --- | --- |
| German | Kamerad |
| Russian | Товарищ |
| Greek | σύντροφε |
| Spanish | camarada |

Protocol pack example:

```
TypeScript

{  
  protocol: "socialist",  
  
  addressForms: {  
    comrade: {  
      de: "Genosse {surname}",  
      ru: "Товарищ {surname}",  
      el: "Σύντροφε {surname}",  
      es: "Camarada {surname}"  
    }  
  }  
}
```

Result:

```
Genosse Müller  
Товарищ Иванов  
Σύντροφε Γιάννη
```

---

# 6\. Important Design Rule

Salve **must never infer subculture automatically**.

These must be **explicitly configured**.

Example:

```
TypeScript

salve.resolve({  
  locale: "de-DE",  
  subcultures: \["student\_corporation"\],  
  corporationRelation: "bundesbruder",  
  surname: "Schmidt"  
})
```

Output:

```
Bundesbruder Schmidt
```

---

# 7\. Subculture Registry

To avoid chaos, Salve should maintain a registry:

```
protocol.student\_corporation  
protocol.socialist  
protocol.military  
protocol.scouting  
protocol.religious\_order  
protocol.academic
```

Each protocol defines:

```
address rules  
greeting rules  
formality overrides
```

---

# 8\. Interaction with Formal Titles

Example:

```
Bundesbruder Professor Müller
```

or

```
Genosse Professor Ivanov
```

Resolution rule:

```
subculture prefix + institutional title
```

Example template:

```
{protocolTitle} {institutionalTitle} {surname}
```

---

# 9\. Greeting vs Address

Subculture protocols may affect:

### Address

```
Genosse Müller  
Bundesbruder Schmidt
```

### Greeting

Example socialist greeting:

```
Freundschaft!
```

or

```
Solidarität!
```

But these should be rare and optional.

---

# 10\. Avoid Political Bias

Salve should treat protocols **symmetrically**.

Examples:

```
student\_corporation  
socialist  
military  
scouting  
monastic  
trade\_union
```

No ideological hierarchy.

---

# 11\. Example Full Context

```
TypeScript

salve.resolve({  
  locale: "de-DE",  
  greetingLanguage: "de-DE",  
  
  subcultures: \["student\_corporation"\],  
  
  corporationRelation: "bundesbruder",  
  
  surname: "Müller",  
  relationship: "member"  
})
```

Output:

```
Bundesbruder Müller
```

---

# 12\. Fallback Safety

If subculture config is incomplete:

```
subculture present but no relation
```

Fallback:

```
Guten Morgen.
```

Never guess.

# 13\. Example Package Structure

```
@salve/core  
@salve/runtime  
@salve/locale-nl  
@salve/locale-el  
  
@salve/protocol-student-corporations  
@salve/protocol-socialist  
@salve/protocol-military
```

# 14\. Why This Is Powerful

Salve becomes capable of handling:

-   academic culture
    
-   political culture
    
-   fraternal societies
    
-   religious orders
    
-   military ranks
    
-   trade union language
    
-   youth movements
    

This is something **no localization library currently models**.

---

# 15\. Conceptual Summary

Salve ends up with **four greeting domains**:

```
LINGUISTIC  
(language / locale)  
  
RELIGIOUS  
(orthodox, muslim, catholic)  
  
CIVIL  
(national holidays, time-of-day)  
  
SOCIOCULTURAL  
(student corporations, socialist, military)
```

These form a **layered protocol stack**.

---

If you want, I can also show you something extremely useful next:

**A complete taxonomy of greeting domains (≈40 cultural systems)** that Salve could eventually support — including military ranks, academic salutations, monastic orders, scouting culture, political movements, and more.

You: 

Wow, that would be fantastic. Please do so, go ahead.

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **structured taxonomy of greeting / salutation domains** that Salve could eventually support.  
The goal is **not to encode ideology**, but to model **real-world social protocols of address** the way CLDR models calendars, numbers, and dates.

Think of this as a **“Cultural Address Protocol Registry”**.

The taxonomy is grouped into major domains. Each domain may contain **protocol packs** distributed as optional Salve modules.

---

# 1\. Civil & Everyday Greetings

(base layer — always enabled)

These are **locale-based greetings** already central to Salve.

Examples:

-   time-of-day greetings
    
-   seasonal greetings
    
-   neutral salutations
    
-   email greetings
    

Examples:

| language | greeting |
| --- | --- |
| English | Good morning |
| Dutch | Goedemorgen |
| Greek | Καλημέρα |

These belong to:

```
Plain text

protocol.civil
```

---

# 2\. Religious Traditions

These greetings depend on **religious identity or liturgical calendar**.

Salve already partly models these.

Examples:

| tradition | greeting |
| --- | --- |
| Islam | السلام عليكم |
| Orthodox Christianity | Χριστός Ανέστη |
| Catholic | Pax vobiscum |
| Judaism | שלום |
| Hindu | Namaste |
| Sikh | Sat Sri Akal |
| Buddhist monastic | Namo Buddhaya |

Protocol packs:

```
Plain text

protocol.islamic  
protocol.orthodox  
protocol.catholic  
protocol.jewish  
protocol.hindu  
protocol.buddhist  
protocol.sikh
```

Some of these affect **greetings**, others **titles**.

Example:

```
Father Dimitrios  
Rabbi Cohen  
Imam Ahmed
```

---

# 3\. Political Movements

Historically many movements developed **specific greeting formulas**.

Examples:

| movement | greeting |
| --- | --- |
| socialist / communist | Comrade / Kamerad / Товарищ |
| fascist regimes (historical) | Camerata |
| anarchist movements | compañero |

Protocol examples:

```
Plain text

protocol.socialist  
protocol.communist  
protocol.anarchist  
protocol.revolutionary
```

Example outputs:

```
Genosse Müller  
Σύντροφε Γιάννη  
Товарищ Иванов
```

These are **address forms**, not necessarily greetings.

---

# 4\. Academic Protocol

Universities have extremely structured address conventions.

Examples:

| role | address |
| --- | --- |
| Professor | Professor Smith |
| Doctor | Dr Müller |
| Rector | Magnificence |
| Dean | Dean Johnson |

Protocol:

```
Plain text

protocol.academic
```

Examples:

```
Professor Papadopoulos  
Herr Doktor Müller  
Madame la Professeure
```

# 5\. Military Protocol

Military cultures rely heavily on **rank-based addressing**.

Examples:

| rank | address |
| --- | --- |
| Captain | Captain Smith |
| Colonel | Colonel Ivanov |
| General | General Zhang |

Protocol:

```
Plain text

protocol.military
```

Καλημέρα, Στρατηγέ.
```

Ranks differ by country.

---

# 6\. Government & Legal Protocol

Formal institutional addressing.

Protocol:

Plain text

```

---

# High-Level Taxonomy

Salve greeting systems could be grouped into **five master domains**:

```
LINGUISTIC  
(locale language rules)  
  
CIVIL  
(calendar + time + neutral greetings)  
  
RELIGIOUS  
(faith-based)  
  
INSTITUTIONAL  
(state, academic, military)  
  
SOCIOCULTURAL  
(fraternities, movements, guilds)
```

Each domain is implemented through **protocol packs**.

---

# Architectural Result

Salve ultimately resolves greetings from **four independent axes**:

```
locale  
religion  
institution  
subculture
```

Example context:

```
locale: de-DE  
religion: catholic  
institution: academic  
subculture: student\_corporation
```

Result:

```
Bundesbruder Professor Müller
```

---

# Final Thought

If implemented carefully, Salve would become the **first software library modeling human greeting protocols as a structured cultural system**.

CLDR handles:

-   numbers
    
-   currencies
    
-   calendars
    

Salve would handle:

-   **human address conventions**
    

which is an entirely new layer of internationalization.

---

If you'd like, I can next show something even more useful:

**A formal data schema for this protocol registry** so that protocol packs (like socialist or student-corporation) can plug into Salve in a standardized way.

You: 

Wow, absolutely. Please do so. Go ahead.

ChatGPT: 

Excellent — what we want now is a **formal protocol schema** that allows any greeting/address convention (student corporations, socialist movements, military ranks, monastic orders, etc.) to plug into Salve in a consistent way.

The key design principle is:

> **Protocols are data, not code.**

The runtime engine interprets the protocol definitions. This keeps Salve extensible and CLDR-compatible.

Below is a **first-class schema design** for the **Salve Cultural Protocol Registry (SCR)**.

---

# 1\. Core Concept

A **protocol pack** defines a system of greetings and/or address forms used by a particular **social context**.

Examples:

```
protocol.student\_corporation  
protocol.socialist  
protocol.military  
protocol.academic  
protocol.monastic
```

Each protocol pack provides:

-   address forms
    
-   greeting formulas
    
-   precedence rules
    
-   contextual triggers
    
-   language localization
    

---

# 2\. Top-Level Protocol Object

Every protocol pack exports a **ProtocolDefinition**.

Example structure:

```
TypeScript

interface SalveProtocolDefinition {  
  protocolId: string  
  version: string  
  domain: ProtocolDomain  
  
  description?: string  
  
  triggers?: ProtocolTrigger\[\]  
  
  addressForms?: AddressForm\[\]  
  greetingForms?: GreetingForm\[\]  
  
  compositionRules?: CompositionRule\[\]  
  
  precedence?: number  
}
```

---

# 3\. Protocol Domain

Protocols are categorized.

```
TypeScript

type ProtocolDomain \=  
  | "religious"  
  | "political"  
  | "academic"  
  | "military"  
  | "corporate"  
  | "student"  
  | "guild"  
  | "youth"  
  | "diaspora"  
  | "ceremonial"  
  | "internet"  
  | "sports"  
  | "professional"
```

Example:

```
domain: "student"
```

---

# 4\. Trigger Conditions

Protocols activate only when certain context conditions match.

```
TypeScript

interface ProtocolTrigger {  
  field: string  
  value: string | string\[\]  
}
```

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "field": "subculture",  
  "value": "student\_corporation"  
}
```

Multiple triggers can be combined.

---

# 5\. Address Forms

Defines titles used to address someone.

```
TypeScript

interface AddressForm {  
  id: string  
  
  role?: string  
  
  template: LocalizedTemplate  
  
  requiredContext?: string\[\]  
  
  optionalContext?: string\[\]  
  
  precedence?: number  
}
```

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "bundesbruder",  
  "role": "member",  
  
  "template": {  
    "de": "Bundesbruder {surname}"  
  }  
}
```

---

# 6\. Greeting Forms

Defines greeting phrases used by the protocol.

```
TypeScript

interface GreetingForm {  
  id: string  
  
  template: LocalizedTemplate  
  
  contexts?: GreetingContext\[\]  
}
```

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "friendship\_salute",  
  "template": {  
    "de": "Freundschaft!",  
    "ru": "Дружба!"  
  }  
}
```

---

# 7\. Localized Template

Supports multiple languages.

```
TypeScript

type LocalizedTemplate \= {  
  \[locale: string\]: string  
}
```

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "de": "Genosse {surname}",  
  "ru": "Товарищ {surname}",  
  "el": "Σύντροφε {surname}"  
}
```

---

# 8\. Composition Rules

Defines how protocols interact with titles and greetings.

```
TypeScript

interface CompositionRule {  
  type: "prefix" | "suffix" | "replace"  
  
  appliesTo: "address" | "greeting"  
  
  template: string  
}
```

Example:

```
prefix: "{protocolTitle} {institutionalTitle} {surname}"
```

Result:

```
Genosse Professor Müller
```

---

# 9\. Context Fields

Protocols may define extra context variables.

Example:

```
TypeScript

corporationRelation  
rank  
orderRole  
movementRank  
guildStatus
```

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "requiredContext": \["corporationRelation"\]  
}
```

---

# 13\. Integration into Salve Runtime

The runtime simply loads protocol packs.

Example:

```
TypeScript

import studentProtocol from "@salve/protocol-student-corporations"  
import socialistProtocol from "@salve/protocol-socialist"  
  
const salve \= new Salve({  
  packs: \[  
    nlLocale,  
    elLocale,  
    studentProtocol,  
    socialistProtocol  
  \]  
})
```

The engine then:

1.  Evaluates triggers
    
2.  Determines applicable protocols
    
3.  Applies precedence
    
4.  Generates greeting/address
    

---

# 14\. Precedence Model

Protocol precedence prevents conflicts.

Example order:

```
1 institutional (judge, monarch)  
2 military  
3 academic  
4 protocol packs  
5 religious  
6 civil greeting
```

---

# 15\. Protocol Safety Rules

Protocols must never activate unless:

```
explicitly enabled
```

This prevents accidental ideological greetings.

Example safe context:

```
subculture: \["socialist"\]
```

Without that → protocol ignored.

---

# 16\. Example Full Context

```
TypeScript

salve.resolve({  
  locale: "de-DE",  
  subculture: "student\_corporation",  
  corporationRelation: "bundesbruder",  
  surname: "Müller"  
})
```

Output:

```
Bundesbruder Müller
```

---

# 17\. Future Compatibility with CLDR

This schema aligns with CLDR principles:

| CLDR concept | Salve equivalent |
| --- | --- |
| LDML locale data | greeting locale pack |
| calendar rules | greeting calendar rules |
| number formats | greeting templates |
| plural rules | context triggers |

Protocols could eventually become:

```
CLDR cultural greeting extension
```

---

# 18\. Registry Governance

Salve should maintain a **central protocol registry**.

Example list:

```
protocol.student\_corporation  
protocol.socialist  
protocol.military  
protocol.scouting  
protocol.monastic  
protocol.academic  
protocol.diplomatic  
protocol.guild  
protocol.professional
```

Each protocol pack has:

```
schema validation  
versioning  
documentation
```

 ├─ metadata.json  
 └─ README.md
```

---

# 20\. The Big Picture

With this architecture, Salve models **three layers of greeting logic**:

```
LINGUISTIC  
(locale)  
  
CULTURAL  
(religion / holidays)  
  
SOCIAL PROTOCOL  
(subcultures / institutions)
```

These combine into a **complete greeting engine**.

---

If you'd like, the next step I can show you is something extremely powerful and practical:

**a full runtime resolution algorithm (≈120 lines of TypeScript)** that evaluates locale greetings, religious events, institutional titles, and protocol packs together in a deterministic way.

You: 

Yes please!

ChatGPT: 

```
TypeScript

/\* Salve runtime resolution algorithm (deterministic, data-driven)  
   - Evaluates: locale greeting packs + optional protocol packs + events + address rules  
   - Outputs: greetingText, addressText, salutation (or greeting/address only)  
   - No third-party deps. Drop into a TS React app or Salve runtime package.  
\*/  
  
type Phase \= "opening" | "closing";  
type Setting \= "ui" | "chat" | "email";  
type Formality \= "formal" | "informal" | "neutral";  
type Relationship \=  
  | "stranger"  
  | "acquaintance"  
  | "friend"  
  | "family"  
  | "superior"  
  | "subordinate";  
type OutputMode \= "salutation" | "greeting" | "address";  
type Role \= "initiator" | "responder";  
type Domain \= "institutional" | "protocol" | "religious" | "civil" | "time" | "baseline";  
  
type GreetingItem \= {  
  id: string;  
  domain: Domain;  
  locale: string; // pack locale id; runtime handles fallback chain  
  text: string; // templated text, e.g. "Καλημέρα{punct}{address}"  
  phase?: Phase;  
  setting?: Setting\[\];  
  formality?: Formality;  
  relationship?: Relationship\[\];  
  role?: Role;  
  dayPeriod?: "morning" | "afternoon" | "evening" | "night" | "midday";  
  eventRef?: string; // e.g. "orthodox.easter\_window" or "personal.nameday"  
  // If call-response pairs are relevant:  
  expectedResponse?: string;  
};  
  
type ProtocolAddressForm \= {  
  id: string; // e.g. "bundesbruder" | "comrade"  
  locales: Record<string, string\>; // locale->template e.g. { "de": "Bundesbruder {surname}" }  
  precedence?: number;  
};  
  
type ProtocolPack \= {  
  protocolId: string; // "student\_corporation" | "socialist" etc.  
  domain: "protocol";  
  triggers: { field: "subcultures"; value: string }\[\];  
  addressForms?: ProtocolAddressForm\[\];  
  greetingForms?: { id: string; locales: Record<string, string\>; precedence?: number }\[\];  
  precedence?: number; // pack-level  
};  
  
type AddressProfile \= {  
  firstName?: string;  
  surname?: string;  
  gender?: "male" | "female" | "nonbinary" | "unknown";  
  titleRole?:  
    | "none"  
    | "mr"  
    | "mrs"  
    | "ms"  
    | "mx"  
    | "dr"  
    | "prof"  
    | "prof\_dr"  
    | "judge"  
    | "police"  
    | "clergy";  
};  
  
type Event \= { id: string; kind: "personal" | "religious" | "civil"; active: boolean };  
  
type Context \= {  
  locale: string; // user locale (BCP47)  
  greetingLanguage?: string; // output language locale (BCP47)  
  now: Date;  
  phase: Phase;  
  setting: Setting;  
  formality?: Formality;  
  relationship?: Relationship;  
  role?: Role;  
  affiliations?: string\[\]; // e.g. \["orthodox","civil","secular"\]  
  subcultures?: string\[\]; // e.g. \["student\_corporation"\]  
  outputMode?: OutputMode;  
  address?: AddressProfile;  
};  
  
type ResolutionResult \= {  
  text: string;  
  greetingText: string;  
  addressText: string;  
  expectedResponse?: string;  
  trace: {  
    greetingId?: string;  
    addressSource?: "none" | "institutional" | "protocol" | "honorific";  
    matchedEvents: string\[\];  
    usedLocales: string\[\];  
    score?: number;  
  };  
};  
  
// ---------------- helpers ----------------  
  
function normalizeLocale(lc: string): string {  
  return lc.trim();  
}  
  
// Minimal CLDR-style fallback chain: "de-AT" -> \["de-AT","de","root"\]  
function localeFallbackChain(lc: string): string\[\] {  
  const norm \= normalizeLocale(lc);  
  if (!norm) return \["root"\];  
  const parts \= norm.split("-");  
  const chain: string\[\] \= \[\];  
  for (let i \= parts.length; i \>= 1; i\--) chain.push(parts.slice(0, i).join("-"));  
  chain.push("root");  
  // de-DE-u-rg-deby... handling omitted here; in Salve implement proper BCP47 parsing.  
  return Array.from(new Set(chain));  
}  
  
function dayPeriod(now: Date): GreetingItem\["dayPeriod"\] {  
  const h \= now.getHours();  
  if (h \>= 5 && h < 11) return "morning";  
  if (h \>= 11 && h < 14) return "midday";  
  if (h \>= 14 && h < 18) return "afternoon";  
  if (h \>= 18 && h < 23) return "evening";  
  return "night";  
}  
  
function pickLocaleTemplate(map: Record<string, string\>, lang: string): string | null {  
  const chain \= localeFallbackChain(lang);  
  // also try language-only match (e.g. "de" for "de-DE")  
  for (const lc of chain) {  
    if (map\[lc\]) return map\[lc\];  
    const base \= lc.split("-")\[0\];  
    if (map\[base\]) return map\[base\];  
  }  
  return null;  
}  
  
// ---------------- scoring ----------------  
  
function domainBaseScore(domain: Domain, evtKind?: Event\["kind"\]): number {  
  if (domain \=== "institutional") return 1200;  
  if (domain \=== "protocol") return 1000;  
  if (domain \=== "religious") return evtKind \=== "religious" ? 900 : 850;  
  if (domain \=== "civil") return evtKind \=== "civil" ? 800 : 750;  
  if (domain \=== "time") return 600;  
  return 400; // baseline  
}  
  
function specificityBonus(it: GreetingItem): number {  
  let s \= 0;  
  if (it.eventRef) s += 50;  
  if (it.relationship?.length) s += 30;  
  if (it.setting?.length) s += 20;  
  if (it.formality) s += 10;  
  if (it.role) s += 5;  
  if (it.dayPeriod) s += 8;  
  return s;  
}  
  
// ---------------- address resolution ----------------  
  
function buildInstitutionalOrHonorificAddress(ctx: Context): { text: string; source: ResolutionResult\["trace"\]\["addressSource"\] } {  
  const lang \= ctx.greetingLanguage ?? ctx.locale;  
  const a \= ctx.address ?? {};  
  const surname \= (a.surname ?? "").trim();  
  const first \= (a.firstName ?? "").trim();  
  const gender \= a.gender ?? "unknown";  
  const title \= a.titleRole ?? "none";  
  const rel \= ctx.relationship ?? "stranger";  
  const form \= ctx.formality ?? (rel \=== "friend" || rel \=== "family" ? "informal" : "formal");  
  
  // Institutional hard overrides  
  if (title \=== "judge") {  
    if (lang.startsWith("de")) return { text: "Euer Ehren", source: "institutional" };  
    return { text: "Your Honor", source: "institutional" };  
  }  
  if (title \=== "police") {  
    if (lang.startsWith("de")) return { text: surname ? \`Herr Wachtmeister ${surname}\` : "Herr Wachtmeister", source: "institutional" };  
    return { text: "Constable", source: "institutional" };  
  }  
  
  // If we cannot safely address (no surname & no reliable first-name informal), degrade to empty.  
  const canInformalName \= first.length \> 0 && (form \=== "informal");  
  const canFormalSurname \= surname.length \> 0 && (form \=== "formal" || rel \=== "stranger" || rel \=== "superior");  
  if (!canInformalName && !canFormalSurname) return { text: "", source: "none" };  
  
  // German example conventions (demo-grade)  
  if (lang.startsWith("de")) {  
    if (form \=== "informal") return { text: first || surname, source: "honorific" };  
    const honorific \= gender \=== "female" ? "Frau" : gender \=== "male" ? "Herr" : "";  
    const academic \=  
      title \=== "dr" ? "Doktor" : title \=== "prof" ? "Professor" : title \=== "prof\_dr" ? "Professor Doktor" : "";  
    const parts \= \[honorific, academic, surname || first\].filter(Boolean);  
    return { text: parts.join(" "), source: "honorific" };  
  }  
  
  // English-ish default  
  if (form \=== "informal") return { text: first || surname, source: "honorific" };  
  const honorific \=  
    title \=== "mr" ? "Mr." :  
    title \=== "mrs" ? "Mrs." :  
    title \=== "ms" ? "Ms." :  
    title \=== "mx" ? "Mx." :  
    title \=== "dr" ? "Dr." :  
    title \=== "prof" ? "Professor" :  
    title \=== "prof\_dr" ? "Professor Dr." :  
    (gender \=== "female" ? "Ms." : gender \=== "male" ? "Mr." : "Mx.");  
  const parts \= \[honorific, surname || first\].filter(Boolean);  
  return { text: parts.join(" "), source: "honorific" };  
}  
  
function buildProtocolAddress(ctx: Context, protocolPacks: ProtocolPack\[\]): { text: string; source: "protocol" | "none" } {  
  const subs \= new Set(ctx.subcultures ?? \[\]);  
  if (!subs.size) return { text: "", source: "none" };  
  
  const lang \= ctx.greetingLanguage ?? ctx.locale;  
  const surname \= (ctx.address?.surname ?? "").trim();  
  if (!surname) return { text: "", source: "none" };  
  
  // pick first applicable protocol address form with highest precedence  
  const candidates: { tpl: string; score: number }\[\] \= \[\];  
  for (const p of protocolPacks) {  
    const active \= p.triggers.every(t => t.field \=== "subcultures" && subs.has(t.value));  
    if (!active) continue;  
    for (const af of p.addressForms ?? \[\]) {  
      const tpl \= pickLocaleTemplate(af.locales, lang);  
      if (!tpl) continue;  
      const score \= (p.precedence ?? 0) \* 10 + (af.precedence ?? 0);  
      candidates.push({ tpl, score });  
    }  
  }  
  if (!candidates.length) return { text: "", source: "none" };  
  candidates.sort((a, b) => b.score \- a.score);  
  return { text: candidates\[0\].tpl.replace("{surname}", surname), source: "protocol" };  
}  
  
// ---------------- greeting resolution ----------------  
  
export function resolveSalve(  
  ctx: Context,  
  greetingPacks: GreetingItem\[\],  
  protocolPacks: ProtocolPack\[\],  
  events: Event\[\]  
): ResolutionResult {  
  const lang \= ctx.greetingLanguage ?? ctx.locale;  
  const usedLocales \= localeFallbackChain(lang);  
  
  // Sensible safe defaults (graceful degradation)  
  const relationship \= ctx.relationship ?? "stranger";  
  const formality: Formality \= ctx.formality ?? (relationship \=== "friend" || relationship \=== "family" ? "informal" : "formal");  
  const role: Role \= ctx.role ?? "initiator";  
  const activeEvents \= new Map(events.filter(e => e.active).map(e => \[e.id, e\]));  
  const dp \= dayPeriod(ctx.now);  
  
  // Address resolution (protocol can override honorific, institutional can override both)  
  const protAddr \= buildProtocolAddress({ ...ctx, formality, relationship, role }, protocolPacks);  
  const instAddr \= buildInstitutionalOrHonorificAddress({ ...ctx, formality, relationship, role });  
  const addressText \=  
    instAddr.source \=== "institutional" ? instAddr.text :  
    protAddr.source \=== "protocol" ? protAddr.text :  
    instAddr.text;  
  
  const addressSource \=  
    instAddr.source \=== "institutional" ? "institutional" :  
    protAddr.source \=== "protocol" ? "protocol" :  
    instAddr.source;  
  
  // Candidate selection with locale fallback  
  const candidates: { it: GreetingItem; score: number; localeRank: number; evtKind?: Event\["kind"\] }\[\] \= \[\];  
  for (const it of greetingPacks) {  
    // Locale applicability: choose best match in fallback chain  
    const localeRank \= usedLocales.indexOf(it.locale);  
    if (localeRank \=== \-1) continue;  
  
    // Context filters  
    if (it.phase && it.phase !== ctx.phase) continue;  
    if (it.role && it.role !== role) continue;  
    if (it.formality && it.formality !== formality && it.formality !== "neutral") continue;  
    if (it.setting && !it.setting.includes(ctx.setting)) continue;  
    if (it.relationship && !it.relationship.includes(relationship)) continue;  
    if (it.dayPeriod && it.dayPeriod !== dp) continue;  
  
    // Event gating  
    let evtKind: Event\["kind"\] | undefined;  
    if (it.eventRef) {  
      const ev \= activeEvents.get(it.eventRef);  
      if (!ev) continue;  
      evtKind \= ev.kind;  
    }  
  
    // Score: domain base + specificity + locale exactness (smaller rank = better)  
    const score \= domainBaseScore(it.domain, evtKind) + specificityBonus(it) \- localeRank \* 2;  
    candidates.push({ it, score, localeRank, evtKind });  
  }  
  
  // Absolute fallback candidate if none matches  
  if (!candidates.length) {  
    const fallbackGreeting \= lang.startsWith("el") ? "Καλημέρα" : lang.startsWith("nl") ? "Goedemorgen" : "Hello";  
    const outMode \= ctx.outputMode ?? "salutation";  
    const text \=  
      outMode \=== "greeting" ? fallbackGreeting :  
      outMode \=== "address" ? (addressText || fallbackGreeting) :  
      (addressText ? \`${fallbackGreeting}, ${addressText}\` : fallbackGreeting);  
  
    return {  
      text,  
      greetingText: fallbackGreeting,  
      addressText,  
      trace: { matchedEvents: \[\], usedLocales, addressSource }  
    };  
  }  
  
  // Deterministic choice  
  candidates.sort((a, b) => b.score \- a.score || a.localeRank \- b.localeRank || a.it.id.localeCompare(b.it.id));  
  const chosen \= candidates\[0\].it;  
  
  // Compose output (written-only patterns; extend per locale/setting in real Salve)  
  const greetingText \= chosen.text;  
  const outMode \= ctx.outputMode ?? "salutation";  
  let text: string;  
  if (outMode \=== "greeting") text \= greetingText;  
  else if (outMode \=== "address") text \= addressText || greetingText;  
  else {  
    if (ctx.setting \=== "email" && ctx.phase \=== "opening" && addressText) text \= \`${addressText},\`;  
    else text \= addressText ? \`${greetingText}, ${addressText}\` : greetingText;  
  }  
  
  return {  
    text,  
    greetingText,  
    addressText,  
    expectedResponse: chosen.expectedResponse,  
    trace: {  
      greetingId: chosen.id,  
      addressSource,  
      matchedEvents: Array.from(activeEvents.keys()),  
      usedLocales,  
      score: candidates\[0\].score  
    }  
  };  
}
```

You: 

So we put a lot of effort already in localization of greetings based on time awareness, especially religious feast days, and we went even a great length in researching how to implement name day celebrations in Greece, in Bulgaria. Now, of course, we should also consider official bank holidays in many countries. So these differ. You've got the American bank holidays, holidays in Puerto Rico, bank holidays in Japan, in France, Germany, and so on. So the official day off, which we might also want to consider as a time-based context for appropriate greeting. But then arises the question how to get this vast data set of worldwide banking holidays. If I go searching on NPM, I can find already over 800 packages dealing with bank holidays for several different countries. There are packages for Swedish bank holidays, packages for French bank holidays, and so on. Of course, we do not want to reinvent the wheel or do duplicate work. So we want to build on the shoulders of that previous work so that our salve library will effectively serve as a wrapper around many hundreds of packages that already arrange and maintain bank holidays worldwide. How do you propose we would go about, how could we wrap and serve and use all these many, possibly hundreds of packages?

ChatGPT: 

You are asking exactly the right architectural question. The moment Salve begins to incorporate **global holiday calendars**, the project risks turning into a **calendar data maintenance nightmare** unless it is designed as a **federated adapter system**.

The correct approach is **not**:

-   copying holiday datasets
    
-   depending on hundreds of npm packages directly
    
-   maintaining our own global holiday database
    

Instead, Salve should implement a **Holiday Provider Interface** and a **plugin-based adapter layer**.  
Salve becomes the **orchestrator**, not the data owner.

Below is the architecture I would recommend.

---

# 1\. Core Idea: Holiday Providers

Salve should not know where holiday data comes from.

It only asks providers:

> “What holidays apply to this date and locale?”

Define a generic interface:

```
TypeScript

interface HolidayProvider {  
  id: string  
  
  supports(locale: string): boolean  
  
  getHolidays(date: Date, locale: string): Promise<HolidayEvent\[\]>  
}
```

HolidayEvent:

```
TypeScript

interface HolidayEvent {  
  id: string  
  name: string  
  type: "bank" | "civil" | "religious"  
  
  start: Date  
  end?: Date  
  
  locale: string  
}
```

Examples:

```
"bank.us.independence\_day"  
"bank.jp.golden\_week"  
"bank.fr.bastille\_day"
```

---

# 2\. Holiday Plugin Architecture

Holiday providers are distributed as **plugins**.

Example npm packages:

```
@salve/holiday-nager  
@salve/holiday-date-holidays  
@salve/holiday-sweden  
@salve/holiday-france  
@salve/holiday-japan
```

Each plugin wraps an existing library.

Example wrapper:

```
TypeScript

import Holidays from "date-holidays"  
  
export class DateHolidaysProvider implements HolidayProvider {  
  
  id \= "date-holidays"  
  
  supports(locale: string) {  
    return true  
  }  
  
  async getHolidays(date: Date, locale: string) {  
  
    const hd \= new Holidays(locale)  
  
    const list \= hd.getHolidays(date)  
  
    return list.map(h => ({  
      id: \`bank.${locale}.${h.rule}\`,  
      name: h.name,  
      type: "bank",  
      start: new Date(h.date),  
      locale  
    }))  
  }  
}
```

This means Salve **delegates all calendar logic**.

---

# 3\. Provider Registry

Salve runtime maintains a provider registry.

```
TypeScript

class HolidayRegistry {  
  
  providers: HolidayProvider\[\] \= \[\]  
  
  register(provider: HolidayProvider) {  
    this.providers.push(provider)  
  }  
  
  async resolve(date: Date, locale: string) {  
  
    const events: HolidayEvent\[\] \= \[\]  
  
    for (const provider of this.providers) {  
  
      if (!provider.supports(locale)) continue  
  
      const res \= await provider.getHolidays(date, locale)  
  
      events.push(...res)  
    }  
  
    return events  
  }  
}
```

---

# 4\. Salve Runtime Integration

The greeting engine simply asks the registry:

```
TypeScript

const holidays \= await holidayRegistry.resolve(  
  context.now,  
  context.locale  
)
```

Then these events are inserted into the **event system** we designed earlier.

Example event:

```
bank.fr.bastille\_day
```

The greeting pack may define:

```
JSON

{  
  "eventRef": "bank.fr.bastille\_day",  
  "text": "Bonne fête nationale"  
}
```

---

# 5\. Preventing Dependency Explosion

You **do not want 800 dependencies**.

Instead, support **a few major meta-libraries**.

Good candidates:

| Library | Coverage |
| --- | --- |
| date-holidays | ~100 countries |
| nager-date | ~110 countries |
| holidays-jp | Japan |
| moment-business-days | business calendars |

So Salve ships adapters for maybe **3–5 major providers**.

---

# 6\. Multiple Providers Can Coexist

Example configuration:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  
  holidayProviders: \[  
  
    new DateHolidaysProvider(),  
    new NagerProvider(),  
  
    new CustomCompanyHolidayProvider()  
  \]  
})
```

This allows:

-   public holidays
    
-   corporate holidays
    
-   university holidays
    

to coexist.

---

# 7\. Event Normalization Layer

Different libraries use different naming.

Salve should normalize them.

Example:

| raw | normalized |
| --- | --- |
| Independence Day | bank.us.independence\_day |
| Bastille Day | bank.fr.bastille\_day |
| Golden Week | bank.jp.golden\_week |

Normalization rules live in:

```
@salve/holiday-normalizer
```

---

# 8\. Caching Layer

Holiday queries should be cached.

Example:

```
TypeScript

holidayCache\["2026-07-04:en-US"\]
```

Most holidays are deterministic.

---

# 9\. Regional Hierarchies

Many holidays depend on subdivisions.

Examples:

```
US-CA  
DE-BY  
JP-TOKYO
```

Holiday providers must support:

```
country  
region  
city
```

Salve simply passes the locale.

---

# 10\. Offline vs Online Providers

Some libraries are offline.

Others require APIs.

Example:

```
NagerDate REST API
```

Adapter example:

```
TypeScript

class NagerApiProvider implements HolidayProvider {  
  
 async getHolidays(date, locale) {  
  
   const year \= date.getFullYear()  
  
   const res \= await fetch(  
     \`https://date.nager.at/api/v3/PublicHolidays/${year}/${locale}\`  
   )  
  
   const json \= await res.json()  
  
   return json.map(h => ({  
     id: \`bank.${locale}.${slugify(h.name)}\`,  
     name: h.name,  
     type: "bank",  
     start: new Date(h.date),  
     locale  
   }))  
 }  
}
```

---

# 11\. Greeting Rule Example

Greeting pack:

```
JSON

{  
  "eventRef": "bank.us.independence\_day",  
  "text": "Happy Independence Day!"  
}
```

For France:

```
JSON

{  
  "eventRef": "bank.fr.bastille\_day",  
  "text": "Bonne fête nationale"  
}
```

# 12\. Handling Multi-Day Holidays

Example:

```
Golden Week (Japan)  
Chinese New Year  
Easter weekend
```

HolidayEvent supports:

```
start  
end
```

So greeting rule can trigger for entire range.

import { Salve } from "@salve/runtime"

Done.

a semantic event engine

rather than

a holiday database
```

This keeps the project manageable.

So Salve's holiday provider layer becomes:

CLDR-compatible localization  
\+ external calendar intelligence
```

Salve Runtime  
   │  
   ├── Greeting Packs  
   │  
   ├── Protocol Packs

Salve orchestrates them all.
