```
locale packs  
protocol packs  
holiday providers  
nameday providers
```

Designing that properly will make the whole system scalable.

You: 

Yes, please, that would be great. Go ahead.

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **clean, scalable Salve plugin architecture spec** that covers the four plugin classes you now need:

-   **Locale packs** (greeting/address data)
    
-   **Protocol packs** (subculture/institutional conventions)
    
-   **Holiday providers** (bank/civil calendars)
    
-   **Nameday providers** (large datasets, often remote)
    

It’s designed to be:

-   runtime/data separated
    
-   tree-shakeable
    
-   deterministic
    
-   CLDR-aligned (data packs convertible later)
    
-   safe by default (no ideological inference)
    

---

# 1) Plugin Categories

Salve should define **four plugin kinds**:

```
Plain text

pack.locale  
pack.protocol  
provider.holiday  
provider.nameday
```

A plugin is either:

-   a **Pack**: contributes *data* (pure JSON/TS object)
    
-   a **Provider**: contributes *events* (computed or fetched)
    

---

# 2) Core Plugin Contract

All plugins share a minimal manifest:

```
TypeScript

export type SalvePluginKind \=  
  | "pack.locale"  
  | "pack.protocol"  
  | "provider.holiday"  
  | "provider.nameday";  
  
export interface SalvePluginBase {  
  kind: SalvePluginKind;  
  id: string;                 // stable canonical id, e.g. "locale.nl", "holiday.date-holidays"  
  version: string;            // semver  
  description?: string;  
  
  // BCP-47 / CLDR-ish coverage declaration  
  supports?: {  
    locales?: string\[\];       // e.g. \["nl", "nl-NL", "nl-BE", "el", "el-GR"\]  
    regions?: string\[\];       // optional: \["BE","NL","GR"\]  
    scripts?: string\[\];       // optional: \["Latn","Grek"\]  
  };  
  
  // Optional: influence selection if multiple plugins overlap  
  precedence?: number;        // higher wins (used ONLY within same kind)  
}
```

---

# 3) Context and Resolution Pipeline Hooks

Salve has a deterministic pipeline. Plugins can hook in only at defined points.

```
TypeScript

export type SalvePhase \= "opening" | "closing";  
  
export interface SalveContext {  
  now: Date;  
  locale: string;  
  greetingLanguage?: string;  
  
  // social  
  relationship?: "stranger"|"acquaintance"|"friend"|"family"|"superior"|"subordinate";  
  formality?: "formal"|"informal"|"neutral";  
  setting?: "ui"|"chat"|"email";  
  role?: "initiator"|"responder";  
  
  // cultural flags  
  affiliations?: string\[\];   // e.g. \["civil","secular","orthodox","muslim"\]  
  subcultures?: string\[\];    // e.g. \["student\_corporation","socialist"\]  
  
  // personal  
  person?: {  
    givenNames?: string\[\];  
    surname?: string;  
    gender?: "male"|"female"|"nonbinary"|"unknown";  
    titleRole?: string;      // "dr","prof","judge", etc.  
    birthday?: string;       // YYYY-MM-DD  
    namedaysEnabled?: boolean;  
  };  
  
  phase: SalvePhase;  
  outputMode?: "salutation"|"greeting"|"address";  
}
```

Events are the lingua franca between providers and packs:

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveEvent {  
  id: string;                          // canonical: "bank.fr.bastille\_day"  
  kind: "bank"|"civil"|"religious"|"personal"|"protocol";  
  label?: string;                      // localized optional label  
  start: Date;  
  end?: Date;  
  confidence?: number;                 // 0..1 (rarely needed)  
  source: string;                      // provider/plugin id  
  precedence?: number;                 // within kind  
}
```

---

# 4) Locale Pack Plugin

Locale packs contribute **lexemes + templates + address rules**.

They are **pure data** and must be loadable in any environment.

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveLocalePack extends SalvePluginBase {  
  kind: "pack.locale";  
  
  // A pack may contain multiple locales (nl, nl-BE, nl-NL etc.)  
  locales: Record<string, {  
    greetings: SalveGreetingRule\[\];  
    addressRules?: SalveAddressRuleSet;  
    composition?: SalveCompositionRules;  
  }>;  
}
```

Greeting rules reference events (by `eventRef`) or time-of-day etc.

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveGreetingRule {  
  id: string;                 // stable  
  locale: string;             // exact key in pack  
  priority: number;           // higher wins inside locale  
  
  // matching conditions  
  when: {  
    eventRef?: string;        // "bank.us.independence\_day", "personal.nameday"  
    dayPeriod?: "morning"|"midday"|"afternoon"|"evening"|"night";  
    phase?: SalvePhase;  
    formality?: "formal"|"informal"|"neutral";  
    relationship?: SalveContext\["relationship"\] | SalveContext\["relationship"\]\[\];  
    setting?: SalveContext\["setting"\] | SalveContext\["setting"\]\[\];  
    role?: SalveContext\["role"\];  
    affiliationsAny?: string\[\];  // e.g. \["orthodox"\]  
    subculturesAny?: string\[\];   // e.g. \["student\_corporation"\]  
  };  
  
  // output  
  template: string;           // e.g. "Καλημέρα"  
  expectedResponseTemplate?: string; // optional call-response  
}
```

---

# 5) Protocol Pack Plugin (Subculture / Institutional Conventions)

Protocol packs contribute **address forms and/or greetings** gated by explicit `subcultures`.

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveProtocolPack extends SalvePluginBase {  
  kind: "pack.protocol";  
  
  protocolId: string;       // e.g. "student\_corporation"  
  triggers: { field: "subcultures"; value: string }\[\];  
  
  addressForms?: SalveProtocolAddressForm\[\];  
  greetingForms?: SalveProtocolGreetingForm\[\];  
  
  // optional: override composition / precedence within protocol context  
  composition?: SalveCompositionRules;  
}
```

Address form:

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveProtocolAddressForm {  
  id: string;               // "bundesbruder" | "comrade"  
  priority: number;  
  locales: Record<string, string\>;   // locale->template "Bundesbruder {surname}"  
  requires?: string\[\];      // e.g. \["person.surname"\]  
}
```

---

# 6) Holiday Provider Plugin

Holiday providers contribute **events** for a given date window.

Key rule: provider returns *events only*; greeting text is still pack-defined.

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveHolidayProvider extends SalvePluginBase {  
  kind: "provider.holiday";  
  
  supportsLocale(locale: string): boolean;  
  
  // fetch holidays for a range (so UI can show upcoming)  
  getHolidays(args: {  
    locale: string;  
    start: Date;  
    end: Date;  
  }): Promise<SalveEvent\[\]>;  
}
```

Examples of adapters:

-   `@salve/provider-date-holidays`
    
-   `@salve/provider-nager-api`
    
-   `@salve/provider-jp-holidays`
    

---

# 7) Nameday Provider Plugin

Namedays behave like holidays but are often huge datasets, so they are their own provider type.

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveNamedayProvider extends SalvePluginBase {  
  kind: "provider.nameday";  
  
  supportsLocale(locale: string): boolean;  
  
  // query by date (small payload)  
  getNamedaysForDate(args: {  
    locale: string;  
    date: Date;  
  }): Promise<{  
    saints: { id: string; names: string\[\]; }\[\];  
  }>;  
  
  // optional: map a user-supplied name to saint id(s) (server-side best)  
  resolveNameToSaintIds?(args: {  
    locale: string;  
    name: string;  
  }): Promise<{ saintIds: string\[\]; confidence: number }>;  
}
```

Salve runtime uses this provider to inject events like:

```
Plain text

personal.nameday
```

…only if a match occurs for the user’s name set.

---

# 8) Composition Rules (Greeting + Address Assembly)

Packs/protocols can define how to assemble output:

```
TypeScript

export interface SalveCompositionRules {  
  patterns: {  
    ui?: string;        // "{greeting}, {address}"  
    chat?: string;  
    emailOpening?: string;  // "{address},"  
    emailClosing?: string;  // "{greeting}"  
  };  
  punctuation?: {  
    comma?: string;  
    fullStop?: string;  
  };  
}
```

Defaults (global safe):

-   salutation: `{greeting}` if address missing
    
-   email opening: `{address},` if address exists else `{greeting},`
    

---

# 9) Runtime Plugin Host

Salve runtime loads plugins and runs a fixed pipeline:

```
Plain text

1) Normalize context + defaults  
2) Collect events:  
   - holiday providers (range)  
   - nameday provider (date)  
   - internal events (birthday, new year window, etc.)  
3) Select greeting candidate rules from locale packs + protocol packs  
4) Resolve address:  
   - institutional role (judge, police)  
   - protocol address (bundesbruder, comrade)  
   - standard honorific (Mr/Ms/Dr/Prof) using locale addressRules  
5) Compose output per setting + phase + outputMode  
6) Emit trace (for demo/dev)
```

---

# 10) Conflict Resolution Rules

Deterministic, global:

1.  Highest `domain precedence` wins in this order:
    
    -   institutional address overrides everything in address slot
        
    -   protocol packs override honorifics (only when explicitly triggered)
        
    -   religious > civil > time-of-day > baseline
        
2.  Within same domain:
    
    -   higher `priority` wins
        
3.  Tie-breakers:
    
    -   narrower locale match wins (`nl-BE` beats `nl`)
        
    -   stable lexical ordering by rule id
        

---

# 11) Safety Defaults (No “Ideology Inference”)

-   Protocol packs MUST require explicit `subcultures` values in context.
    
-   Religious greetings MUST require explicit `affiliations`.
    
-   Providers may return events, but rules should require `affiliationsAny` unless the event is civil/bank.
    

This keeps Salve safe and predictable.

---

# 12) Packaging Guidance

Recommended NPM layout:

-   `@salve/runtime`
    
-   `@salve/core`
    
-   `@salve/locale-nl`
    
-   `@salve/locale-el`
    
-   `@salve/protocol-student-corporations`
    
-   `@salve/protocol-socialist`
    
-   `@salve/provider-date-holidays`
    
-   `@salve/provider-nager-api`
    
-   `@salve/provider-namedays-remote-el`
    

And optional meta-package:

-   `salve` (convenience)
    

---

# 13) What This Enables Immediately

With just these contracts, a React developer can:

-   bundle Dutch + Greek greeting/address packs
    
-   choose 1 holiday provider adapter
    
-   route namedays to their own REST API
    
-   optionally add niche subculture greetings as separate installable packs
    

All without Salve owning any global datasets.

-   a **monorepo package layout** showing exactly where these contracts live and how packs/providers are tested and published.

ChatGPT: 

Excellent — this is the right moment to **freeze the structural backbone of SALVE** so that the ecosystem can grow without breaking compatibility. I will provide two things:

1.  **Canonical JSON Schemas** for each plugin kind
    
2.  **Recommended monorepo layout** for the SALVE project
    

The schemas are designed to be:

-   **CLDR-compatible in spirit** (LDML concepts: locale inheritance, event-based data)
    
-   **strictly data-only** (runtime logic lives elsewhere)
    
-   **JSON-first** (convertible to LDML XML later)
    
-   **versionable**
    
-   **pack-distribution friendly**
    

---

# 1\. Global Plugin Manifest Schema

Every plugin package MUST include a `salve.manifest.json`.

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/plugin-manifest.json",  
  "id": "locale.nl",  
  "kind": "pack.locale",  
  "version": "1.0.0",  
  "description": "Dutch greetings and address conventions",  
  "supports": {  
    "locales": \["nl", "nl-NL", "nl-BE"\],  
    "regions": \["NL", "BE"\]  
  },  
  "precedence": 10,  
  "entry": "data.json"  
}
```

### Fields

| Field | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| id | globally unique plugin id |
| kind | plugin category |
| version | semver |
| supports | CLDR-style coverage |
| precedence | conflict resolution weight |
| entry | JSON data file |

---

# 2\. Locale Pack Schema

File: `data.json`

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/locale-pack.json",  
  
  "localePack": "nl",  
  
  "locales": {  
  
    "nl": {  
  
      "greetings": \[  
  
        {  
          "id": "nl.good\_morning",  
          "priority": 10,  
          "when": {  
            "dayPeriod": "morning"  
          },  
          "template": "Goedemorgen"  
        },  
  
        {  
          "id": "nl.hello",  
          "priority": 1,  
          "template": "Hallo"  
        }  
  
      \],  
  
      "addressRules": {  
  
        "honorifics": {  
  
          "male": "heer",  
          "female": "mevrouw",  
          "neutral": "beste"  
        }  
  
      }  
  
    }  
  }  
}
```

### Greeting Rule Schema

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "string",  
  "priority": "number",  
  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "string",  
    "dayPeriod": "morning|midday|afternoon|evening|night",  
    "formality": "formal|informal|neutral",  
    "relationship": \["friend","family"\],  
    "setting": \["ui","chat","email"\],  
    "role": "initiator|responder",  
    "affiliationsAny": \["orthodox","muslim"\],  
    "subculturesAny": \["student\_corporation"\]  
  },  
  
  "template": "string",  
  
  "expectedResponseTemplate": "string"  
}
```

---

# 3\. Protocol Pack Schema

Example: socialist greetings, student corporations.

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/protocol-pack.json",  
  
  "protocolId": "student\_corporation",  
  
  "triggers": \[  
    {  
      "field": "subcultures",  
      "value": "student\_corporation"  
    }  
  \],  
  
  "addressForms": \[  
  
    {  
      "id": "bundesbruder",  
  
      "priority": 100,  
  
      "locales": {  
        "de": "Bundesbruder {surname}"  
      }  
    }  
  
  \],  
  
  "greetingForms": \[  
  
    {  
      "id": "corporation\_greeting",  
  
      "priority": 80,  
  
      "locales": {  
        "de": "Vivat"  
      }  
    }  
  
  \]  
}
```

---

# 4\. Holiday Provider Manifest Schema

Provider plugins do not contain greeting text.

They only declare the provider.

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/provider-manifest.json",  
  
  "id": "holiday.date-holidays",  
  
  "kind": "provider.holiday",  
  
  "version": "1.0.0",  
  
  "description": "Adapter for date-holidays library",  
  
  "supports": {  
    "regions": \["\*"\]  
  },  
  
  "entry": "provider.js"  
}
```

Runtime loads provider implementation.

---

# 5\. Nameday Provider Manifest

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/provider-manifest.json",  
  
  "id": "nameday.el",  
  
  "kind": "provider.nameday",  
  
  "version": "1.0.0",  
  
  "supports": {  
    "locales": \["el", "el-GR"\]  
  },  
  
  "entry": "provider.js"  
}
```

The actual dataset may live remotely.

---

# 6\. Canonical Event Schema

Providers return events shaped like:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "bank.fr.bastille\_day",  
  
  "kind": "bank",  
  
  "start": "2027-07-14",  
  
  "end": "2027-07-14",  
  
  "label": "Bastille Day",  
  
  "source": "holiday.date-holidays"  
}
```

Kinds:

```
bank  
civil  
religious  
personal  
protocol
```

---

# 7\. Nameday Dataset Format

Large datasets must use **two-layer design**.

### Date → Saint IDs

```
JSON

{  
  "2027-01-07": \["saint.ioannis"\],  
  "2027-01-20": \["saint.efthymios"\]  
}
```

### Saint ID → Names

```
JSON

{  
  "saint.ioannis": {  
    "names": \[  
      "Ioannis",  
      "Yannis",  
      "Giannis",  
      "John",  
      "Jean",  
      "Jan"  
    \]  
  }  
}
```

This allows **fuzzy mapping**.

---

# 8\. CLDR Compatibility Strategy

SALVE should mirror CLDR structure:

| CLDR concept | SALVE |
| --- | --- |
| locale inheritance | locale pack fallback |
| calendar events | event providers |
| display names | templates |
| LDML | JSON schema |

Later transformation path:

```
SALVE JSON  
→ LDML XML  
→ CLDR contribution
```

---

# 9\. Monorepo Layout

Recommended:

```
salve/  
│  
├─ packages/  
│  
│  ├─ core/  
│  │   ├─ src/  
│  │   └─ package.json  
│  
│  ├─ runtime/  
│  │   ├─ src/  
│  │   └─ package.json  
│  
│  ├─ schema/  
│  │   ├─ plugin-manifest.schema.json  
│  │   ├─ locale-pack.schema.json  
│  │   ├─ protocol-pack.schema.json  
│  │   └─ provider.schema.json  
│  
│  ├─ locale-nl/  
│  │   ├─ salve.manifest.json  
│  │   └─ data.json  
│  
│  ├─ locale-el/  
│  │   ├─ salve.manifest.json  
│  │   └─ data.json  
│  
│  ├─ protocol-student-corporations/  
│  │   ├─ salve.manifest.json  
│  │   └─ data.json  
│  
│  ├─ protocol-socialist/  
│  │   ├─ salve.manifest.json  
│  │   └─ data.json  
│  
│  ├─ provider-date-holidays/

│

│  │   ├─ provider.ts

```

Plugin compatibility:

```
SALVE major version = schema version
```

Example:

```

```

Breaking schema change → major version bump.

Each pack should pass:

```
salve validate-pack
```

Checks:

-   schema validation
    
-   locale coverage

-   precedence conflicts

# 15\. The Big Picture

Salve becomes a **global greeting intelligence layer**:

```
Context  
   ↓  
Event Providers  
   ↓  
Locale + Protocol Packs  
   ↓  
Composition Engine  
   ↓  
Greeting / Salutation Output
```

No duplication of datasets.

Only orchestration.

---

If you'd like, I can also show you something **very important for Salve’s long-term success**:

**the formal precedence model for greeting selection** (a deterministic scoring system for rules).

Without that, greeting conflicts between packs will eventually become chaotic.

You: 

All right, please do that as well, and then show how it solves the problem that might arise that greeting conflicts between PACs might become chaotic. So what would be the formal precedence model for greeting selection, deterministic scoring?

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **formal, deterministic precedence model** for Salve’s greeting selection that prevents chaos when many PACs (packs/providers/protocols) overlap.

The model is intentionally **simple, explainable, and auditable** (critical for developer trust), while still supporting rich specificity.

---

## 1) Key Idea

Salve chooses exactly **one “best” rule** from the candidate set by computing a **total order**:

> **Sort candidates by a fixed tuple** (highest-first), with stable tie-breakers.

No fuzzy “AI” decisions. No nondeterminism.

This makes conflicts predictable, testable, and debuggable.

---

## 2) Candidate Definition

A **candidate** is a rule from a Locale Pack or Protocol Pack that:

-   matches locale fallback chain
    
-   matches basic context filters (phase, setting, etc.)
    
-   and, if it references an event (`eventRef`), that event is active
    

Each candidate is evaluated with its metadata:

-   `kind`: `pack.locale` or `pack.protocol`
    
-   `domain`: `institutional | protocol | religious | bank | civil | time | baseline`
    
-   `rulePriority`: `priority` field on rule
    
-   `packPrecedence`: from the plugin manifest
    
-   `matchSpecificity`: computed
    
-   `localeDistance`: computed
    
-   `eventStrength`: computed
    
-   `freshness`: computed for “window events” (e.g., first-open-in-month)
    

---

## 3) The Ordering Tuple

For each candidate rule `r`, compute:

```
Plain text

ScoreTuple(r) =  
(  
  DomainRank(r),               // primary: what kind of rule is it?  
  EventRank(r),                // does it match a specific event?  
  PackPrecedence(r),           // plugin-level precedence  
  RulePriority(r),             // rule-level priority inside the pack  
  SpecificityScore(r),         // how many constraints did it match?  
  LocaleMatchScore(r),         // exact locale match beats fallback  
  SettingMatchScore(r),        // exact setting beats generic  
  FormalityMatchScore(r),      // exact formality beats generic  
  RelationshipMatchScore(r),   // exact relationship beats generic  
  WindowFreshnessScore(r),     // used for "don't repeat" / first-open  
  StableIdTieBreak(r)          // stable sort by rule id + pack id  
)
```

Salve selects the candidate with the **lexicographically greatest** tuple.

This guarantees determinism.

---

## 4) DomainRank

This is what stops “chaos” at the top.

Define a **fixed rank table** (constants in runtime):

```
Plain text

institutional   = 9000   (Your Honor, Herr Doktor conventions)  
protocol        = 8000   (Bundesbruder, Comrade, etc.)  
religious       = 7000   (Christos Anesti, Ramadan greetings)  
bank            = 6500   (public/bank holiday greetings)  
civil           = 6000   (national day, etc.)  
time            = 5000   (good morning / good evening)  
baseline        = 1000   (hello)
```

Implication:

-   A matching **protocol** rule always beats a mere time-of-day greeting.
    
-   A matching **religious** holiday rule beats “good morning.”
    
-   Bank holidays beat regular time-of-day greetings.
    

This is how you *avoid packs fighting* in an uncontrolled way.

---

## 5) EventRank

Not all events are equal. Some are “major” (e.g., Easter), some minor.

Events from providers have:

-   `kind` (bank/civil/religious/personal/protocol)
    
-   optional `precedence`
    
-   optional `confidence`
    

Compute:

```
Plain text

EventRank =  
  0 if no eventRef  
  else 1000 + (event.precedence || 0) + floor(100 \* confidence)
```

This means:

-   event-based rules systematically beat non-event rules within a domain
    
-   but still remain under DomainRank control
    

---

## 6) PackPrecedence and RulePriority

These two numbers solve “two different packs both define the same greeting.”

-   `packPrecedence`: “which pack wins” (policy level)
    
-   `rulePriority`: “which rule wins inside a pack” (local level)
    

Example use:

-   You ship an “official” `@salve/locale-el` pack with precedence 10
    
-   A developer ships an in-house `@company/salve-el-overrides` pack with precedence 100
    

Now company overrides win without touching Salve internals.

---

## 7) SpecificityScore

Specificity should be **countable and predictable**, not vague.

Define weights for matched constraints:

```
Plain text

eventRef          +40  
affiliationsAny   +18  
subculturesAny    +18  
dayPeriod         +10  
phase             +8  
setting           +8  
formality         +6  
relationship      +6  
role              +4
```

Then:

```
Plain text

SpecificityScore = sum(weights of fields present in rule.when)
```

Important: this counts **rule specificity**, not whether it matches (it already matches).  
More constrained rules win over generic rules inside the same domain/priority.

---

## 8) LocaleMatchScore

Locale fallback is a major source of chaos unless it’s explicit.

Compute locale distance:

-   exact locale match: `0` distance
    
-   language-only fallback: `1`
    
-   root fallback: `2+`
    

Then:

```
Plain text

LocaleMatchScore = 100 - 10\*distance
```

So `el-GR` beats `el` beats `root`.

---

## 9) Setting/Formality/Relationship Match Scores

These avoid subtle conflicts where a generic UI greeting would “steal” from an email greeting.

Example:

-   setting-specific rule beats setting-generic rule.
    

```
Plain text

SettingMatchScore =  
  +10 if rule.when.setting exists  
  else 0
```

Same for formality/relationship:

```
Plain text

FormalityMatchScore =  
  +8 if rule.when.formality exists  
  else 0  
  
RelationshipMatchScore =  
  +8 if rule.when.relationship exists  
  else 0
```

---

## 10) WindowFreshnessScore

This solves your “don’t repeat Happy New Year after already greeted” requirement.

Some rules are “windowed” (New Year window, first open in month, etc.).

Salve should maintain a **local greeting memory** per user:

-   “already used rule X during window W”
    

Then:

```
Plain text

WindowFreshnessScore =  
  +20 if rule is windowed AND not yet used in its window  
  else 0
```

If it was already used: it becomes ineligible, or gets a heavy penalty.

This prevents repetitive greetings deterministically.

---

## 11) Stable Tie-Breakers

To guarantee absolute determinism, finalize with:

```
Plain text

StableIdTieBreak = (packId, ruleId) lexicographic ascending
```

So even perfect ties resolve the same way every time.

---

# 12) Why This Prevents PAC Chaos

## Problem A: Two locale packs define “Happy National Day”

-   Both match same event.
    
-   Both match locale.
    
-   Who wins?
    

Solution:

-   `packPrecedence` decides (policy-level override).
    
-   If same pack precedence, `rulePriority` decides.
    

No chaos.

---

## Problem B: A protocol pack and a religious pack both match

Example: a user is in a socialist subculture and it’s Easter.

Which greeting wins?

Solution:

-   DomainRank controls: protocol (8000) vs religious (7000)
    
-   Unless developer chooses to configure otherwise by domain policy.
    

This avoids accidental “Christos Anesti” overwriting “Comrade …” or vice versa.

(And you can expose a configuration knob to change domain ranking if desired.)

---

## Problem C: A bank holiday and time-of-day greeting both apply

Solution:

-   DomainRank bank (6500) > time (5000) → holiday greeting wins.
    

---

## Problem D: Locale fallback conflicts

Example: `nl-BE` vs `nl` rules both match.

Solution:

-   LocaleMatchScore: exact locale wins.
    

---

## Problem E: Overly generic pack steals output

Example: root-level “Hello” wins due to high rulePriority.

Solution:

-   LocaleMatchScore strongly favors closer locale.
    
-   DomainRank + specificity ensures “Hello” (baseline) can’t beat time-of-day or civil event rules.
    

---

# 13) Implementation Sketch (No full code)

To implement:

1.  Build candidate list from all loaded packs
    
2.  Filter by matching rules
    
3.  Compute ScoreTuple
    
4.  Sort candidates by tuple
    
5.  Pick top
    
6.  Record usage if rule is windowed
    

This is O(N log N) with small N per locale.

---

# 14) Developer-Facing Controls

Expose 3 “levers”:

1.  **packPrecedence** (recommended override mechanism)
    
2.  **domain ranking policy** (rarely changed, but possible)
    
3.  **safe mode** (disable protocol/religious domains unless explicit)
    

These levers are enough to prevent conflict without making the system complex.

---

# 15) Suggested Debug Trace Output

A trace object should show the computed tuple so developers can see why something won:

```
JSON

{  
  "winner": "el.orthodox.christos\_anesti",  
  "tuple": {  
    "domainRank": 7000,  
    "eventRank": 1100,  
    "packPrecedence": 10,  
    "rulePriority": 90,  
    "specificity": 72,  
    "localeMatch": 100,  
    "setting": 10,  
    "formality": 8,  
    "relationship": 0,  
    "freshness": 20  
  },  
  "runnerUp": "el.time.kalispera"  
}
```

This makes “chaos” debuggable.

---

If you want, next I can provide **formal normative wording** for this precedence algorithm in RFC style (MUST/SHOULD/MAY), so it can go straight into your Salve RFC.

You: 

What I now am to propose might be a bit controversial, so I want you to assess my proposal critically, but also with an open mind. So, some events, like holidays, are tied to specific historical contexts and connotations. So, for example, the 1st of May in Belgium is called Dag van de Arbeid, but in many countries the 1st of May is an official holiday, specifically for people within the socialist tradition. Therefore, it might be appropriate, but maybe a bit daring, because it could offend the sensibilities of some users or people from the audience. But still, it might be interesting to consider that at such occasions we would do a bit of a kind of historical wink or tongue-in-cheek. I mean, for example, on the 1st of May, we could greet people of all stripes and feathers. with comrades, precisely because the first of May is the international feast day for the socialist movement. So, in that case, we could greet people with comrades. Similarly, in France, on the Bastille Day, so the national feast day of France, the 14th of July, we could greet people always and univocally with Bon Fête Nationale, Concitoyen. So, we could expand this to, I assume, lots of that kind of cases. What do you think? Would this be appropriate? Should we put this feature behind a configurable flag? And if we were to implement this, how would you propose we would go about in realizing this?

ChatGPT: 

Your proposal is interesting and not unreasonable, but it **touches a sensitive design boundary**: when software starts to **attribute cultural, ideological, or political identity to users**, even playfully, it can easily be perceived as presumptive or partisan. For a library intended for **internationalization and localization infrastructure**, this risk is real. That said, the idea itself—**event-specific greeting styles with historical flavor**—can be implemented safely if the system is designed carefully.

Below is a critical assessment and a concrete design that would allow the feature while avoiding most pitfalls.

---

# 1\. Conceptual Assessment

Your idea actually mixes **three different concepts**:

1.  **Civil holiday greetings**  
    Example:
    
    -   “Happy Independence Day!”
        
    -   “Bonne fête nationale !”
        
2.  **Historically flavored rhetorical style**  
    Example:
    
    -   “Concitoyens !” on Bastille Day
        
    -   “Comrades” on May Day
        
3.  **Ideological or subcultural addressing conventions**  
    Example:
    
    -   “Comrade” in socialist contexts
        
    -   “Bundesbruder” in student fraternities
        

Only the **first category is universally safe**.

The **second category (historical flavor)** is acceptable if framed as **rhetorical or ceremonial**, not ideological.

The **third category must remain opt-in** because it implies identity.

Your proposal sits **between 2 and 3**.

---

# 2\. Recommendation

The feature is acceptable if:

1.  It is **disabled by default** for production use.
    
2.  It is framed as **ceremonial or historical register**, not ideological inference.
    
3.  It is **explicitly configurable by developers**.
    

In other words:

**The library must never infer ideology.**

But it can offer **festive rhetorical styles tied to historical events**.

---

# 3\. Proposed Feature: Ceremonial Register

Introduce a new concept:

```
register
```

Possible values:

```
neutral  
ceremonial  
playful  
historical
```

Example:

| Register | Example |
| --- | --- |
| neutral | “Happy Labor Day” |
| ceremonial | “Bonne fête nationale” |
| historical | “Citoyens, vive la République !” |
| playful | “Comrades, happy May Day!” |

Developers choose the register.

Default:

```
neutral
```

---

# 4\. Configuration Example

Runtime configuration:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  rhetoricalRegister: "neutral"  
})
```

Or:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  rhetoricalRegister: "ceremonial"  
})
```

Possible values:

```
neutral  
ceremonial  
historical  
playful
```

---

# 5\. How It Fits into the Precedence Model

Add a new scoring dimension:

```
RegisterMatchScore
```

Rules may specify:

```
register: "ceremonial"
```

Example rule:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "fr.bastille.citoyens",  
  "priority": 40,  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "bank.fr.bastille\_day"  
  },  
  "register": "historical",  
  "template": "Bonne fête nationale, concitoyen !"  
}
```

If runtime register is `neutral`, this rule is ignored.

If runtime register is `historical`, it becomes eligible.

---

# 6\. May Day Example

Safe implementation:

Neutral:

```
Happy Labour Day
```

Ceremonial:

```
Bonne fête du travail
```

Historical:

```
Comrades, happy International Workers' Day!
```

This ensures:

-   no ideological inference
    
-   developer choice
    

---

# 7\. Bastille Day Example

Neutral:

```
Bonne fête nationale
```

Ceremonial:

```
Bonne fête nationale à tous
```

Historical:

```
Citoyens, vive la République !
```


---

# 9\. Interaction With Subculture Packs

If a **socialist protocol pack** is installed:

```
@salve/protocol-socialist
```

Then the greeting:

```
Comrade
```

can appear **only if**:

```
subcultures: \["socialist"\]
```

This prevents ideological guessing.

---

# 10\. Ethical Design Principle

The rule should be:

> Salve MAY express historical or ceremonial language,  
> but it MUST NOT infer political, religious, or ideological identity.

---


# 12\. Potential Benefits

This feature would:

-   showcase cultural richness
    
-   demonstrate event awareness
    
-   make the demo engaging
    
-   highlight Salve's expressive range
    

Without compromising neutrality.

---

# 13\. Possible Risks

These must be acknowledged:

1.  Some organizations avoid political tone entirely.
    
2.  Automatic greetings may appear sarcastic in corporate settings.
    
3.  Humor varies culturally.
    

Hence the **register toggle is essential**.

---

# 14\. My Final Assessment

Your idea is **good but needs framing**.

It should be implemented as:

```
Ceremonial / Historical Register
```

not as:

```
Ideological greeting inference
```

This preserves the playful idea while keeping the system professional.

---

✅ **Verdict:**  
Implement it as an **optional rhetorical register system**.

If you want, I can also show you a **much more powerful extension** of this idea that would make Salve extremely expressive:

A **Greeting Style Engine** where greetings can adopt stylistic modes such as:

```
formal  
bureaucratic  
poetic  
liturgical  
ceremonial  
playful  
archaic
```

This would integrate beautifully with the precedence model and could make Salve far more distinctive than ordinary localization libraries.

You: 

Wow, that is truly a creative insight of you. So please go ahead with your proposal.

ChatGPT: 

Excellent — this extension actually fits **very naturally into SALVE’s architecture** and can become one of its most distinctive features. What we are essentially introducing is a **Greeting Style Engine** layered on top of the existing **context → event → rule selection pipeline**.

The key idea is:

> SALVE determines **what** greeting is appropriate (event, time, locale), and the **style engine** determines **how** it is expressed.

This keeps the architecture clean and prevents combinatorial explosion in greeting packs.

Below is a complete design that integrates smoothly with the precedence model and plugin system.

---

# 1\. Concept: Greeting Style Layer

SALVE already resolves:

```
context → event → greeting rule
```

Now we add:

```
greeting rule → stylistic rendering
```

So the pipeline becomes:

```
context  
  ↓  
event providers  
  ↓  
rule selection engine  
  ↓  
style engine  
  ↓  
final greeting text
```

The **rule decides semantic content**.  
The **style decides rhetorical expression**.

Example:

Semantic greeting rule:

```
GOOD\_EVENING
```

Style variations:

| Style | Output |
| --- | --- |
| neutral | Good evening |
| formal | Good evening, Sir |
| ceremonial | A pleasant evening to you |
| poetic | May this evening find you well |
| archaic | Good even to thee |

---

# 2\. Core Style Types

Define a controlled vocabulary:

```
neutral  
formal  
ceremonial  
playful  
poetic  
archaic  
liturgical  
bureaucratic  
minimal
```

Developers may extend this list via plugins.

Default:

```
neutral
```

---

# 3\. Runtime Configuration

Example runtime config:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  style: "neutral"  
})
```

Or:

```
TypeScript

const salve \= new Salve({  
  style: "ceremonial"  
})
```

Example:

```
May 1
```

| Style | Greeting |
| --- | --- |
| neutral | Happy Labour Day |
| ceremonial | Happy International Workers’ Day |
| historical | Comrades, happy May Day |

---

# 4\. Style Packs

Styles should be implemented as **plugins**.

New plugin type:

```
pack.style
```

Example package:

```
@salve/style-poetic
```

Manifest:

```
JSON

{  
  "kind": "pack.style",  
  "id": "style.poetic",  
  "version": "1.0.0"  
}
```

---

# 5\. Style Transformation Rules

A style pack defines **transformations**.

Example schema:

```
JSON

{  
  "style": "poetic",  
  "rules": \[  
    {  
      "base": "good\_evening",  
      "locale": "en",  
      "template": "May this evening greet you kindly"  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

If no transformation exists, SALVE falls back to base greeting.

---

# 6\. Style Weight in Precedence Model

Extend scoring tuple.

Old tuple:

```
DomainRank  
EventRank  
PackPrecedence  
RulePriority  
SpecificityScore  
LocaleMatchScore  
SettingMatchScore  
FormalityMatchScore  
RelationshipMatchScore  
WindowFreshnessScore  
StableTieBreak
```

Add:

```
StyleMatchScore
```

Computation:

```
+15 if rule supports requested style  
+5 if rule supports style family (e.g. ceremonial → formal)  
0 otherwise
```

Thus style preference influences selection but **never overrides event/domain hierarchy**.

---

# 7\. Style Families

To prevent fragmentation, styles can inherit from families.

Example hierarchy:

```
neutral  
 ├─ formal  
 │   ├─ ceremonial  
 │   └─ bureaucratic  
 ├─ playful  
 ├─ poetic  
 ├─ archaic  
 └─ liturgical
```

Fallback logic:

```
ceremonial → formal → neutral
```

---

# 8\. Style Interaction with Locale Packs

Locale packs may embed style variants.

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "fr.bastille.ceremonial",  
  "style": "ceremonial",  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "bank.fr.bastille\_day"  
  },  
  "template": "Bonne fête nationale"  
}
```

Historical variant:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "fr.bastille.historical",  
  "style": "historical",  
  "template": "Citoyens, vive la République !"  
}
```

---

# 9\. Composition Interaction

Styles can also affect **address structure**.

Example:

Neutral:

```
Hello, Dr. Müller
```

Ceremonial:

```
Good evening, Herr Doktor Müller
```

Poetic:

```
A pleasant evening to you, Dr. Müller
```

Style packs may override composition templates.

---

Your demo page could include:

```
Greeting Style:  
• Neutral  
• Formal  
• Ceremonial  
• Poetic  
• Archaic  
• Playful
```

Switching styles shows SALVE’s expressive power.

```

```

```

```

Liturgical Greek:

```
Ευλογημένη ημέρα
```

Many apps will want **strict neutrality**.

Provide runtime flag:

```
safeMode: true
```

Which restricts styles to:

```
neutral  
formal
```

# 12\. Example: Full Rendering

Context:

```
locale: fr-FR  
date: July 14  
style: ceremonial
```

Event provider:

```
bank.fr.bastille\_day
```

Rule selection:

```
fr.bastille.ceremonial
```

Output:

```
Bonne fête nationale !
```

Style: historical

Output:

```
Citoyens, vive la République !
```

---

# 13\. Why This Is Powerful

This system lets SALVE express **cultural nuance without exploding the rule set**.

Without style engine you would need:

```
time × event × formality × relationship × style
```

rules.

With style engine:

```
semantic rules  
+  
stylistic overlays
```

Much cleaner.

Possible packs:

```

@salve/style-liturgical  
@salve/style-playful  
@salve/style-archaic  
@salve/style-bureaucratic
```

These could even become **community contributions**.

# 15\. Big Architectural Picture

SALVE becomes:

```
Context engine  
+  
Event engine  
+  
Greeting rule engine  
+  
Style engine  
+  
Composition engine
```

Each modular and pluggable.

---

If you like, the next thing I could show you — and this is **extremely important for the long-term success of SALVE** — is a **Greeting Ontology**, a formal taxonomy of greeting types (salutation, valediction, seasonal greeting, ceremonial address, liturgical acclamation, etc.).

That ontology would make the dataset **systematic instead of ad-hoc**, which is exactly what would make SALVE credible enough to eventually integrate into CLDR.

You: 

Okay, please proceed. I'm very curious.

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **Greeting Ontology** for SALVE: a formal taxonomy of *what kind of greeting utterance* a string is, independent of language. This gives you a systematic backbone for:

-   data packs (so they aren’t ad-hoc lists)
    
-   rule selection (more predictable)
    
-   composition (greeting + address + punctuation)
    
-   call/response pairs
    
-   future CLDR-style compatibility (typed resources)
    

This ontology is **written-only** (per your scope).

---

## 1\. Core Ontology Design Goals

A greeting utterance in SALVE should be classified along these orthogonal axes:

1.  **Speech act type** (salutation vs valediction vs acclamation…)
    
2.  **Temporal anchoring** (time-of-day, date/event, season…)
    
3.  **Social anchoring** (relationship, formality, institutional rank…)
    
4.  **Register/style** (neutral, ceremonial, poetic…)
    
5.  **Conversation role** (initiator vs responder)
    
6.  **Channel setting** (UI/chat/email) — still written-only
    

SALVE can model these as typed “Greeting Acts”.

---

## 2\. Top-Level Greeting Act Types

These are the main classes SALVE should support.

### A. Salutation

A greeting used when *opening* interaction.

Examples:

-   “Hello”
    
-   “Good morning”
    
-   “Καλημέρα”
    
-   “Beste …,” (email opener)
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.salutation
```

### B. Valediction

A greeting used when *closing* interaction.

Examples:

-   “Goodbye”
    
-   “See you”
    
-   “Καληνύχτα” (when leaving at night)
    
-   “Met vriendelijke groet(en)” (email closing)
    
-   “Kind regards”
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.valediction
```

### C. Welcome

A greeting used when *receiving/hosting* someone, often not time-dependent.

Examples:

-   “Welcome!”
    
-   “Καλώς ήρθες / Καλώς ήρθατε”
    
-   “Welkom”
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.welcome
```

### D. Congratulatory Greeting

A greeting that expresses good wishes *because something is being celebrated*.

Examples:

-   “Happy birthday”
    
-   “Χρόνια πολλά”
    
-   “Fijne feestdagen”
    
-   “Eid Mubarak”
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.congratulation
```

### E. Commemorative / Observance Greeting

A greeting that marks an event in a solemn way (remembrance days etc.).

Examples:

-   “Lest we forget” (contextual)
    
-   “Happy/Meaningful Memorial Day” (tone varies)
    
-   (Many cultures prefer neutrality here.)
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.observance
```

### F. Acclamation

A ritualized phrase often used in liturgical or ceremonial contexts, frequently with a response pair.

Examples:

-   “Χριστός Ανέστη” → “Αληθώς Ανέστη”
    
-   “Peace be with you” → “And also with you” (variants)
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.acclamation
```

### G. Courtesy Address (Standalone)

An address-only utterance, not necessarily a “greeting word”.

Examples:

-   “Your Honor”
    
-   “Herr Doktor”
    
-   “Bundesbruder Müller”
    
-   “Genosse Ivanov”
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.address\_only
```

### H. Check-in / Wellbeing Inquiry

A greeting-like opener whose core function is asking about wellbeing.

Examples:

-   “How are you?”
    
-   “Alles gut?”
    
-   “Τι κάνεις;”
    
-   “Hoe gaat het?”
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.checkin
```

### I. Acknowledgment / Return Greeting

A response act acknowledging a greeting (may differ from initiating greeting).

Examples:

-   “Hello!” in reply
    
-   “Καλημέρα” in reply
    
-   “And to you too”
    
-   Response half of acclamations
    

Type id:

```
Plain text

act.acknowledge
```

---

## 3\. Temporal Anchoring Types

These are not “acts” but **anchors** that explain *why* a particular greeting is chosen.

### 3.1 Time-of-day anchors

```
Plain text

anchor.dayperiod.morning  
anchor.dayperiod.midday  
anchor.dayperiod.afternoon  
anchor.dayperiod.evening  
anchor.dayperiod.night
```

### 3.2 Date-position anchors

```
Plain text

anchor.date.new\_year\_window  
anchor.date.month\_start  
anchor.date.weekday\_specific
```

### 3.3 Event anchors (provider-driven)

```
Plain text

anchor.event.bank\_holiday  
anchor.event.religious\_feast  
anchor.event.personal\_birthday  
anchor.event.personal\_nameday  
anchor.event.civil\_national\_day
```

### 3.4 Season anchors

```
Plain text

anchor.season.spring  
anchor.season.summer  
anchor.season.autumn  
anchor.season.winter  
anchor.season.holiday\_season   (e.g. “Happy holidays” period)
```

---

## 4\. Social Anchoring Types

Again, orthogonal axes SALVE uses for selection + composition.

### 4.1 Relationship

```
Plain text

social.relationship.stranger  
social.relationship.acquaintance  
social.relationship.friend  
social.relationship.family  
social.relationship.superior  
social.relationship.subordinate
```

### 4.2 Formality

```
Plain text

social.formality.formal  
social.formality.informal  
social.formality.neutral
```

### 4.3 Institutional status

```
Plain text

social.status.judicial  
social.status.military  
social.status.academic  
social.status.clergy  
social.status.diplomatic  
social.status.civil\_service
```

### 4.4 Subculture protocol

```
Plain text

social.protocol.student\_corporation  
social.protocol.socialist  
social.protocol.guild  
social.protocol.scouting  
... (registry)
```

---

## 5\. Register / Style Axis

This is your style engine.

```
Plain text

style.neutral  
style.formal  
style.ceremonial  
style.liturgical  
style.poetic  
style.playful  
style.archaic  
style.bureaucratic  
style.minimal
```

Important rule:

-   style is *never inferred*; it is configured or defaults to neutral.
    

---

## 6\. Conversation Role Axis

```
Plain text

role.initiator  
role.responder
```

This supports:

-   call/response pairs
    
-   greeting replies
    
-   “Likewise” style responses
    

---

## 7\. Output Form Axis

This controls composition, punctuation, and address placement:

```
Plain text

form.greeting\_only  
form.address\_only  
form.salutation  (greeting + address)  
form.email\_opening  
form.email\_closing
```

---

## 8\. How This Maps to SALVE Data Rules

Each greeting rule should carry typed metadata:

Example (conceptually):

-   `act`: `act.congratulation`
    
-   `anchor`: `anchor.event.personal_nameday`
    
-   `style`: `style.ceremonial`
    
-   `role`: `role.initiator`
    
-   `form`: `form.salutation`
    

This makes datasets consistent and enables validation like:

-   A valediction should not have `phase=opening`
    
-   An acclamation should have an `expectedResponse`
    
-   An email closing should end with comma-less conventions per locale pack
    

---

## 9\. Why This Prevents Ad-hoc Expansion

Without ontology, people will add random keys:

-   “greetingType: ‘cool’”
    
-   “context: ‘holidayish’”
    
-   “kind: ‘special’”
    

With ontology, every rule must state:

-   **what act** it is
    
-   **what anchors** it depends on
    
-   **what social constraints** it assumes
    
-   **what style register** it expresses
    

This makes packs comparable, mergeable, and CLDR-adjacent.

---

## 10\. Minimal Ontology for MVP

If you want a tight MVP subset (still systematic):

Acts:

-   `salutation`
    
-   `valediction`
    
-   `welcome`
    
-   `congratulation`
    
-   `acclamation`
    
-   `address_only`
    

Anchors:

-   `dayperiod.*`
    
-   `event.bank_holiday`
    
-   `event.religious_feast`
    
-   `event.personal_birthday`
    
-   `event.personal_nameday`
    

Social:

-   relationship + formality
    

Style:

-   neutral/formal/ceremonial (others later)
    

That’s enough to be powerful without bloat.

---

## 11\. Validation Rules Enabled by the Ontology

Examples of machine-checkable constraints:

-   If `act=acclamation` then `expectedResponseTemplate MUST be present`.
    
-   If `act=valediction` then `phase MUST be closing`.
    
-   If `anchor=personal_nameday` then `namedaysEnabled MUST be true` OR `affiliationsAny includes a tradition that uses namedays` (policy configurable).
    
-   If `form=email_opening` then punctuation pattern MUST match locale composition rules.
    
-   If `social.formality=formal` and address requires gendered honorific but `gender=unknown`, then address block MUST be suppressed (graceful degradation).
    

This is where SALVE becomes “standards-grade”.

---

## 12\. CLDR Compatibility Note

CLDR’s LDML already separates:

-   locale data categories
    
-   “display names”
    
-   calendars
    

SALVE can align by treating these ontology types as:

-   stable identifiers comparable to CLDR key namespaces
    

Example key namespace style:

```
Plain text

salve/acts/salutation  
salve/anchors/dayPeriod/morning  
salve/styles/ceremonial
```

---

If you want, next I can translate this ontology into a **canonical JSON schema** (enums + structural constraints) so that every pack rule is validated against the ontology automatically.

You: 

That's a very good idea, so please continue.

ChatGPT: 

Below is the **canonical JSON Schema layer for the SALVE Greeting Ontology**.  
The goal is to make **every greeting rule machine-verifiable**, so packs cannot drift into inconsistent or ad-hoc structures.

This schema layer sits **on top of the plugin schemas** we defined earlier and specifically validates **greeting rule entries** inside locale packs or protocol packs.

The design principles are:

-   strict ontology enums
    
-   composable rule structure
    
-   compatibility with the SALVE precedence engine
    
-   compatibility with CLDR-style data organization
    
-   future convertibility to LDML
    

---

# 1\. Greeting Rule Schema Overview

Each greeting rule object must contain:

| Field | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| `id` | stable identifier |
| `act` | greeting act type |
| `form` | output structure |
| `style` | rhetorical register |
| `when` | selection conditions |
| `template` | rendered greeting text |
| `priority` | precedence within pack |

Optional fields:

-   `expectedResponseTemplate`
    
-   `addressTemplate`
    
-   `metadata`
    

---

# 2\. Canonical Greeting Rule JSON Schema

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://json-schema.org/draft/2020-12/schema",  
  
  "title": "SALVE Greeting Rule",  
  
  "type": "object",  
  
  "required": \[  
    "id",  
    "act",  
    "form",  
    "style",  
    "template"  
  \],  
  
  "properties": {  
  
    "id": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "pattern": "^\[a-z0-9.\_-\]+$"  
    },  
  
    "act": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "enum": \[  
        "salutation",  
        "valediction",  
        "welcome",  
        "congratulation",  
        "observance",  
        "acclamation",  
        "address\_only",  
        "checkin",  
        "acknowledge"  
      \]  
    },  
  
    "form": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "enum": \[  
        "greeting\_only",  
        "address\_only",  
        "salutation",  
        "email\_opening",  
        "email\_closing"  
      \]  
    },  
  
    "style": {  
      "type": "string",  
      "enum": \[  
        "neutral",  
        "formal",  
        "ceremonial",  
        "liturgical",  
        "poetic",  
        "playful",  
        "archaic",  
        "bureaucratic",  
        "minimal"  
      \]  
    },  
  
    "priority": {  
      "type": "integer",  
      "minimum": 0,  
      "maximum": 100  
    },  
  
    "template": {  
      "type": "string"  
    },  
  
    "expectedResponseTemplate": {  
      "type": "string"  
    },  
  
    "when": {  
  
      "type": "object",  
  
      "properties": {  
  
        "eventRef": {  
          "type": "string"  
        },  
  
        "dayPeriod": {  
          "type": "string",  
          "enum": \[  
            "morning",  
            "midday",  
            "afternoon",  
            "evening",  
            "night"  
          \]  
        },  
  
        "phase": {  
          "type": "string",  
          "enum": \[  
            "opening",  
            "closing"  
          \]  
        },  
  
        "setting": {  
          "type": "array",  
          "items": {  
            "type": "string",  
            "enum": \[  
              "ui",  
              "chat",  
              "email"  
            \]  
          }  
        },  
  
        "relationship": {  
          "type": "array",  
          "items": {  
            "type": "string",  
            "enum": \[  
              "stranger",  
              "acquaintance",  
              "friend",  
              "family",  
              "superior",  
              "subordinate"  
            \]  
          }  
        },  
  
        "formality": {  
          "type": "string",  
          "enum": \[  
            "formal",  
            "informal",  
            "neutral"  
          \]  
        },  
  
        "affiliationsAny": {  
          "type": "array",  
          "items": { "type": "string" }  
        },  
  
        "subculturesAny": {  
          "type": "array",  
          "items": { "type": "string" }  
        }  
  
      }  
  
    },  
  
    "addressTemplate": {  
      "type": "string"  
    },  
  
    "metadata": {  
      "type": "object",  
      "additionalProperties": true  
    }  
  
  }  
  
}
```

---

# 3\. Example Valid Greeting Rule

Greek Orthodox Easter greeting:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "el.christos\_anesti",  
  
  "act": "acclamation",  
  
  "form": "greeting\_only",  
  
  "style": "liturgical",  
  
  "priority": 90,  
  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "religious.orthodox\_easter"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Χριστός Ανέστη",  
  
  "expectedResponseTemplate": "Αληθώς Ανέστη"  
}
```

# 4\. Example Civil Greeting

French Bastille Day greeting:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "fr.bastille\_day",  
  
  "act": "congratulation",  
  
  "form": "greeting\_only",  
  
  "style": "ceremonial",  
  
  "priority": 60,  
  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "bank.fr.bastille\_day"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Bonne fête nationale"  
}
```

# 5\. Example Time-of-Day Greeting

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "nl.goedemorgen",  
  
  "act": "salutation",  
  
  "form": "greeting\_only",  
  
  "style": "neutral",  
  
  "priority": 10,  
  
  "when": {  
    "dayPeriod": "morning"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Goedemorgen"  
}
```

---

# 6\. Example Address Rule

German academic title greeting:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "de.herr\_doktor",  
  
  "act": "salutation",  
  
  "form": "salutation",  
  
  "style": "formal",  
  
  "priority": 70,  
  
  "when": {  
    "formality": "formal"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Guten Tag",  
  
  "addressTemplate": "Herr Doktor {surname}"  
}
```

Rendered result:

```
Plain text

Guten Tag, Herr Doktor Müller
```

---

# 7\. Example Protocol Greeting

Student corporation:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "de.bundesbruder",  
  
  "act": "salutation",  
  
  "form": "address\_only",  
  
  "style": "formal",  
  
  "priority": 80,  
  
  "when": {  
    "subculturesAny": \["student\_corporation"\]  
  },  
  
  "template": "Bundesbruder {surname}"  
}
```

---

# 8\. Validation Rules Enabled by Schema

The schema allows automated checks.

Example rules:

| Condition | Validation |
| --- | --- |
| `act=acclamation` | `expectedResponseTemplate` SHOULD exist |
| `act=valediction` | `phase=closing` |
| `form=email_opening` | `phase=opening` |
| `form=address_only` | `template` MUST include address token |

A SALVE CLI could enforce this.

Example:

```
Bash

salve validate-pack ./locale-el
```

---

# 9\. Schema Integration in Monorepo

Recommended location:

```
packages/schema/  
   greeting-rule.schema.json  
   locale-pack.schema.json  
   protocol-pack.schema.json  
   plugin-manifest.schema.json
```

All packs must reference them.

---

# 10\. Example Locale Pack Using Schema

```
@salve/locale-el
```

```
locale-el/  
  salve.manifest.json  
  greetings.json
```

greetings.json:

```
JSON

{  
  "$schema": "https://salve.dev/schema/greeting-rule.schema.json",  
  
  "rules": \[  
    { ... },  
    { ... }  
  \]  
}
```

---

# 11\. Why This Matters

Without this ontology schema:

-   packs drift
    
-   rules conflict
    
-   naming becomes inconsistent
    
-   rule validation impossible
    

With it:

SALVE becomes **a standards-grade linguistic dataset**, not just code.

---

# 12\. Long-Term Impact

If SALVE ever integrates into CLDR:

the ontology maps neatly to:

| SALVE | CLDR |
| --- | --- |
| greeting acts | message categories |
| events | calendar events |
| styles | message variants |
| locale packs | LDML locale files |

# 13\. Next Logical Step

There is **one final piece** that would make SALVE extremely robust:

A **Global Event Namespace Registry**.

Right now events are referenced like:

```
bank.fr.bastille\_day  
religious.orthodox\_easter  
personal.nameday
```

But if we formalize the namespace we avoid collisions forever.

Example:

```
salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day  
salve.event.religious.orthodox.easter  
salve.event.personal.nameday  
salve.event.civil.eu.day\_of\_europe
```

This becomes the **semantic backbone connecting event providers and greeting rules**.

If you want, I can design that registry next — and it will make the SALVE architecture essentially **complete and future-proof**.

You: 

Wow, absolutely fantastic, so go ahead.

ChatGPT: 

Designing a **Global Event Namespace Registry** is indeed the last foundational layer needed to make SALVE coherent and extensible. It ensures that all event identifiers used by providers and greeting rules follow a **stable, collision-free taxonomy**, similar to how CLDR standardizes locale identifiers.

Below is a practical specification.

---

# 1\. Purpose of the Event Namespace

The Event Namespace provides:

-   **stable identifiers** for events used by greeting rules
    
-   interoperability between **event providers** and **locale packs**
    
-   avoidance of **naming collisions**
    
-   deterministic mapping between events and greetings
    
-   a path toward **standardization**
    

Greeting rules reference events through `eventRef`.

Example:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day
```

Providers emit events using the same identifiers.

---

# 2\. Canonical Event ID Structure

All SALVE events follow this pattern:

```
Plain text

salve.event.<domain>.<region?>.<category?>.<event\_name>
```

Segments are lowercase and dot-separated.

| Segment | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| salve.event | namespace root |
| domain | event category |
| region | ISO region code when applicable |
| category | optional subdomain |
| event\_name | stable identifier |

---

# 3\. Event Domains

These are the top-level categories.

```
Plain text

bank  
civil  
religious  
personal  
seasonal  
protocol  
custom
```

Explanation:

| Domain | Description |
| --- | --- |
| bank | statutory or public holidays |
| civil | civic or commemorative days |
| religious | religious feast days |
| personal | user-specific events |
| seasonal | culturally seasonal greetings |
| protocol | ritual or institutional greetings |
| custom | developer-defined |

---

# 4\. Bank Holiday Events

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.<country>.<holiday>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.us.independence\_day  
salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day  
salve.event.bank.be.labour\_day  
salve.event.bank.jp.golden\_week  
salve.event.bank.de.tag\_der\_deutschen\_einheit
```

Country codes follow **ISO-3166-1 alpha-2**.

---

# 5\. Civil Observances

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.civil.<region?>.<event>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.civil.eu.day\_of\_europe  
salve.event.civil.un.human\_rights\_day  
salve.event.civil.be.armistice\_day
```

Civil events may be:

-   international
    
-   regional
    
-   national
    

---

# 6\. Religious Events

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.religious.<tradition>.<feast>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.religious.christian.christmas  
salve.event.religious.orthodox.easter  
salve.event.religious.islam.eid\_al\_fitr  
salve.event.religious.islam.eid\_al\_adha  
salve.event.religious.jewish.rosh\_hashanah  
salve.event.religious.hindu.diwali
```

This allows precise cultural targeting.

---

# 7\. Personal Events

Personal events come from user data.

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.personal.<type>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.personal.birthday  
salve.event.personal.nameday  
salve.event.personal.anniversary
```

These are produced by **personal event providers**.

---

# 8\. Seasonal Events

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.seasonal.<season>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.seasonal.spring  
salve.event.seasonal.summer  
salve.event.seasonal.autumn  
salve.event.seasonal.winter  
salve.event.seasonal.holiday\_season
```

Used for greetings like:

```
Plain text

Happy holidays  
Season’s greetings
```

---

# 9\. Protocol Events

Protocol events enable ceremonial or institutional greetings.

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.protocol.<protocol>.<occasion>
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

salve.event.protocol.student\_corporation.foundation\_day  
salve.event.protocol.military.veterans\_day
```

These events usually originate from **protocol packs**.

---

# 10\. Custom Events

Developers may define custom events.

Format:

```
Plain text

salve.event.custom.<vendor>.<event>
```

Example:

```
Plain text

salve.event.custom.company.product\_launch
```

Custom events must never collide with standard namespaces.

---

# 11\. Event Object Schema

Providers emit events in this form:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day",  
  "kind": "bank",  
  "start": "2027-07-14",  
  "end": "2027-07-14",  
  "label": "Bastille Day",  
  "source": "holiday.date-holidays"  
}
```

Optional fields:

| Field | Purpose |
| --- | --- |
| precedence | event importance |
| confidence | probabilistic events |
| metadata | provider-specific info |

---

# 12\. Event Matching in Greeting Rules

Greeting rule:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "fr.bastille\_day",  
  
  "act": "congratulation",  
  
  "form": "greeting\_only",  
  
  "style": "ceremonial",  
  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Bonne fête nationale"  
}
```

SALVE runtime matches the event to rule.

---

# 13\. Namespace Governance

To avoid chaos, SALVE should maintain a **registry document**:

```
Plain text

docs/event-registry.md
```

Each entry contains:

| Field | Description |
| --- | --- |
| event id | canonical identifier |
| domain | bank/civil/religious/etc |
| description | human explanation |
| scope | global/regional/local |

---

Example entry:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day  
Domain: bank  
Scope: France  
Description: National Day of France (14 July)
```

---

# 14\. Event Alias Mechanism

Different providers may use different names.

Example:

```
Plain text

bastille\_day  
national\_day\_france
```

Registry allows aliases:

```
Plain text

aliases:  
\- national\_day\_france
```

Runtime resolves alias → canonical id.

---

# 15\. Provider Mapping Layer

Holiday provider adapters map their source data.

Example:

```
Plain text

Date-holidays → salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day
```

Mapping table stored in provider package.

---

# 16\. Event Precedence

Some events override others.

Example ranking:

| Rank | Domain |
| --- | --- |
| 9000 | protocol |
| 8000 | religious |
| 7000 | personal |
| 6500 | bank |
| 6000 | civil |
| 5000 | seasonal |
| 1000 | baseline |

Used in greeting selection tuple.

---

# 17\. Event Windows

Events may define active ranges.

Example:

```
Plain text

salve.event.seasonal.holiday\_season
```

Range:

```
Plain text

Dec 20 → Jan 5
```

Providers must include start/end dates.

---

# 18\. Example Event Flow

User context:

```
Plain text

locale: fr-FR  
date: 14 July
```

Holiday provider emits:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day
```

Greeting rule matches:

```
Plain text

Bonne fête nationale !
```

Style engine modifies output if necessary.

---

# 19\. Event Registry Example (Excerpt)

```
Plain text

salve.event.personal.birthday  
salve.event.personal.nameday  
salve.event.bank.us.independence\_day  
salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day  
salve.event.bank.be.labour\_day  
salve.event.religious.christian.christmas  
salve.event.religious.orthodox.easter  
salve.event.religious.islam.eid\_al\_fitr  
salve.event.religious.islam.eid\_al\_adha  
salve.event.seasonal.holiday\_season
```

---

# 20\. Final Architecture

With the namespace registry, SALVE’s conceptual stack becomes:

```
Plain text

Context Engine  
   ↓  
Event Providers  
   ↓  
Event Namespace Registry  
   ↓  
Greeting Rule Engine  
   ↓  
Style Engine  
   ↓  
Composition Engine  
   ↓  
Output Greeting
```

---

# 21\. Why This Completes the System

With the ontology, schemas, and event namespace:

SALVE now has:

-   a **formal semantic model**
    
-   a **deterministic rule engine**
    
-   a **pluggable provider ecosystem**
    
-   a **standards-compatible data format**
    

In other words:

SALVE is no longer just a library.

It becomes a **global greeting knowledge system**.

-   ontology (acts, anchors, styles)
    
-   event namespace
    
-   locale packs
    
-   protocol packs
    
-   precedence logic
    
-   style engine
    
-   event providers (bank + nameday)
    

Everything is **data-only**, as it would appear inside packs in the SALVE monorepo.

# 5\. Nameday Provider Dataset (Greek)

Two-stage structure.

### Date → saint ids

```
JSON

{  
  "2027-01-07": \["saint.ioannis"\],  
  "2027-01-20": \["saint.efthymios"\]  
}
```

### Saint → names

```
JSON

{  
  "saint.ioannis": {  
    "names": \[  
      "Ioannis",  
      "Yannis",  
      "Giannis",  
      "John",  
      "Jan",  
      "Jean"  
    \]  
  }  
}
```

User name:

```
Plain text

Jan
```

Fuzzy mapping:

```
Plain text

Jan → saint.ioannis
```

Event emitted:

```
Plain text

salve.event.personal.nameday
```

Greeting rule triggered:

```
Plain text

Χρόνια πολλά
```

# 8\. Full Greeting Resolution Example

Context:

```
Plain text

locale: nl-BE  
date: 1 May  
time: 09:30

```

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.be.labour\_day
```

Matching rules:

| Rule | Score |
| --- | --- |
| nl.labour\_day.ceremonial | highest |
| nl.goedemorgen | lower |

Winner:

```
Plain text

Een mooie Dag van de Arbeid gewenst
```

You: 

This is great. Please add the draft is minimal version 1 implementation roadmap.

ChatGPT: 

Below is a **pragmatic SALVE v1 roadmap** designed to get a **usable library into developers’ hands quickly** while preserving the architecture we designed. The key principle is:

> **Implement the core runtime and a minimal data ecosystem first; everything else is modular and can arrive later.**

This prevents SALVE from becoming an over-ambitious research project.

---

# SALVE v1 Minimal Implementation Roadmap

## Guiding Principles

1.  **Runtime first, data second**
    
2.  **Minimal plugin system**
    
3.  **Only a few events**
    
4.  **Two locales**
    
5.  **One provider per event type**
    
6.  **Deterministic rule engine**
    

Goal:

```
Plain text

A working library that can produce correct greetings  
based on locale, time-of-day, and a few events.
```

---

# Phase 1 — Core Runtime

### Objective

Build the **SALVE engine** capable of resolving greetings from context.

### Packages

```
Plain text

@salve/core  
@salve/runtime
```

### Implement

Core context model:

```
Plain text

locale  
date  
time  
relationship  
formality  
style  
phase
```

Basic greeting resolution pipeline:

```
Plain text

context  
 → rule filtering  
 → precedence scoring  
 → best rule  
 → rendering
```

Basic precedence tuple:

```
Plain text

DomainRank  
EventRank  
PackPrecedence  
RulePriority  
LocaleMatchScore
```

Skip advanced scoring fields initially.

### Deliverable

Example API:

```
TypeScript

const greeting \= salve.greet({  
  locale: "nl-BE",  
  date: new Date(),  
  style: "neutral"  
})
```

---

# Phase 2 — Minimal Plugin System

### Objective

Allow loading packs and providers.

Implement plugin registry:

```
Plain text

pack.locale  
pack.style  
provider.holiday  
provider.nameday
```

Simple loader:

```
TypeScript

salve.use(localeNl)  
salve.use(localeEl)  
salve.use(dateHolidaysProvider)
```

No dynamic runtime loading yet.

---

# Phase 3 — Event Namespace

Implement minimal event registry.

Initial set:

```
Plain text

salve.event.bank.be.labour\_day  
salve.event.bank.fr.bastille\_day  
salve.event.religious.christian.christmas  
salve.event.personal.nameday
```

Hardcode registry in runtime initially.

Later move to dataset.

---

# Phase 4 — Holiday Provider

Implement **one holiday provider**.

Recommended:

```
Plain text

date-holidays
```

Adapter package:

```
Plain text

@salve/provider-date-holidays
```

Capabilities:

```
Plain text

bank holidays  
civil holidays
```

This instantly gives ~100 countries.

---

# Phase 5 — Nameday Provider

For v1:

Use **simple JSON dataset** instead of full fuzzy resolver.

Package:

```
Plain text

@salve/provider-namedays-el
```

Features:

```
Plain text

date → saint  
saint → names
```

Fuzzy name mapping can be simple lowercase match.

---

# Phase 6 — Locale Packs

Ship **two official packs**.

```
Plain text

@salve/locale-nl  
@salve/locale-el
```

Each pack should include:

-   baseline greetings
    
-   time-of-day greetings
    
-   a few holiday greetings
    
-   optional nameday greeting
    

Example rule categories:

```
Plain text

morning greeting  
evening greeting  
hello  
labour day  
christmas  
nameday
```

---

# Phase 7 — Style Engine (Minimal)

Implement **only three styles** initially:

```
Plain text

neutral  
formal  
ceremonial
```

No style packs yet.

Locale packs include style variants directly.

---

# Phase 8 — Composition Engine

Implement simple rendering patterns:

```
Plain text

greeting\_only  
salutation  
email\_opening  
email\_closing
```

Minimal composition:

```
Plain text

"{greeting}"  
"{greeting}, {address}"
```

---

# Phase 9 — Developer Debug Mode

Expose explanation API:

```
TypeScript

salve.debug(context)
```

Returns:

```
JSON

{  
  "selectedRule": "...",  
  "event": "...",  
  "candidates": \[...\]  
}
```

This dramatically improves developer trust.

---

# Phase 10 — Demo Website

A polished demo is essential.

Features:

-   hero greeting
    
-   timeline cycling events
    
-   inline editable context variables
    
-   style switcher
    
-   developer trace
    

This is your **marketing asset**.

---

# What v1 Deliberately Postpones

These features are powerful but **not required for v1**.

## Advanced scoring fields

Postpone:

```
Plain text

specificity score  
window freshness  
relationship weighting
```

Basic precedence is enough initially.

---

## Protocol packs

Subculture greetings can wait.

Example postponed packs:

```
Plain text

@salve/protocol-socialist  
@salve/protocol-student-corporations
```

---

## External nameday APIs

Initial implementation can use static dataset.

Remote queries can arrive later.

---

## Full ontology enforcement

JSON schema validation can come in v1.1.

---

## CLDR export

Future feature once dataset stabilizes.

# Final Vision

After v1 stabilizes, SALVE could evolve into:

```
Plain text

A universal greeting localization framework
```

similar to how **CLDR standardized number/date formatting**.

But for greetings.

---

If you want, I can also show you something that could **dramatically increase the visibility of the SALVE project**:

A **one-page “SALVE manifesto”** explaining the problem and vision in a way that would resonate on Hacker News and GitHub.

You: 

I was just thinking on another feature which might be useful, and it has to do with how people identify. So we already explored how people can have various affiliations or they identify with. So, for example, they may follow the Greek Orthodox liturgical calendar and or they might identify with Muslim feast days and observances, and they may be a member of a fraternity and so on and so forth. Another kind of affiliation I would like you to consider is for people who identify or feel affiliated with another locality, another tradition. So it's not so much about the expats or people from the diaspora, like let's say Turkish Muslims living in Germany, but it's rather about people who are situated, for example, in Belgium and they speak Dutch and follow the official bank holidays of Belgium and also they might associate with Catholic feasts. But then say they have a specific fondness or affiliation with another tradition, like, say, some affinity for Japanese anime culture or people who are learning a language, like Spanish, and then they would like to be reminded of specific Japanese and or Spanish feast days.

ChatGPT: 

Your idea is actually **very natural within the SALVE architecture** and it fits perfectly with the conceptual direction we have already taken. The key is to model this **not as identity, but as affinity**.

This distinction is extremely important:

| Concept | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| **identity / affiliation** | things the user *belongs to* (religion, nationality, locale) |
| **affinity / interest** | things the user *likes or studies* |

The feature you propose clearly belongs to the second category.

This means SALVE can remain **culturally respectful and deterministic**, while also supporting **personal cultural interests**.

---

# 1\. The New Domain: Cultural Affinities

We introduce a new context field.

```
Plain text

affinities
```

Examples:

```
Plain text

affinities:  
\- japanese\_culture  
\- spanish\_language\_learning  
\- anime  
\- latin\_america
```

These affinities **do not override locale logic**.  
They simply **enable additional events to surface**.

---

# 2\. Why This Is Useful

This feature enables SALVE to act not only as a greeting engine but also as a **cultural reminder engine**.

Examples:

User in Belgium with:

```
Plain text

locale: nl-BE  
affinities: \[japan\]
```

On **Tanabata (7 July)** SALVE might produce:

```
Plain text

Vandaag vieren ze in Japan Tanabata
```

Or optionally:

```
Plain text

Happy Tanabata!
```

Similarly:

```
Plain text

affinities: \[spanish\]
```

On **Día de los Reyes (Jan 6)**:

```
Plain text

Hoy es Día de los Reyes en España
```

---

# 3\. How This Fits the SALVE Architecture

We simply add another **event domain**.

Existing domains:

```
Plain text

bank  
religious  
personal  
subculture
```

Add:

```
Plain text

affinity
```

These events are **informational by default**, not greetings.

---

# 4\. Affinity Event Pack

Example:

```
JSON

{  
  "domain": "affinity",  
  
  "affinity": "japanese\_culture",  
  
  "events": \[  
    {  
      "id": "salve.event.affinity.jp.tanabata",  
      "date": "07-07"  
    },  
  
    {  
      "id": "salve.event.affinity.jp.golden\_week",  
      "dateRange": \["05-01", "05-05"\]  
    }  
  \]  
}
```

These events **activate only if affinity is enabled**.

---

# 5\. Greeting Rule Example

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "nl.affinity.jp.tanabata",  
  
  "act": "information",  
  "priority": 15,  
  
  "when": {  
    "eventRef": "salve.event.affinity.jp.tanabata"  
  },  
  
  "template": "Vandaag vieren ze Tanabata in Japan"  
}
```

Note:  
This **does not override the main greeting**.

Instead SALVE may produce:

```
Plain text

Goedemorgen  
  
Vandaag vieren ze Tanabata in Japan
```

---

# 6\. Priority Model

Affinity events should have **low precedence**.

Priority tiers become:

| Tier | Domain |
| --- | --- |
| 1 | personal |
| 2 | religious |
| 3 | bank |
| 4 | subculture |
| 5 | affinity |

So affinity events never disrupt core greetings.

---

# 7\. Language Learning Use Case

This is particularly elegant.

Example context:

```
Plain text

locale: nl-BE  
affinities: \[spanish\]
```

On **Día de los Muertos**:

SALVE could output:

```
Plain text

Hoy es Día de los Muertos
```

or:

```
Plain text

Vandaag is het Día de los Muertos in Mexico
```

This turns SALVE into a **cultural awareness tool**.

---

# 8\. Anime / Pop Culture Affinities

Your anime example is also interesting.

Possible events:

```
Plain text

anime\_day  
studio\_ghibli\_day
```

Example pack:

```
Plain text

@salve/affinity-anime
```

Event example:

```
JSON

{  
  "id": "salve.event.affinity.anime.anime\_day",  
  "date": "04-15"  
}
```

